HEADLINE: For the Sake Of Argument

 BODY:
Weisberg's article was hardly an unbiased or accurate answer to the question on your cover. For example, Weisberg wrote, "After 24 years in the Senate, he leaves behind a record long on concepts and short on concrete results." This is false, as are other opinions expressed in the article.
More than $80 billion was distributed to New York State thanks to legislation introduced by Moynihan. He was the principal architect of the Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1993. That cut in half the federal deficit -- a reduction of more than $500 billion in the ensuing years. 
 He sponsored the Family Support Act. This strengthened child-support requirements and provided additional financing for job training and education for unemployed parents. As part of the Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1993, he sponsored amendments to the Family Support Act to improve access to health care for children and further strengthen child-support enforcement.
In 1993, he ensured needed changes in the Earned Income Tax Credit. Result? In New York State, 890,000 families, almost 20 percent of all families, received tax credits totaling $1.45 billion.
In 1994, the Senate Finance Committee unanimously approved his legislation (S.1231) to expand Social Security coverage for domestic workers. That also simplified reporting requirements for employers.
There are many other examples: better transportation for New York, help for subway riders, preserving New York's beaches, developing Western New York, fighting gun violence, fighting terrorism. He supported the arts, the best nominations for the judiciary and more -- much more.
In his last election, he received the largest majority of votes in the history of New York State. Clearly, the citizens of New York have a more accurate picture of the senator than what was written with such animus in your article.
 
Martin E. Segal
New York 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: MIDSTREAM;
Derring-Do of the Stay-at-Home Dad

BYLINE: By JAMES SCHEMBARI; James Schembari is an editor at The Times. His column on the prime earning, spending and striving years appears the fourth Sunday of each month. E-mail may be sent to jimschem@nytimes.com.

 BODY:
I HAVE had this fantasy for a number of years, and while it is one of my few actually attainable dreams, I have never had the courage to make it happen. I suppose that's because I find the fantasy -- to quit work and stay home to raise my four children -- more attractive than what it would take to do it.
One of the highlights of my day is to walk my three sons to school in the morning. There is an ease to those moments, and my boys talk to me more intimately, with more openness, than at any other time of the day. After I deliver them and head for my train to the city, still glowing from their company, the fantasy is strongest. 
 I suspect that a lot of other men have similar thoughts, but I also suspect that we all get hung up on the same concern: How would we ever be able to do it?
Not only would the loss of income make it difficult, but how do we give up identities that rely so much on what we do at work? The male ego is notably hefty. On that note, are any of us guys prepared to have our wives be the sole breadwinners? As one stay-at-home dad said: "I feel funny buying my wife $40 worth of roses with her money."
Still, men are doing it. The Census Bureau estimates that 459,000 fathers were staying home to take care of their children, ages 14 and under, in 1993, the latest year for which figures were available, and that almost three-fourths of them were the primary sources of care.
"Twenty years ago it was almost freakish," said Peter Baylies, 44, of North Andover, Mass., who publishes At-Home Dad, a newsletter. "Now it's considered unusual. So at least we're making some progress."
I see two stay-at-home dads when I walk my sons to school. They never race for the train, and I know that they're not looking at me fantasizing that they were. I have told both men how much I admire them.
"Some guys are envious," said one of the dads, Thomas J. Lawton Jr., 39, who worked for a company that guaranteed student loans before deciding four years ago to stay home with his two boys, ages 6 and 9. "They say, 'If I could stay home, I would do it in a minute.' Other guys think, 'He can't hold down a job, he couldn't achieve.' I was in a management position before I left work and had the love and adoration of my staff. I guess I still do."
The other dad, Kirk Schubert, 45, who has four children, ranging in age from 2 to 9, and another on the way, doesn't hear many comments about his ability to hold down a job, but he does get tired of being called Mr. Mom. He left work as a derivatives trader in Manhattan in 1997, after his wife took a job with UBS Warburg in Stamford, Conn. He could afford to stay home, but he also wanted to.
"There was a very clear routine at work, but over time it was hard to tell one day from the other," he said. "I felt like I was not marking the days of my life with really important things. With the kids, I don't have that feeling.
"I remember a hit I got during a high school baseball game," he added. "When I got to second base, I couldn't get the smile off my face. I felt that way when I left work."
After hearing lines like that, who wouldn't be tempted? But Mr. Baylies, a stay-at-home dad with two school-aged children, says making the leap isn't easy -- and not just because of the economics. It can take a long time to feel comfortable with the new role, he said, and the isolation can be tough. Gone is the camaraderie of work.
"When I walked around the block with the stroller, I would only see moms," he said. "Pretty soon you're talking about blankies and sippy cups."
His advice for any man wanting to try this at home is to first consider what kind of dad he is. "Do you have the patience to be with your child all day long?" he asked. "Are you outgoing and self-motivated? Are you willing to live with a lower income?"
Although many dads are saying yes, a lot are obviously saying no. The Census Bureau said that while almost a half-million dads were home taking care of their children, there were about another half-million at home who were not.
Kristine Smith, a family demographer at the bureau, said that situation could be attributed to several factors. Some dads were out of the house trying to find jobs, while some families decided to keep their children in day care. But she said some men just weren't interested in taking care of their children.
In other words, many men are handed the opportunity to be stay-at-home dads, but they don't share my fantasy. They would rather go back to school, back to work -- anything but push a stroller.
Many men say stay-at-home fathering is a luxury that only the wealthy can afford, which seems to make sense until you realize how long women managed the same feat. I know an excuse when I hear one.

HEADLINE: L.I. @ WORK;
Skilled Foreign Workers: More to Come

BYLINE: By WARREN STRUGATCH

 BODY:
ROBERT THOMPSON wasn't "Bob" until he came to Long Island. When he lived and worked in Europe, and before that in Canada, he was called Mr. Thompson.
"I walked into an office not long after I got here, and the receptionist asked me my name," he said. "I blurted out, 'Mr. Thompson.' The receptionist just looked at me in wonder for a moment and said, 'But what do I call you?' " 
 An Ottawa native, Mr. Thompson, 52, realized he had committed a cultural blunder. "Down here," he said, referring to the United States, "people automatically call one another by first names. It takes a bit getting used to for those of us who come here from other cultures."
Mr. Thompson is the chairman and chief executive of the QueryObject Systems Corporation, a publicly traded software company in Roslyn Heights. About a third of its 60 employees are foreign-born.
As the Long Island economy becomes more and more high-tech, foreign-born professionals and managers like Mr. Thompson -- Bob, if you will -- are taking their places in a culture that is often strikingly different from the ones left behind in like Russia, Poland and India, even Canada. But many high-tech executives on the Island believe that recruiting well-educated foreigners is a key to keeping the economy humming.
Their numbers will likely be increasing. Last month, President Clinton signed the American Competitiveness in the 21st Century Act. Beginning in January, it will increase the number of H-1B visas issued annually, from 115,000 to 195,000 through 2003. Individuals eligible for this visa are in "specialty occupations," including scientists, researchers, high-tech professionals, health-care workers and, inexplicably, high-fashion models. The visas last six years.
There are about 3,000 foreign workers on Long Island holding this visa, said John Bolton, an associate at Nixon Peabody, a Garden City law firm, who specializes in immigration issues. "Many of these visitors do ultimately apply for permanent residence," he said.
The competitiveness act was passed with much support nationally from the software industry, but on Long Island, support has been tepid, at least publicly. The Long Island Association, the regional Chamber of Commerce affiliate that includes significant representation from organized labor, did not support the legislation. But "the buzz among our members was that, definitely, we need to bring in more foreign workers in order to fill jobs," said Mitchell Pally, the vice president for legislative affairs.
On Long Island, the complaint is often heard that there are not enough qualified workers, although rarely is that complaint hitched to hiring foreign workers as a solution. Earlier this month, Tomo Razmilovic, the president of Symbol Technologies in Holtsville, departed from his prepared speech to an association of business executives to call attention to the shortage of skilled labor, which he identified as the single biggest challenge facing Symbol. Mr. Razmilovic, a Croatian-born Swedish citizen, did not mention the competitiveness act in his comments about the labor shortage. Indeed, few Long Island business leaders have commented on the issue.
Peter Goldsmith, the president of the Long Island Software & Technology Network, called the legislation a Band-Aid solution. "Our companies need the help now," he said. "But procedures need to be put in place to train our own people here."
Privately, many companies like Query Object have addressed the labor shortage by recruiting professionals overseas, helping them to get visas but also to deal with the mundane issues of resettlement: housing, furniture and at least minimal acculturation. Many of them first worked for QueryObject in an earlier global network of software programmers in cities like Moscow and Warsaw but also in smaller cities in China, India and Pakistan. The network was set up by a previous management team; about two years ago, new investors decided to concentrate operations on Long Island and began recruiting workers from the far-flung network to resettle on Long Island.
"The company has been very good to us," said 36-year-old Piotr Parlewicz (pronounced PEE-oat PARL-ah-veech), an information architect from Poland. "It took about four months for them to get me and my wife a visa. They rented us a house in North Bellmore. We told them we needed a bigger house, and they found us one in Hicksville. Now we are looking to buy a house. But will the government let us stay here? Who knows? We are in a suspended state."
A colleague, Grzegorz Nowakowski (pronounced GREG-orz no-vah-KOF-ski), is even more unsettled. Unlike Mr. Parlewicz, his countryman, he is not certain whether he will return to Poland or remain in the United States. "My soul is Polish, my heart is Polish," said Mr. Nowakowski with some emotion. "My friends are all in Poland. When I return there for a visit, coming back here is very difficult for me."
One thing he misses is the cosmopolitan life he knew in Warsaw. "New York City is the capital of the world, but Long Island is a place to come home after work, to sleep," said Mr. Nowakowski, 34, who is single and lives in Levittown. After work, he said, groups of co-workers sometimes go out together to movies or bookstores with cafes. "Sometimes, after work, we just play on the computers," he said with some resignation.
Another colleague, Humera Qazi (pronounced who-MARE--ah QUAH-zee), depicts herself as happily adjusted to American life. A native of Kashmir, the strife-torn border state between India and Pakistan, she was raised mainly in London. "I feel comfortable both in the West and the East," she said.
After coming to the United States on a student visa about 11 years ago, Ms. Qazi, now 28, was hired by a Long Island software company that agreed to sponsor her for a green card. When the company refused to pay for her master's degree in business, which she completed two years ago at Hofstra University, she decided it was time to change jobs. She liked the more generous tuition reimbursement policies at QueryObject and was hired there.
"I think you will find that the foreigners who come to the U.S. to work are in general very big on education," she said. "However, it is such a different system than what most of us are used to. I never got used to calling my professors by their first names, and certainly not putting my feet up on the desk."
If some of her colleagues are pining for the familiarity of home as the holiday season rolls around, Ms. Qazi is not among them. "My husband is here; I have many friends here," she said brightly. "You'd be surprised how many Kashmiri there are on Long Island. We run into each other all the time at birthdays, social events, private parties. Every day my circle seems to get bigger."

SECTION: Section 11; Page 7; Column 1; Real Estate Desk

LENGTH: 1547 words

HEADLINE: Ford Thinks Green for Historic River Rouge Plant

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA

DATELINE: DEARBORN, Mich.

 BODY:
THE Ford Motor Company revolutionized the world of manufacturing in the early part of the 20th century at its 1,100 acre industrial complex along the River Rouge here. People came from all around the world to observe a place where iron ore went into one end and a finished automobile rolled out the other a few days later.
Now the company, which is still controlled by the founding Ford family, is again trying the draw the attention of the world to the plant. It has begun a $2 billion project to build a new automobile plant on the brownfield site -- a site contaminated as a result of its earlier operations -- with a plan to actually improve the environment around it. 
 Both company officials and environmental advocates describe the new plant, on 550 acres of the factory site, as the largest industrial ecology effort in the world. "Deciding to rebuild on a brownfield is exactly the right approach, since it covers 80 to 85 percent of the environmental consequences," said Allen Hershkowitz, a senior scientist at the Natural Resources Defense Council, one of the nation's premier environmental groups. "There are 400,000 brownfields in this country that need this approach."
One of the things Ford plans to do is control the runoff of storm water, which sweeps pollutants from surfaces like buildings and parking lots directly into nearby rivers and lakes. The plan includes a -- literally -- green roof on the new factory, covered with soil and ground cover plants to retain and use rainfall, and parking lot surfaces that absorb falling water and channel it to shrub-laden channels attractive to songbirds and other wildlife.
Like everything else here, the scope of the effort is gargantuan. The 450,000 square foot rooftop, about the size of 10 football fields, will be the largest living roof on an industrial property anywhere in the world, Ford officials believe.
The story of the Rouge complex is typical of brownfields in many parts of the country. At the start of the last century, it was a marshy area in the flood plain of the slow, twisting River Rouge southwest of the then-small city of Detroit.
But it was also near the boyhood home of Henry Ford, who was coming to believe that controlling all phases of a process was the key to low-cost mass production of his popular automobiles. So he bought thousands of acres of land in Dearborn, filling in what were considered useless marshes where necessary. By the time he finished, mills at the Rouge refined iron ore into steel for use in car bodies, engine blocks and other components; melted glass for windows and headlights; produced early plastics from soybeans; and assembled the parts into Model T and Model A cars.
Almost a century later, attitudes have changed. Like other manufacturing companies, Ford wants to be in only the core of the automotive business. Today it designs cars and limits manufacturing to such critical components as engines and transmissions and final assembly. The steel plant is an independent company, and most glass and other components come from outside suppliers.
As its role changed, the future of the Rouge complex came into question. Would the company, as have so many before it, abandon an obsolete, polluted site to build gleaming new factories on paved-over farm fields in rural areas?
THE future of the Rouge apparently was determined when William Clay Ford Jr., the great-grandson of the founder, became chairman of the company early last year. "There were a lot of discussions about what to do with Rouge," he said in an interview. "When I became chairman, those discussions ended.
"This is our heritage," he added. "This was the most copied industrial facility in the world, and we are going to do it over again."
Ford officials insist that the new Rouge plant makes economic and business sense, although they concede that it will cost about 10 percent more than a conventional plant built on a rural site. The green roof on the assembly plant, they say, will be good insulation and will hold temperatures near the roof at within 5 degrees of 75 degrees, winter and summer.
Having a trained work force and a location close to the market for the products that the plant will produce are important competitive advantages, insisted James J. Padilla, the company's group vice president for worldwide manufacturing.
Guarantees of employment in Ford's contract with the United Automobile Workers Union would make it difficult to leave the Rouge plant, he said. But equally important, he said, is the cost of logistics.
"If you start shipping stampings around the world, you end up shipping a lot of air, which is expensive" because stampings are irregularly shaped, Mr. Padilla said. "You could move a plant to where labor costs are the lowest, but by the time you ship parts in and ship the vehicle out, you could end up with no economic benefit."
Part of the reason the Rouge project is possible is that the state of Michigan has progressive brownfield legislation, which calls for variable cleanup standards based on future use and provides financial incentives to undertake projects.
The state has "the best brownfields program in the nation," said Keith Schneider, program director of the Michigan Land Use Institute, a research and policy analysis group. He said legislation first passed in 1990 and later modified encourages the reuse of old industrial sites. Ford is expected to get $220 million in state and local tax credits and other incentives to assist the Rouge project, which will take more than two years to complete.
The architect for the project is William McDonough of Donough Braungart Design Chemistry of Charlottesville, Va., whom Mr. Hershkowitz describes as "the dean of green design."
Mr. McDonough said Mr. Ford had invited him to the company's world headquarters in Dearborn in early 1999 to explain his ideas for sustainable design and production. He said Mr. Ford then pointed out the window of his 14th floor office toward the nearby Rouge complex and asked, "Can you apply your thinking to that place?"
Mr. McDonough said he views the Rouge project as a "living laboratory" for design that does not deplete the environment and said the involvement of the giant automaker, which had revenues of $162 billion last year, is important in helping spread sustainable concepts. "Ford is big enough to create markets," he said, suggesting that the company's purchases will encourage the development of environmentally friendly products.
Since the Rouge complex is almost entirely built on or paved over, making room for environmental activity is taking some ingenuity. There are 100 miles of railroad lines on the complex, but 50 miles are no longer needed so they will be torn out to provide some open space.
A LARGE-SCALE effort to use vegetation to clean up contaminated land will take place on 30 acres of land near the steel mill. The land was formerly occupied by ovens to make coke. The process was dirty and contaminated the soil with complex hydrocarbon compounds.
The notion is that the plants will absorb the chemicals in their roots and change them within the plant's own chemistry into nontoxic, or less toxic, substances. The plants chosen, in association with professors from Michigan State University, will be ones native to the area, including sunflowers. "We want plants that like to grow here," one Ford official said.
There will also be an effort to improve the condition of the River Rouge, which was straightened out and lined with concrete in an Army Corps of Engineers flood control measure in 1970's. "The system works," said Timothy J. O'Brien, director of Ford's environmental quality office. "But it washes all the fish out into the Detroit River," a channel connecting Lakes Huron and Erie.
Part of the plan is to restore an oxbow bend in the river to give fish a place where the water is slowed down enough that they can hold their own. Another is to build a fish ladder in the dam that Henry Ford had built across the river to provide electric power for his estate, Fair Lane, so the fish will have access to the upper part of the river.
With an eye toward a stock market that might see environmental efforts as detrimental to shareholder interests, Ford officials say they are putting a close cost-benefit lens on the Rouge project.
"We have done time-adjusted return analyses on these projects," said George Sharp, the controller for the Rouge project. "Where the returns are better than the costs of capital, they build shareholder value."
Not every option has a clearcut answer. For example, the water runoff control program, including the green roof, is expected to cost $13 million. But regulators may impose runoff control rules that could force the company to build a conventional water treatment plant that would cost $48 million. If the green system treats the water naturally to meet the new standards, the savings to the company would be clear. But if the water treatment plant cannot be bypassed, Ford may end up paying more.
Obviously, the company's plans are not governed only by financial considerations, Mr. O'Brien said. "This place was a model of a 20th century industrial property," he said. If we do well, it could be a model for 21st century renewal."

SECTION: Section 11; Page 8; Column 1; Real Estate Desk

LENGTH: 288 words

HEADLINE: Public Plazas On Private Property

 BODY:

To the Editor:
A recent study of the 1961 zoning incentive greatly underestimates the program's vast citywide benefits "A Public Realm on Private Property," Oct. 10 .
The real property and commercial rent levies alone from the additional 16 million square feet built because developers included plazas, arcades, walkways and atriums in their projects according to mandated standards total an estimated $155 million annually. Taking into account transfer, construction and other real-estate-related taxes, that added space has produced a revenue harvest of approximately $3 billion since the program's inception. 
 On the other side of the ledger, the acquisition, improvement and maintenance of the 80 acres of public space created under the program (more than the total area of four major city parks and some of it on Manhattan's most expensive real estate) have cost the taxpayers nothing.
Finally, the overwhelming majority of the plazas and other spaces deemed "marginal" in the report (41 percent) were built prior to 1975 and 1977, the years when the standards were significantly revised. Of the 89 plazas built since then, only 7 percent were characterized by the study's authors as marginal. The revisions have essentially solved whatever design problems arose under the prior regulations.
Corrective action and enforcement are in order where public spaces on private land are locked, poorly maintained or allowed to appear privatized for one reason or another. The record shows, however, that the zoning incentive has made our city a big gainer in revenue, public space, housing units and offices to accommodate economic growth.
 
BERNARD H. MENDIK
Chairman, Real Estate Board
of New York 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: LONG ISLAND JOURNAL;
India: So Close to Home

BYLINE: By MARCELLE S. FISCHLER; E-mail: lijournal@nytimes.com

 BODY:
THE auditorium at Vedic Heritage, a Hindu temple in Hempstead, cannot be compared with Carnegie Hall. The 700-seat theater is in the basement of a former Y.M.C.A. Upstairs, a sanctuary is dedicated to Hanuman, the monkey god. The former indoor swimming pool has been turned into a shrine where congregants slip off their shoes and take offerings to their goddesses. The temple's spiritual leader, who is known as Guru Maa Saraswati or simply as Guru Maa, has a large bedroom at the rear of the complex.
But for musicians like Jasraj, a leading north Indian classical vocalist; Hariprasad Chaurasia, a virtuoso on the Indian bamboo flute; and Kelucharan Mahapatra, a master of the ancient Indian dance form called Odissi, the temple is a lucky charm. All three performed for the first time at the same event on Nov. 12 in a six-hour concert on the Vedic Heritage stage. 
 "This is my home," Mr. Jasraj said before the concert, sitting cross-legged on Guru Maa's bed and bestowing accolades upon the cleric whose stature in India is that of a senior cardinal. "This is the greatness of Guru Maa. This is what she brought together. This is spiritual music, very holy music."
Guru Maa arranged for the three superstars to celebrate the lunar holiday, Dipawali, the Indian Festival of Lights, at her temple. In the United States, the holiday, observed in India on Oct. 26, is celebrated for two to three weeks.
The serene, ruminative ragas sung by Mr. Jasraj and the dulcet tones Mr. Chaurasia created with his flute seemed mystical. But for many Indians who live on Long Island, Guru Maa's greatest achievement goes beyond the coup of bringing the three revered artists together. They credit Guru Maa, 61, with reviving the culture of India for those who have left their homeland.
"We are trying to keep our culture alive here," said Kusum Gupta of Plainview, a payroll supervisor at the Nassau County Medical Center who came to the concert in a gold satin sari. "When I came here in 1968, I was one of very few immigrants here. We didn't have much Indian society or culture or temples. We didn't have any cultural programs. We didn't have any classical Indian music like this."
"It gives us inner peace," Mrs. Gupta said outside the auditorium, as the program inside continued in Hindi. "It reminds us of our culture, how rich and how fulfilling it is. We can still have that deepness and that attachment to our roots while we are away from our country."
Dr. Shikaripur N. Sridhar, the director of the Center for India Studies established at the State University at Stony Brook three years ago, said there has been a major change in the cultural evolution of Indians on the Island over the last few years.
"The ambience has changed," Professor Sridhar said. "They have been highly educated, influential and effective in the community. Their presence is now being reflected in the community in America. They are affecting the mainstream culture."
Professor Sridhar estimates that there are more than 225,000 Indians in the New York metropolitan area and about 35,000 on the Island. Another estimate put the Long Island population at about half that number.
The profile of the Indian immigrant has changed over the last 10 to 15 years: many recent arrivals have found blue-collar jobs as taxi drivers, newsstand workers and gas-station attendants. But those who came in the first wave of Asian immigration after civil rights legislation opened the gates in 1965 were professionals, many of them in medicine, academia and information technology. Thus large numbers of Indians immediately entered the American middle class, bought homes in suburban areas and sent their children to the finest universities.
Because of their proficiency in English, Dr. Sridhar said, interacting with other Americans was easier for Indians than for other immigrants.
Still, misunderstanding of a foreign culture has caused problems, said Mukesh Malkan, Vedic Heritage's troubleshooter.
"Initially, our temple used to pay taxes even though it's a religious organization and not for profit," said Mr. Malkan, who has been coming from Paramus, N.J., to pray at the temple for 10 years. "It was a mistake by the Village of Hempstead. It was miscommunication. The village didn't accept us initially."
In July 1999, a Hempstead village police officer extinguished the temple's outdoor ceremonial fire, because he contended it was a code violation. After a series of meetings between temple and village officials, Mayor James Garner sent a letter of apology to Guru Maa last month.
There were not many Indians in Plainview when Mrs. Gupta moved there in 1968. Her Jewish neighbors would place their Hanukkah menorahs in their windows, but she would never display the candles she lighted for her own Dipawali celebration.
"There were so few, and nobody knew what our traditions were," Mrs. Gupta recalled. "Now we don't hesitate to light those candles outside the house. This is my festival. I want to celebrate. At one time we were thinking twice. Now I put out candles around my house, on my walkway, the decks, the way we used to do it in India, the way I celebrated in my parents' house."
"In years past nobody knew Indian culture," she continued. "Nobody knew Indians so much. Now you can say: 'O.K., we are part of United States. We are one of them.' "
When Dr. Purshottam Bhangdia, a Garden City anesthesiologist, came to Long Island in 1973, there were few immigrants from each of India's 26 states and only one Indian association, as the social groups are called. Now there are associations for each state and multiple Indian organizations. "If I or my wife saw an Indian in the department store or the grocery store, my wife would run and meet them," Dr. Bhangdia said. "Now there are so many."
Initially, Dr. Bhangdia said, he had to go to a temple in Queens. Now there are almost 20 Hindu temples on the Island, including those in Hicksville, Stony Brook, Hempstead, Huntington and Merrick. Muslim Indians can worship at 14 different mosques. There are also two Sikh temples, in Glen Cove and Plainview, and a Jain congregation has bought a site for a temple in Lake Success.
There is an Indian cinema in Hicksville, and another, which opened last month in Brentwood, shows only Hindi movies.
The hub of Indian life in New York had long been in Jackson Heights, Queens, but that hub is now shifting to the middle of the Island, said Dr. Sridhar of the Center for India Studies. Along Broadway in Hicksville, there are half a dozen Indian restaurants, boutiques that sell traditional jewelry and delicately embroidered saris, and grocery stores that are stocked with saffron and curries.
"There are so many Indians on Long Island now they don't have to travel to Queens," Dr. Bhangdia said. "They have everything on Long Island."
Dr. Bhangdia credited Guru Maa's Vedic Heritage organization with keeping Indian traditions alive and the culture vibrant. Prayer meetings are held in Hindi every Saturday and in English on Tuesday evenings, both followed by question-and-answer periods about philosophy and religion. Vedic rituals are taught, and yoga and meditation classes offered. There are traditional dance classes for children on Sundays.
"In this country we don't miss anything from India except our culture and parents," said Dr. Bhangdia. "Now we can get culture here. Parents are the only thing we miss."
As Guru Maa described her background, she grew up with "not a gold but a diamond spoon" in her mouth in a wealthy family in northern India. At 32, she left her husband and two sons to study with a guru, Acharya Viswanath Dev Sarma, at his institute affiliated with Calcutta University. At the institute she received a Ph.D. in 1977 in Indology, the study of Indian history, literature, philosophy and mysticism. She moved to Forest Hills in 1978, became a guru in 1981 and moved into her Hempstead quarters in 1983.
Despite their numbers and diversity, Indians on the Island tend to stick together, Guru Maa said. Regional and linguistic differences -- there are 22 major languages in India -- and the caste structure, with its four principal castes and myriad subcastes, are less of an obstacle in the United States, she said.
"I think people here are closer than in India," Guru Maa said. "Everybody here is far away from home, so we should be one together. Here that feeling of faith, feeling of love, feeling of devotion is stronger than in India. In India you will find each sect has their own spiritual head, each sect has their own guru. Here I have disciples from each part."
Dr. Sanghpriya Thakker, a psychiatrist from Manhasset who attended the concert, said the 17 Indian families in her neighborhood were from all different parts of India. "We all get together every few months at least," Dr. Thakker said. "We have Muslims, people from north India, people from south India all together. The caste system doesn't disappear, but people don't pay attention to that."
Guru Maa said she was not trying to stop assimilation. "I like this country a lot," she said. "But for a single moment I don't forget my own country. I never forget my own language. I belong to India. I am very proud that I am an Indian. I have no guilt about that, nor am I ashamed of my dress or my food. I like the Western world. Everything is so wonderful. But I don't dislike my own heritage."
Word of Guru Maa's work with Indians in the United States has reached her homeland, where she is building a Vedic Heritage center near Bombay.
Sunita Budhiraja, the concert's master of ceremonies, came from Bombay for the event. "Among artists she is well known," Ms. Budhiraja said. "She has done a great job trying to retain a culture in a different country. The house is full. It is heartwarming to see the community here keeping their roots."
Dressed in their finest traditional garb, Manesh and Raji Dave of New Hyde Park came to the concert with their children, Vrutti, 14, and Neil, 9. Recently, the family began studying classical Indian music.
"Before, they wanted to be Westernized," said Mrs. Dave, a lab technician who studied the ancient music traditions as a youngster in India. "Now more and more even the younger generation wants to learn about Indian music. It's a very good feeling inside for the youngsters to be joining in."
 
A Diverse Island
 This series looks beyond the stereotypes that sometimes taint relations between immigrants and native Long Islanders, examining immigrants' lives and how they are being woven -- or not -- into the social and economic fabric. Previous articles described the aspirations of Muslims on the Island, a Korean nail salon owner, a Colombian landscaper, an Indian liquor store owner, a Trinidadian who manages a convenience store, a Hungarian who owns a construction company and an Irish banker.

SECTION: Section 14CN; Page 1; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1713 words

HEADLINE: Standing Pat? Not Likely

BYLINE: By ROBERT A. HAMILTON

 BODY:
FOR more than 35 years, Loretta Roberge and the brothers Courtland and Carlisle Fowler have been integral members of the Mohegan Indian tribe. Courtland Fowler remembers working together in the 1970's to draft the tribe's first constitution, meeting in one another's houses because the tribe had no office, no typewriter and no telephone.
Now the tribe operates the Mohegan Sun Casino at its reservation off Route 32 in Montville, taking in an estimated $1 billion a year. During the elections to fill the nine-member tribal council this year, none of the three longtime fixtures of Mohegan government, people who were instrumental in getting the tribe federal recognition and later the right to build a casino, were returned to office. Neither was the former chairman, Roland J. Harris. 
 "We had a good idea who was going to be elected going into it," Courtland Fowler said. "I guess we just have to wait and see how it all works out." He declined further comment, and his brother and Ms. Roberge did not return repeated calls seeking comment.
As the Indian casinos mature, the tribes are replacing the older leaders who ignited their good fortunes with a younger leadership with a different vision. Two years ago, the Mashantucket Pequots, who run the Foxwoods casino, voted out Skip Hayward as tribal council chairman in favor of Kenneth M. Reels. Mr. Hayward was the leader who had almost single handedly brought the tribe together and who had successfully fought for gambling rights. When he ran for chairman once more a year later, he lost again.
Marvin B. Roffman, a gaming analyst and principal of the New Jersey-based securities firm Roffman-Miller Associates, said the elections almost seemed to be corporate power plays by members as the tribes' main business, gambling, becomes phenomenally successful.
"It's amazing what happens when the money starts rolling in and the coffers start getting filled," he said, noting that both tribes had also replaced the professional managers who were initially brought in to manage their casinos. "When they get rich and powerful they want to do more things themselves. Anybody who does business with the Indian tribes had better realize their future can be pretty tenuous."
Members of both tribes either will not discuss the issues in the campaigns or will not discuss them for attribution. Outsiders say they think that the rapid growth of the two casinos played more a part in the outcomes of the votes than any tribal governmental issues.
"When you're running to be tribal council chairman, you're also running to manage the casino and the huge revenue stream that it generates, and that's what's really at stake," said Jeff Benedict, author of the new book "Without Reservation" (HarperCollins, $26), which criticizes the process by which the Mashantuckets gained federal recognition. "The change in power at Mashantucket was driven by greed and distrust, and to some extent by racial divisions. This was a tiny tribe that has grown to more than 600 people, and it is filled with newcomers who have their eyes on the money, and the power that comes with it."
The changes continued this month when the Mashantucket Pequots elected two new members to its tribal council, Charlene R. Jones and Fatima C. Dames, who unseated Gary A. Carter, a three-term member, and Antonio M. Beltran, who had served one term.
Officials for both tribes said the leadership changes were simply part of the democratic process and no different than the results of many first selectmen and mayoral races around the state every two years. As time passes, the electorate often seeks new leadership, they said.
"A tribal nation is like any other democratic government, and sometimes that's what happens," said Joseph A. Colebut, a Mashantucket, and the tribe's director of state governmental affairs. "We have elections every year, and the people's voices are heard."
Mark F. Brown, the new tribal chairman for the Mohegans, acknowledged that there was a perception that the elections represented some sort of a coup and said that this notion had to be overcome.
"People get the idea 'This guy has a machine gun and wears a beret' -- it's definitely an issue," Mr. Brown said. "But I think when they see what this is really all about, people will realize it's not a major change in the government at all. Mostly what people need to see is that there are no drastic changes taking place."
Certainly there are financial incentives to being a councilor in both tribes.
The 1,350 Mohegan members receive an annual stipend of $10,000 as well as educational, housing and other benefits. For council members, the stipend is $218,000; the vice chairman receives $318,000; and the chairman gets $408,000.
Though the Mashantuckets have never disclosed stipends to tribal members or councilors, published reports have said the payments are about twice as much as for the Mohegans.
Mr. Hayward, the newly re-elected chairman of the Mashantuckets, who was unavailable for comment, was first elected chairman in 1975, when there were only 33 members on the tribal rolls and he was the only tribal member living on the reservation.
At the time he was clearing trees for housing and trying to entice other members to move back.
Among his earliest economic development efforts included a pizza restaurant, a greenhouse where he raised hydroponic lettuce, and a maple syrup operation. But it wasn't until the tribe had opened a high-stakes bingo hall on the reservation in 1986 that it began to make large amounts of money. The casino opened in February 1992. Membership in the tribe now stands at about 650.
Mr. Reels was no newcomer to the Pequots when he was first elected chairman in 1998. He had worked for the tribe since 1987 and was elected to the seven-member council in 1991.
It was widely recognized by local government officials that Mr. Reels wanted to put the Mashantuckets on a firmer business footing.
One of the first actions of the new council was to close a shipyard in New London that built high-speed ferries; it had been a pet project of Mr. Hayward, a former pipe fitter at the Electric Boat shipyard in Groton, but the shipyard never received enough orders to become profitable.
Mr. Colebut declined to discuss any issues concerning the tribal council race two years ago, however. "I really don't want to get into issues or personalities," Mr. Colebut said. "The main thing is there is a tribal government process, and change is incorporated into that process."
At Mohegan, Mr. Harris, the former chairman, had been involved in the planning of the casino, which opened in 1996, and in a $1.1 billion expansion that is underway and that is to include a new casino, a 10,000 seat arena, 34-story hotel and convention center, and retail shops. He was unavailable for comment.
He and Jayne Fawcett, a former vice chairwoman who is still a member of the tribal council, were the top vote getters in a preliminary round of balloting in early August, when 56 candidates for office were narrowed to 18 candidates for the final ballot later that month.
The Mohegan historian Melissa J. Fawcett, the daughter of Jayne Fawcett, said throughout the tribe's history, which has been documented for hundreds of years, there has been more turnover in the political offices than in some of the more traditional positions, so the election results there should not have come as a surprise.
Since the American Revolution, the Mohegans have only had three medicine women, "but by contrast, we've had a bazillion chiefs and chairmen -- that's the nature of the beast," she said.
"Our belief is that the right person appears at the right time," Ms. Fawcett said. "It's a spiritual belief that the creator is guiding us to who or what we need as a people, as long as we're doing the right thing, as long as we give thanks and maintain our traditions.
"Getting elected doesn't mean you are the best person in the tribe, or the most respected person," she continued. "It just means that that person is what the tribe needs at that time. To say, 'They threw that great guy Roland Harris out' -- and he is a great guy -- is really too simplistic."
Mr. Brown, the winner, said he thought the vote was a response to his campaign promises to maintain communication with the membership.
"This entity was growing very quickly down here, and I think people felt a little isolated from what was going on," Mr. Brown said. "This is their casino, so why shouldn't they feel a part of it?"
"If things never change, you start to get complacent, and you're not going to be doing your job as well as you could," he added. "Change is good, it keeps us on our toes."
Lewis B. Rome, a Hartford lawyer who has represented the tribe for several years, said he thought outsiders have made more of an issue of the election results than tribal members.
"Elections always cause trauma to somebody and raise concerns, but I think over time you're going to see real harmony here," Mr. Rome said. "This is a tribe that sought federal recognition for 18 long years, long before anybody could spell casino. This is not about their economic engine, it's about recreating a nation."
Sometimes when a business undergoes leadership changes, lenders become nervous. And borrowed money has been key to the expansion of both casinos.
But Mr. Roffman, the analyst, said he did not believe that the upheavals would have any effect on the tribe's ability to do business, whether raising funds for their expansion or attracting vendors.
"They are so successful, so profitable, it's hard to conceive of them having any problems like that," Mr. Roffman said. "It would be easier for them to borrow money than for Xerox. They're obviously dealing from a position of considerable strength."
John E. Echohawk, director of the Native American Rights Fund, said he saw nothing unusual about the progression that is taking place at the two Connecticut tribes, because the same thing happens with some regularity at tribes that don't have large casinos.
"Sometimes things change, and sometimes they don't, and you can't always say why," Mr. Echohawk said. "I don't know as it's possible to make any general observations about what happened, other than it's politics."

SECTION: Section 2; Page 34; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk

LENGTH: 1980 words

HEADLINE: ART/ARCHITECTURE;
When Getting to It Is Part of a Museum's Aesthetic

BYLINE: By HERBERT MUSCHAMP

 BODY:
MICHAEL MALTZAN has designed a perfect museum for the work of Ed Ruscha. However, Maltzan's clients, the powers at the Museum of Modern Art, want to use it for paintings by van Gogh, Cezanne, Picasso and Matisse. Well, if they insist.
The MOMA Swingline Building, as this project is now called, is to open in 2002. Housed in a former office supply factory in Long Island City, Queens, it will display works from the permanent collection and changing exhibitions during the expansion of the museum's midtown campus. Reachable by the No. 7 subway line, the Swingline is not far from P.S. 1, the contemporary art venue with which the Modern formed an alliance two years ago. 
 For bicoastal types, the project will recall the Temporary Contemporary in downtown Los Angeles, the converted bus garage used for exhibitions while the nearby Museum of Contemporary Art was under construction. Like the Temporary, it signifies a moment of cultural transition. More than any event in recent decades, the Temporary (now known as the Geffen Contemporary) changed the cultural face of Los Angeles. The Swingline could help reinvent the Museum of Modern Art.
Maltzan, who is based in Los Angeles, has designed a destination logo, a variation on what Robert Venturi once called the "bill-ding board." The building itself is a box, a shed with given dimensions, topped with a roofscape of black and white boxes that spell out the MOMA brand. But the architect's intention is to connect this finite object to the larger cityscape and to the experience of leaving behind the great center of Manhattan. "Those Who Go" and "Those Who Stay," Boccioni's masterpieces of Futurist painting, could be the presiding images for the task of enticing visitors to escape inertia and venture into Outer Borough space.
If you've visited P.S. 1, you know that getting there is integral to the aesthetic experience. Since the publication of Brian O'Doherty's 1976 essay, "Inside the White Cube," context has been a critical factor in modern art. Surroundings for art are psychological as well as visual. They connote different value systems.
To get to P.S. 1, for example, you must first break through the psychological barrier of leaving Manhattan. Moments later, you have a radical change of scene. You shift from the verticality of Manhattan to the horizontality of Queens, from the subway's underground tunnel to its elevated tracks. For a few minutes, you are treated to the aerial, Peter Pan perspective of gliding through streets or just above rooftops. The view from the window is blurred.
Given the emphasis modern artists have placed on found objects, it is not difficult to accept the trip through urban space as part of the architecture of destination. It is an unfolding, processional space, a modern equivalent of the organized exteriors and interiors that lead us into and through Baroque buildings. Over the years, the Modern and other museums have handed us different lenses for viewing this experience: works by Cezanne, Braque, Leger, Feininger, O'Keeffe, Scheeler, Tinguely, Warhol, Grooms and Ruscha, among others. Consider Maltzan's project a pair of designer frames.
The roof of the Swingline MOMA is the building's main facade. It is a composition of black boxes, arrayed across the roof like a sculptural relief. (Some boxes conceal mechanical equipment.) White letters and letter fragments spell out the MOMA logo, a motif many of us associate with the museum's shopping bags and consumer advertising campaigns. The logo becomes fully legible only when you are just in front of the building, or nearly so. Approach it from either side, by train or street, and the boxes read as a blur of abstract shapes.
The facade does more than identify a place. It signifies a shift in mentality. The black surfaces and geometric forms of the boxes recall the Modern's Bauhaus period, or sculptures by Tony Smith, Clement Meadmore or Tony Rosenthal. The lettering, meanwhile, gradually composes itself into the image of a corporate brand. The roofscape changes, as you watch, from art to commerce. I suspect Maltzan intended this to be seen as a subtle critique of the Modern's current expansion into marketing. Are his clients in on the joke?
The street facade is a paint job: a two-tone, horizontal articulation of the two-story space within: white for the ground floor, a steely blue, or perhaps orange (yes!), for the mezzanine level above, the two sections divided by a recessed metal strip. The entrance is a glass box, outfitted with sliding doors, the MOMA logo frosted across them, the letters forming variations on MMoMoMAMAAo as the doors open and close, like the windows of two subways passing. And so the street facade projects the same mordant message as the roof facade. Think color field painting crossed with upscale shopping, Clement Greenberg morphing into Charles Saatchi.
Until now, the Modern has never given us an architecturally ambitious interior. This is one reason the sculpture garden stands out. Maltzan, however, has made up for this with a large, spatially complex lobby unmatched even by Yoshio Taniguchi's design for the midtown expansion. A two-level area occupying nearly a quarter of the building's 140,000 square feet, the lobby is a transitional space between an industrial urban context and exhibition galleries. Within the space are desks for ticketing and information, galleries for artists' projects, lavatories, cloak rooms and such.
BUT the main event here is space: room for looking up, looking down, looking around, just looking. Here, the processional sequence shifts to the dream logic of reversal. The city grid is replaced by diagonals. Sharp angles soften into curves. Space flows freely up and down staircases and ramps, creating multiple pathways within what amounts to an interior landscape: a fixed but flowing garden of sculptural void. As if to compensate for the flat, neutral lobby of the mother institution, this entrance recalls the plastic, expressive spaces of mid-century modern design. It really is a Swingline.
The main exhibition areas are beyond, in a vast, two-story, loftlike box, to be configured with partitions as curatorial needs dictate. Thus the Swingline continues by architectural means the theme of the Modern's current show, "Open Ends," and its belief in the idea that the modern is not a closed historical chapter. That is the continuing contribution to history that the idea of modernity has to make: history is a work in progress. Past, present and future live through us.
Michael Maltzan, too, is a work in progress. Born on Long Island in 1959, he was educated at Harvard and trained in the Cambridge, Mass., office of Machado & Silvetti, architects known for their tightly controlled, not to say rigid, aesthetic. Later, he worked in the office of Frank O. Gehry, where he was project designer for the fabulous Walt Disney Concert Hall, now under construction. Since leaving Gehry's office in 1995, Maltzan has been preoccupied with synthesizing these extremes of the super-rational and the hyper-intuitive. It's an impossible thing to do, of course, but the tension between them could drive Maltzan toward a mature vocabulary of his own. The Swingline project takes a big step in that direction.
The Museum of Modern Art deserves great credit for choosing Maltzan, not just because the choice shows faith in the work of younger architects, or because this particular architect has produced a good design, but because the project displays a greater willingness to reckon with institutional stagnation than the Modern has heretofore indicated.
If you are interested in modern art, then on some level you care about the idea of the "true self." The concept comes from D. W. Winnicott, the British psychoanalyst who specialized in child development. The term signifies a being unburdened by the expectations of others. To unload the burden, it helps to be nurtured by what Winnicott called the "good enough mother": not perfect, but able to sustain a reasonable balance between presence and absence, discipline and play, bonding and separation.
Probably many of us were first drawn to the Museum of Modern Art because it embodied some grown-up version of the good enough mother, an authority figure who could guide us toward our true selves. We could get angry and frustrated with the Modern, but it would be there the next morning, a showcase for artists who knew that something was not being said and discovered it was up to them to say it. The galleries recounted a history of previously unspoken statements. They aroused the desire to be heard, even in those who were far from formulating what needed to be said or in what medium.
The Modern is no longer a good enough mother. It's more like Mommie Dearest: too busy playing MOMA to nurture itself or others. Like other New York cultural institutions, the Modern's idea of success now appears to rest on a solid foundation of comfort with dullness, self-promotion and entitlement. Combined with the museum's aggressive ventures into e-commerce, this comfort with listless programming has produced an identity crisis. Taniguchi's design for the museum's expansion, I fear, is beginning to look like a reincarnation of the old Alexander's store on Third Avenue. It may turn out to be the showroom for a mail order catalog. Pick out your favorite Czech poster, then go online and buy one.
These are the issues Rem Koolhaas was reckoning with in his 1997 design for the museum's expansion. The study model included a tower, labeled "MOMA Inc." The tower was capped with a golden square, called "the aura," in the manner of an Egyptian obelisk. The gesture was meant to signify the museum's awareness of itself as a powerful institution in a global economy that relies increasingly on culture as a form of international currency (think Bilbao). Presented as a study of ideas rather than a formal solution, the project also took a straightforward look at the Modern's place in the contemporary city where culture is rapidly displacing manufacturing in the economic base.
When Koolhaas was rejected as a finalist, the museum let it be known -- to the architect, among others -- that it did not consider the proposal a serious one. The Modern took the position that having convinced himself that he could not win the commission, the architect had decided to send it up. Koolhaas denied this, with good reason. His proposal was neither more nor less cynical than any other design to come out of his studio.
This is the way Koolhaas works. He confronts institutions of power with analytic clarity. It is the basis of his imaginative gifts. Why invite him to the party and expect him to be someone else? Koolhaas was being the good enough mother. He was showing how the reality of the museum's present condition could be creatively transformed into a way of rejuvenating itself. I didn't care who got the commission. What I care about is the Modern's capacity for surprise.
COULD the Swingline project become the reincarnation of the Modern? Looking at this design, I think of the scene in "Who's Afraid of Virginia Woolf?" in which George and Martha start bickering about their imaginary child, and Martha goes on and on about how "the little bugger" is the one pure, shining thing that hasn't been dragged down by her brutal, drunken wreck of a marriage. Perhaps the Modern, too, wants to be saved from its false self. And its little tot will be real.
It's easy to get to Queens, but not comfortable to imagine going there. That discomfort may be the best thing that the Swingline project has going for it. Like a Ruscha picture, Maltzan's design is a still shot from a motion sequence. Maltzan has described the design as a kind of essay on the experience of getting to the art. Which is kind of a great description for art itself.

SECTION: Section 14LI; Page 1; Column 1; Long Island Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 2245 words

HEADLINE: The Store They Love and Hate

BYLINE: By DAVID WINZELBERG

DATELINE: HUNTINGTON STATION

 BODY:
RICHARD BALSANO became a customer of the Home Depot long before his baseboard heating pipe picked a bad time to spring a leak. "I had to get stuff to fix it right away," he said. "Where else are you going to go on a Sunday night?"
The Huntington resident and do-it-yourselfer said the convenience of the superstore was its major attraction. "You don't have to go to more than one place," he said. 
 But for Mr. Balsano and other Huntingtonians, that place was no nearer than Farmingdale or Commack or Jericho. That's why there's real excitement over the opening on Thursday of a local Home Depot, right here on New York Avenue in Huntington Station. Not since the Great Pumpkin has something this big and orange been so anxiously awaited.
The Home Depot, a handyman or -woman nirvana, has become part of the suburban landscape. Americans spend more money on home improvements at the Home Depot than anywhere else. Long Island is a major part of the company's success: all 15 of the stores already open on the Island exceed the corporate sales average.
But not everyone is happy about the Home Depot's arrival here. Local government officials may tout the store as a boon to the economy, but homeowners in the neighborhood behind the site are worried about its reputation for noise, traffic congestion and litter. Nearby hardware stores, garden centers and lumber yards may offer better customer service, but they sometimes wither and die when a new Home Depot shows up. The company may be known for great bargains, but many customers are put off by the crowds and heavy-industry ambience.
Bernie Case, a Huntington resident, said he prefers the smaller hardware store. "They still take the time to talk to you and they know you," he said. "Also I'm in and out. I go to Home Depot for big projects, when I have to buy lumber."
Even Mr. Balsano said he wouldn't consider himself a regular customer of the new Home Depot until he has had a chance to shop there. "It will depend on the experience and how much traffic there is," he said. "On certain days it can be pretty rough."
One thing everyone agrees on: a new Home Depot has an impact on the community. Here are how some of the interested parties view the situation in the Huntington Station area.
 
THE MOM AND POPS
Little Guys Wary Of 900-Pound Gorilla
Laurette Prisco was getting low on doughnuts. As a slow stream of customers entered the store her father and uncle built nearly half a century ago, she greeted them by their first names and offered them a late Saturday morning snack.
The doughnuts are a weekend tradition at Lamberta's Hardware, which has been run by Mrs. Prisco, 52, and her husband Charlie, 53, for a quarter century.
"A couple of people have asked me, 'Where's the coffee?' " Mrs. Prisco said. "Maybe I should think about putting in a coffee maker."
There may not be much time. Lamberta's, a 3,000-square-foot store at 160 Depot Road, is less than a mile from the new Home Depot, which is nearly 40 times bigger.
The Priscos sell convenience and service. Their bread and butter is the local customer who needs one box of brads or one can of paint and doesn't want to drive all the way to Farmingdale to spend 10 minutes dodging Home Depot fork trucks just to find the item, followed by 10 minutes in a checkout line.
Mrs. Prisco pointed to a man on his way to his car. "I got that guy out in less than a minute," she said. "That's not going to happen over there."
Some of her customers are in no hurry, lingering to look at the family photos by the cash register or to talk politics or the weather. Mrs. Prisco has time to chat. You won't get that at a Home Depot either.
Mr. Prisco was in the back of the store, delivering the other half of Lamberta's promise. "He's Mr. Fixit," Mrs. Prisco said. "People are always bringing in stuff we don't usually repair. They'll ask him to rewire a lamp or fix a fan. But he doesn't like to turn anyone down, so he tries."
Mr. Prisco acknowledged that when Home Depots opened in Farmingdale and Commack in 1990, Lamberta's suffered, especially in sales of bigger-ticket items like power tools. But it survived, and he said he is cautiously optimistic.
"We're not as worried as when they first came into the area," he said.
Frank Cosentino, the owner of County Line Hardware at 173 West Hills Road, two miles from the Home Depot, said his store is having its best year ever, and he is more concerned with a downturn in the economy than his newest competition.
"We're never going to match them for the variety and breadth of inventory," he said. "But we have a big edge in service. We're on a first-name basis with 90 percent of our customers."
But for many consumers, price is the bottom line. A recent check on some national brand items showed Home Depot prices as much as one-third lower than those at two local independent hardware stores. A 45-gallon Rubbermaid plastic trash can was $27.99 at the independent store and $18.92 at the Home Depot. A quart of Minwax stain was $12.99 at the mom and pop and $8.97 at the Home Depot. One hundred feet of Romex electrical wire, $16.89 at the independent, was $11.00 at the orange superstore.
There were seven hardware stores in downtown Huntington when Harry Bifulco opened Save-On Hardware at 169 New York Avenue 43 years ago. "We're the last of the Mohicans," said the 85-year-old nuts and bolts man. He worried about his son Harry and daughter-in-law Barbara, who will be running the family business, and he is unhappy about the behemoth they now must battle.
Mr. Bifulco said it was unfair that the town gave special treatment to the Home Depot. "They get a tax break and we get summonses for displaying garbage cans in our driveway," he said.
An air of melancholy came over Mr. Bifulco as his eyes made a quick inventory of his store. "We've watched it grow for all these years," he said. But he also said he expected his store to survive. "Do you know why?" he asked, and then pointed to a sign behind the counter that read: "They can't. We do."
 
THE EMPLOYEES
200 in Search Of a Solid Future
After three years at a health care company, where he was in charge of disposing bio-hazardous waste, Robert Antonelli was ready for a fresh start. When he drove by the Home Depot site and saw the big orange and white sign looming high above New York Avenue, he decided to fill out an application. "I was looking for a secure, stable company with growth opportunities," he said. "And it's close by."
The Home Depot will staff its Huntington Station location with about 200 people, most living within a short distance of the store, said its manager, Jim Duffy.
Mr. Antonelli, 32, lives in South Huntington a few miles south of his new job, which began in October. During the weeks preceding the opening he has been taking a course on the hundreds of products for sale throughout the seven aisles of the flooring department.
"You have to know a lot of things, like what kinds of adhesives to use and how to set different types of flooring on different surfaces," Mr. Antonelli said. He will also have to get used to the predawn calisthenics and company cheers that employees perform every morning.
"It's like stretching," said Felicia Tracey, a kitchen designer and transfer from the Commack store. "It gets you up and happy for the day."
Starting wages at the Home Depot are between $11 and $22 an hour, with stock options offered to every employee. Many longtime employees are rich from their stock purchases, and employee loyalty is high.
"I bleed orange," said Jim Devine, a flooring manager. He has been with company for 11 years, working previously in Paramus, N.J., and Freeport.
"Flooring is the most difficult department in the company, both physically and mentally," he said. "There is a lot of heavy merchandise and you have to be good at math."
Fiddling with a tape measure he pulled from the pouch of his orange apron, Mr. Antonelli said he is ready for the challenge. "I'm anxious to get started."
 
THE NEIGHBORS
Worries of Trucks Rattling the Dishes
From his front door, Martin Alvo has an unobstructed view of the new Home Depot garden department and the store's four loading docks. In a few days he will also see customer traffic, and that has him worried.
Horizon Drive, the street that trucks will use to enter the Home Depot, fronts the 22 town houses on Horizon Court and crosses a foot path used by students coming to and from Huntington High School a few hundred yards west of the store on Oakwood Road. Mr. Alvo said he fears a tragedy in the making. "I asked a town official, 'What are you going to do when a kid gets killed crossing the street?' and he told me, 'Kids get killed in traffic accidents every day.' "
As president of the Horizon Court Homeowners Association, Mr. Alvo has been monitoring the Home Depot's preparations for 15 months. He said he is glad to see a revival of the Big H shopping center, where the megastore is opening. "The place was dilapidated," he said. But he believes the planning was flawed.
"It was so easy for them to do it so nobody would be affected," Mr. Alvo said as he spread a large blueprint of the shopping center across his kitchen counter. Horizon Court homeowners are still waiting for the sound-abating fencing and the additional $40,000 of landscaping that was promised by shopping center officials, and is now part of the newly amended site plan.
The Home Depot will be open from 5 a.m. to midnight six days a week and 7 a.m. to midnight on Sundays, but the town has barred truck deliveries to the site from 8 p.m. to 8 a.m. to limit noise. Another concession to the neighbors was to build curbs that will direct traffic leaving the store back north on Horizon Drive. Designed to prevent cars and trucks from cutting through the residential streets behind the center, it will also increase traffic in front of the homes on Horizon Court.
"I feel sorry for Marty. He's really got it bad," said Michael Herman, who lives on Panorama Drive behind the new store and has been a persistent thorn in the sides of several town officials. "They don't want to let everyone know what's going on."
Despite having the traffic forced back onto Horizon, Mr. Herman is anticipating more cars and trucks on the streets of his neighborhood. "New York Avenue is the only legitimate access to the shopping center. It should have all been thought out with a legitimate study," he said. "I'm not opposed to Home Depot. I'm opposed to the way they did it."
 
THE DEVELOPMENT
Jobs, Tax Revenues, An Economic Spark
"Prior to Home Depot it was becoming an eyesore and a blight," George Hoffman, a town spokesman, said of the Big H shopping center. "Now, along with Old Navy and the Outback Steakhouse, this will be a vibrant economic center for the area."
RD Management, a New York City company that owns more than 200 shopping centers across the country, bought a 50 percent stake in the Big H in the early 1980's and took full control in 1998, about the same time that the Home Depot was secured as an anchor tenant. A Kmart is being built next door and should open next year.
To grease the wheels, the Town of Huntington has given tax breaks. A town official confirmed that RD Management will receive a tax abatement that cuts the shopping center's $1 million property tax burden to $500,000 now, increasing $50,000 each year for the next 10 years.
The Huntington town supervisor, Frank Petrone, said the Big H's new tenants will be a great boost to the center, which he said had become rundown and "crime infested." A town committee has been interviewing engineering firms to consult on coordinated retail, commercial and residential development for the Huntington Station area.
"We have a jewel here," Mr. Petrone said. "We'd like to build an economic center around the railroad station. The revival of the center is going to open up other possibilities for the area."
(Similar hopes are pinned on the Home Depot that opened in Hempstead in June. "It's one of the best things that have happened to this village," said Mayor James Garner. An Old Navy is scheduled to open there in the spring and Stop and Shop is planning to put a store nearby.)
The Big H renaissance pleases longtime tenants. At the New China restaurant, the owner, Charlie Ching, recalled a few good years from his opening in 1986 until the early 1990's, when the Pathmark supermarket closed and Big H fell on hard times. "The shopping center was quiet," Mr. Ching said. "Everybody moved out. Now I'm so happy. Things are working out. I've been waiting for a long time."
Dennis Portillo, manager of Randazzo's Pizzeria, a Big H tenant since 1992, said: "Before we were having a hard time. Now it will be fantastic."
When he became manager of the Huntington Home Depot on Sept. 1, Mr. Duffy wasn't sure how it would all come together. "I was looking at four walls and a ceiling. It was a little daunting," he said. About five million units of merchandise, more than 40,000 different products, began arriving in mid-October, beginning with kitchen cabinets, faucets and floor tile. The stream of goods will continue to flow into the 114,000-square-foot store right up to the day before the opening, when the Christmas trees will be delivered.
Once the store opens, the manager estimated that 15 truckloads a day will be required to keep the shelves stocked.
"I expect us to be very busy," Mr. Duffy said.
