SECTION: Section 2; Page 33; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk

LENGTH: 2255 words

HEADLINE: ART/ARCHITECTURE;
Can Design In America Avoid The Style Trap?

BYLINE: By PHILIP NOBEL; Philip Nobel's most recent article for Arts & Leisure was about the Airstream trailer.

 BODY:
DESIGN is the art that is hidden in plain sight. In the United States, those design disciplines not prefaced by "fashion" have long been the neglected stepchildren of culture and commerce. But unlike fashion, which trades in distant desire, the work of product designers, graphic designers and architects is all around us: sneakers and staplers, trivets and towers, Web sites and Weber grills.
By many measures, design has never been more popular in this country; new magazines line the shelves, design-intensive products turn shoppers' heads. But design has gained its current popularity by aping the devices of its sociable cousin, couture: guru stars, a rolling fashion cycle, collaboration with eager media. Following the success of Wallpaper, which pioneered the design-as-couture formula in the press, mainstream magazines are joining the design trades in overheated boosterism of the field. In March, Time announced "The Rebirth of Design," followed by the portentous sub-head, "Function Is Out, Form Is In"; the cover of the September Playboy featured a playmate cradling a baby blue iBook. 
 Are we witnessing the maturing of American design? Discriminating consumers have long bemoaned the sorry state of design here -- ignored by the majority; ghettoized in a few SoHo shrines -- and designers have often expressed envy for the European scene, where their colleagues are household names and even small towns have stores selling chic wares. America may finally be catching up. Today, shoppers outside of the urban bastions of high design can easily find a celebrated specimen to buy: a Zip drive, the VW Beetle, an OXO Good Grips spoon. Not since the 1950's and 60's, when an optimistic futurism transformed the look of everything from houses to housewares, has there been such a penetration of designerly design into everyday arenas.
At the same time, what exactly is meant by "good design" has never been more vague, and many designers, lacking firmer principles, aspire only to a place in the glamorized design economy. Is good design the perfection of an object for commercial success? For the glory of the designer? For beauty? For glamour? For use?
The first annual National Design Awards, announced in September at the White House and presented earlier this month at the Cooper-Hewitt National Design Museum in New York, offer an occasion to examine these unpopular questions. They also beg others. The awards, organized by the Cooper-Hewitt, an arm of the Smithsonian, are timely but tentative. Frank Gehry, the master of the Guggenheim Museum in Bilbao, won the lifetime achievement award; for its effective use of design as a weapon in the marketplace, Apple won the corporate achievement award. The exhibition designer Ralph Appelbaum, the inventor Paul MacCready and the landscape architect Lawrence Halprin were recognized with midcareer awards. A sixth category, American Originals, honored John Hejduk, the mystic architect who died in July, and Morris Lapidus, whose festive modern hotels have been beloved by the public but scorned by critics for most of his 98 years. The jury included the cerebral architect Daniel Libeskind and Martha Stewart, who arrived at the deliberations with a warm blueberry pie.
In some ways the current design vogue is a reprise of the 1980's, when every redone kitchen featured a trophy Alessi teapot. But just as our prosperity -- and decadence? -- has surpassed that era, the glory of design is now more complete. With a new intensity, pop culture has discovered that design can be glamorous and designers have discovered that glamour sells.
But glamour has its limits. Design, tied as it is to life through use, should have larger ambitions than to feed a market hungry for new images of affluence. What is lost when glamour reigns?
One place to look for answers is Lafayette Street in Lower Manhattan, a retail strip that has exploded with the city's economy. In 1994, the street was home to one store selling vintage modern furniture; now there are a dozen, making it a mile-long carnival of laminate amoebas at prices that would have shocked their designers.
The work of Charles and Ray Eames is a Lafayette Street staple. During and after World War II, the Eameses excelled at creating elegant products in the humble new materials of plywood and plastic. They did so with an eye on fashion, but glamour was always secondary to the quotidian problem at hand: a simple chair ($1,500 on Lafayette Street); a splint developed for the Navy ($500).
Much of what is hawked on Lafayette Street is from the family of products endorsed in the 1950's by the Museum of Modern Art's bluntly named Good Design program. That initiative hailed products -- Gerber steak knives, Aalto vases -- that toed the museum's sensual modernist line: clean forms to suggest clean living; no populist politics, please. What was "good" then could be determined by that dogma. But those were simpler times. Since the collapse of the modern movement and its mandarins, it has been an open field. The Postmodern style, too, is long gone, but its critique of modernism -- down with rigor, up with choice -- remains. Everything goes.
This could be a golden age. Designers of all types, with their powerful tools, can and do make everything the market will bear. But, freed from a dominant style, design is faced with the style trap: the limits imposed by glamour.
Nothing could be more American than to reward victory in the marketplace, and the last few years have seen some incredible victories: not just in the iMac, which saved Apple with its fruit-stripe colors, but in more modest products as well. The drugstore toothbrush rack has become a circus of ergonomic grips, flexing heads and prehensile gum-probes -- an entire retail sector transformed by design.
Function is not ignored in these modish redesigns -- of toothbrushes or tennis shoes or kitchen tools -- but they introduce products to a cycle that reduces its importance; when design is glamorized, it begins to solve problems of image first, and problems of use -- living -- second.
The classic Swingline staplers were redesigned last year in see-through iMac hues. The designs were a success; they were featured in the magazines and included in the Cooper-Hewitt's Design Triennial, a sister project to the National Design Awards. Now, having abandoned a design that had gone unchanged since the 1950's, designers will have to re-imagine the staplers every few years to keep their glamour alive. From the moment they hit the shelves, there was a countdown to the next style change: the exact mechanism that triggered the glories and failures of American automobile design, after cars began to be available in colors other than black.
Once in the cycle of forced design and redesign -- the style trap -- it can be hard to escape. Unless it achieves a new iconic status, a tall order in an accelerated market, a newly glamorized product can go from timeless to ephemeral, functional to fabulous, humble to heroic -- and then disappear. One such icon after another might succumb, not because of failed functionality or market appeal, but because it couldn't survive the transplant to the hothouse of prime time design.
To keep from being dazzled, designers need steadier principles, something more lasting than each season's shifting hemline height. They might look to an American tradition that lends itself to design: can-do, make-do problem solving. For every design project starts with a problem, even if it is just how to distinguish one product from the next on a shelf.
A few blocks east of Lafayette Street, in the kitchen supply shops along the Bowery, a collection of enduring American design is on permanent display. It's a world of self-effacing classics that intersect with fashion at their own pace: Revere Ware, Metro shelving, and all the homely accouterments of a Tuesday at the diner: the milk can with the tricky lid, fluted glass sugar dispensers, Pyrex bubble coffee pots.
The same spirit can be found on the Bowery's sidewalks -- and sidewalks all over the country -- in ad hoc, kit-of-parts lampposts. Or on the street: The UPS truck, cobbled from sheet metal, is a rolling testament to enduring design. Its style is accidental, not an image-conscious exercise in the latest mode: the result of solving a problem with the materials at hand.
The grid is not an American invention, but it's a strategy that flowered here, from facades to the mile-square divisions of the Midwest: a perfect market-driven makeshift that became a defining style. Like a skyscraper's steel cage or the standardized wood framing, it evolved to solve a problem -- how to build in America, where the land is vast, the materials are cheap and labor is scarce -- and it left its mark long after that problem was solved. IKEA, a Swedish import, has said it best. When it opened its first stores here in the mid-1990's, in a bid to become the Sears of the design economy, IKEA used this slogan: "It's a big country. Someone's got to furnish it."
This alternative course is much quieter, but more entrenched than design glamour, and as eccentrically homegrown as hot rod culture or jazz. It's a way of working that mints few marketable geniuses, but that hews close to the genius of American material culture: design that is market-driven but media averse.
ARCHITECTURE is still several generations ahead of the other design fields in deploying glamour at the national level, but it may not be profiting from that distinction. Its stars are genuine American celebrities -- Philip Johnson is in the glossies now pitching Absolut vodka -- and this prominence has raised awareness among Americans that architecture exists, that their fellow countrymen are involved in its making and that it can be good. Susan Yelavich, assistant director for public programs at the Cooper-Hewitt, said recently that "Frank Gehry is the sole figure who will change the fact that when you say the word design, people think jeans."
But there is another lesson in Mr. Gehry's work that has been obscured by the hype surrounding his celebrity. Not just that he worked hard, paid his dues and earned the right to indulge in a signature style. But also that, since 1978, when he reclad his own house in Santa Monica, Calif., with corrugated metal and chain link, his free-form designs have been grounded in an intuitive understanding of materials. His Fishdance restaurant in Kobe, Japan, known for the giant chain-link fish sculpture out front, is also a deep meditation on the states that copper can take near the sea. The Guggenheim in Bilbao might be revered less for its petals and lobes than for its innovative titanium skin.
Similarly, the great triumphs of American architecture are not formal -- the shapes that a media-conscious architect might make to get attention or keep it -- but integral to solving problems closely allied with materials and the market. The basic components of American construction, aluminum studs and gypsum wallboard and dropped ceilings (even vinyl siding) are ways to build a lot, cheaply, with expensive labor -- the pragmatic building blocks of a lasting, rooted style.
Those materials might be an obvious place to focus design intelligence, to move the practice forward, to meet life halfway with better buildings, to find purpose and eventually poetry, from the inside out. But, in a cautionary tale that other corners of the design world might heed, the lights of American architecture are engaged elsewhere: chasing glamour.
With everything possible but every form exhausted, rising theorists of a younger generation are designing projects based on seductive, vapid "blobs" -- the hot new shape for 2000. The whole arc of American building has come to this: selfish formalism born from the need to claim space in the fashion show of the design press.
That's fine for brassieres and boas, but shouldn't we ask more from our buildings, the most profound of which still aspire to a public trust? Or from our toasters and teapots and spoons, which might yet serve some private, daily need with quiet dignity? Utility, not vanity, should drive change in design; that's one of the things that distinguishes it from art.
It is interesting that just now, as all restraints save market logic and the temptations of glamour have fallen away, there is an effort to link American design with American Pragmatism, the philosophy that sought to formalize the show-me bedrock of American thought. The Museum of Modern Art recently held a conference, "Things in the Making: Contemporary Architecture and the Pragmist Imagination," that featured heated conversations between star architects, Peter Eisenman and Rem Koolhaas, and star Neo-Pragmatist philosophers, Richard Rorty and Cornell West.
Behind that program there was a hint of subversion: the suggestion that camera-shy Pragmatism, glamorized, might be an acceptably cool intellectual cover for the application of common sense. It could also point to a way around the style trap. Not by encouraging design that is counter to the whims of the market (that would be positively un-American), but by inspiring work that might also thrive in economic down times, when the demand for star dust may abate, but a toothbrush, a stapler and a roof over one's head will still be welcome conveniences. Then, one day, we might yet have that elusive thing: a design culture that reflects the way Americans think and live, not just the way they shop.

HEADLINE: LINCOLN CENTER;
An Internal Matter

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I agree with Bernard Holland that performers and audiences have been shortchanged by the acoustics and space configurations at Lincoln Center, but turning the complex into a classical Lincstrasse, a la Vienna, as he seems to suggest, won't solve the problem "Let's Rebuild the Sound, Too, Please," Nov. 19 . 
 Better acoustics could be gained by internal restructuring. This has been done elsewhere. Backstage space could be gained, for example, by removing the first 10 to 20 rows of seats and moving the stage forward -- a matter of engineering -- as appalling as the loss of seats might be to economics-minded administrators. Who would ante up the money for razing and rebuilding? Given the present high cost of attending opera and music performances, who would be able to afford tickets in this "new Age of Pericles" that Mr. Holland describes?
 
JANET I. WASSERMAN
Manhattan

HEADLINE: COUNTY LINES;
Where Westchester and Harlem Meet

BYLINE: By JANE GROSS; E-Mail: Countylines@nytimes.com

 BODY:
IN the parking lot, hard by the Hudson River, S.U.V.'s with every conceivable bell and whistle jockey for space with battered gypsy cabs. In the produce section, sugar cane and green plantains are heaped beside shitake mushrooms and edible flowers. At the cash registers, one customer pays with an American Express gold card and the next with food stamps.
This is Fairway at 133rd Street and 12th Avenue, a 35,000-square-foot market, cheaper and more spacious than its Upper West Side cousin, that is as much a social experiment as a grocery store. It is where Westchester County and Harlem meet. 
 Where else but on the subway do the region's most pampered suburbanites meet up with poor, nonwhite fellow New Yorkers? What else but Stilton, nova, baguettes and coffee beans from the world over would bring us to this desolate stretch under the elevated portion of Riverside Drive, shadowy even in daylight and home to bus garages, soda distributors and car detailers?
For those who live on the Hudson River side of the county and drive to work on the Saw Mill River Parkway, this Fairway is a solution to the grocery store woes described in this section a few weeks ago. Weekends it's mayhem, as Upper West Siders with cars join the Monday to Friday crowd, wise to the bargains compared with Zabar's, Citarella and even the original Fairway at Broadway and 74th Street. But on weekday evenings, the store belongs to Harlem families and Westchester commuters.
A quick detour off the West Side Highway at 125th Street leads to an ample parking lot once shared by urban fishermen and prostitutes and now secured by cyclone fencing and a security patrol. These clear wintry nights, the George Washington Bridge twinkles, red on the outbound, white on the inbound.
Whatever talk there once was about the danger of shopping here has been silenced by the Giuliani crime crusade. The only risk that remains is a fender-bender, as drivers play a polite game of "After you Alfonse, after you Gaston," at a tricky intersection, all the while dodging shopping carts.
The store opened in the waning days of 1995, amid a zoning hoo-ha that involved a massive retail space in a manufacturing zone. That dispute is long forgotten. It was always less interesting than the store's unique demographics.
Would shopping there, as a privileged professional, inspire the guilt I'd suffer on Caribbean vacations, passing through shanty-towns on the way to a hotel? Or would it be the pleasing mix-and-match that Manhattan had offered, before I chose to go to sleep to the crickets and wake to the birds?
Sometimes I get that guilty feeling in the rear of the store. Here the industrial racks of olive oil and fancy mustards give way to standard-issue grocery items like Pop Tarts and Campbell soup -- and acres of Goya products. This is the territory of single mothers, trailing noisy children who must come because there is no baby sitter. Their carts are piled high with economy-size packages of chicken wings and sugary cereal.
Cut to the cheese counter, the ecologically correct paper products, the botanical soaps and shampoos, the array of nuts, dried fruits and granolas. In the center of the store, the mothers and children are replaced by yuppie couples with his-and-her cell phones, pin-striped men with expensive shoes, women whose shopping for the holidays is leisurely because a nanny minds the kids.
But sometimes on the way to the Kitty Litter or the Pledge, I venture through the aisles of rice and beans. Or I pass the ham hocks while looking for the skinless and boneless chicken breasts, in the 38-degree meat room. Smiles pass easily between shoppers, who admire one another's children or rescue an item about to topple from someone else's shopping cart.
Ray Venezia, the store's general manager, says his mission is democratic. He points to Earth's Best organic baby food, a steal at 50 cents a jar, popular with mothers, regardless of ZIP code. He says that with Fairway prices, you don't have to be Jewish to want Hebrew National cold cuts or a gourmand to buy ground ostrich at $3.69 a pound.
But the real equalizer, Mr. Venezia says, is the scene around the open buckets of picholines, manzanillas, cornichons, sun-dried tomatoes and balsamic onions. Rich or poor, black or white, urban or suburban, we all sneak a snack when we think no one is looking.

HEADLINE: Ecology Group Becomes Developer Of Hudson Site

BYLINE: By ROBERT WORTH

DATELINE: BEACON

 BODY:
THE abandoned waterfront of this old industrial town, on a grassy peninsula jutting into the Hudson, has spectacular 180-degree views of the river and the Hudson Highlands to the south. The owner of the land plans to develop it, and has drawn up an ambitious list of possible projects that includes a 150-room hotel, a restaurant, even some stores and private homes.
In many towns, the proposals would provoke angry protests from environmentalists. But in Beacon, the developer is an environmental group. 
 Scenic Hudson, based in Poughkeepsie, has been buying property in this struggling town of 14,000 for almost a decade, and now wants to help lead an economic renaissance by developing its premier piece of real estate on the Hudson.
"The waterfront was the lifeblood of the town in the 19th century, and we're hoping it will be the lifeblood again," said Steve Rosenberg, the group's director of land preservation.
The key to the group's unusual role is an effort to guide development in environmentally sensitive ways, rather than merely setting aside certain areas for preservation and letting sprawl overrun the rest, Mr. Rosenberg said. The waterfront project will not interfere with river views or harm the neighboring wetlands, and there will be a civic plaza and promenade to ensure public access to the river.
"For an environmental group to embrace growth and development is something that has been missing in our dialogue on land development for a long time," said John Nolon, the director of the land use law center at Pace University. "It is unprecedented, and one of those brilliant ideas that when it comes along, you say why hasn't somebody done this before."
The decision to develop the waterfront was not an easy one, said Ned Sullivan, Scenic Hudson's executive director. "The board decided it was no longer enough for environmental groups in general and Scenic Hudson in particular to just oppose bad development projects on the Hudson," he said. "It was time for us to proactively work with communities to demonstrate that positive waterfront development is possible."
To that end, Scenic Hudson has become partners with the Manhattan-based Dia Center for the Arts, which announced plans last year to move its collection of contemporary art -- now mostly in storage -- into a 300,000-square-foot abandoned paperboard factory just south and east of the waterfront by 2002.
Together, Dia and Scenic Hudson have worked with town and state officials on a plan to redevelop Beacon as a mecca for art lovers and tourists without damaging its natural beauty or the charm of its 19th-century mills and houses.
The town already has the Tallix Foundry, which has cast the works of many well-known contemporary sculptors. And one developer plans to build a 600-seat theater and museum in another abandoned factory building.
Scenic Hudson began buying property in Beacon, which is 60 miles north of Manhattan in Dutchess County, with more conventional environmental goals.
The group bought and set aside about 2,000 acres of forested land on Fishkill Ridge to the east of the city, including the 1,531-foot Mount Beacon, where on a clear day you can see the World Trade Center to the south and the Catskills to the northwest. It helped arrange the construction and improvement of various walking trails, including one that runs across the waterfront. And it set aside a 12-acre park at the mouth of Fishkill Creek.
But after it bought the waterfront site for $4 million in 1997, the group's leaders held several discussions with town officials and residents to ask them what they wanted to see there.
"We said to them, 'We don't need another park, we need a working waterfront,' " said Clara Lou Gould, who has been mayor of Beacon since 1990. "They, to their credit, listened to the people."
The group made plans to build a promenade, a fishing pier and a civic plaza on about 11 acres of the waterfront, and began seeking proposals for a mixed-use development on the rest of the property.
Like many cities along the Hudson, Beacon was badly in need of an economic boost after the slow loss of its manufacturing jobs and the departure of area companies like I.B.M. By the 1980's, many of the buildings on the city's Main Street were boarded up. One hotel was transformed into low-income housing, and the residents included some drug addicts. "Main Street was not a jolly place," Ms. Gould said.
At first, some residents and business owners were skeptical of Scenic Hudson's involvement, fearing it would simply take more land off the tax rolls, Ms. Gould said.
But the town's businesspeople soon recognized that Scenic Hudson's presence could help them by drawing more tourists, according to Sheila Wicklow, a co-owner of The Pie Shop and president of the Beacon Business Association.
Marc Capichioni grew up outside Poughkeepsie and is now president of Beacon Business Services, which provides temporary employees and computer services. He moved the company to Beacon six months ago. "Beacon always had a bad reputation, but it's turned itself around," he said. "Restoring the waterfront is a great idea and I think it's really helping to repopulate the area."
To many residents, Scenic Hudson's greatest contribution lay in helping broker the deal that brought the Dia Center for the Arts to Beacon.
Although the former factory was donated by its owner, International Paper, state and local economic development and arts programs are providing about $2.8 million of the $20 million cost of renovating the 70-year-old building, with the rest coming from donations.
The center expects 60,000 to 70,000 people a year to visit its collection, which includes a 430-foot-long series of paintings by Andy Warhol and a series of steel shapes weighing 150 tons by the artist Richard Serra, Michael Govan, the center's director, said.
That prospect has already begun to lure developers and Manhattan artists to the town.
"You see a lot more people dressed in black looking around for property," said Doug Berlin, a developer who moved here and began buying property himself three years ago.
One developer, Milton L. Ehrlich Inc., agreed to follow Dia to Beacon and do arts-related projects last year and has already become the town's largest landowner, spending a total of almost $10 million on 20 separate parcels, said Brian Quinn, the company's chief operations officer.
Ehrlich plans to build a 600-seat theater, studios for individual artists and an inn in one of its buildings, a 154,000-square-foot former hat and textile factory on Fishkill Creek. The performance space will be managed by a nonprofit group, as will the small art museum the company plans to build on part of its property.
Ehrlich has also proposed a plan for Scenic Hudson's waterfront property: a hotel and about 100,000 square feet of office buildings. The company, which has set up a separate operation in Beacon called Beacon Terminals Associates, is one of several companies whose proposals Scenic Hudson is now reviewing.
To developers who came earlier to Beacon, the arrival of Scenic Hudson and Dia has been a tremendous vindication, as well as a substantial boost.
When Ron Sauers moved to Beacon from Peekskill in 1989, the east end of Main Street was a row of burned-out brick buildings. He keeps a dated photograph of one block in his office to remind visitors how far the town has come. "When we started, the comments were, 'You're out of your mind,' " Mr. Sauers said. "But the beautiful thing was, it caught on."
Today, the east end of Main Street, where Mr. Sauers has his office, is an elegant row of beautifully restored brownstones, with an antique store, restaurants and a gift shop.
When he finished renovating several one-bedroom apartments over offices he had renovated on Main Street in the mid-1990's, Mr. Sauers said, he could not find anyone to rent them. Today, he has a waiting list.
Mr. Sauers and his partners plan to renovate an old mill at the eastern end of Main Street into a restaurant, inn and art gallery. Built in 1822, the long-abandoned mill sits on the edge of the fast-running Fishkill Creek, which once provided its power.
Standing inside the mill's circular interior, Mr. Sauers gazed up at its high ceiling and the mountains visible through its open windows, and conjured up the restaurant he will build here.
"The way I see it, Beacon is a puzzle, and Scenic Hudson and Dia provided the two most important pieces," he said. "As I say to other builders: we couldn't screw this up if we tried."
 
Unconventional Visions With the Sites to Match
 ALTHOUGH it is based in Manhattan, the Dia Center for the Arts maintains a 20-square-mile ranch in the desert of southwestern New Mexico to display Walter De Maria's "Lightning Field," which consists of 400 stainless steel poles arranged in a grid one mile long and one kilometer wide.
So it is hardly surprising that Dia chose an unconventional setting for its permanent collection: an abandoned factory in Beacon, a depressed Hudson River town 60 miles north of Manhattan.
Dia was established in 1974 by Philippa de Menil and Heiner Friedrich to collect the work of a small number of contemporary artists, and to commission art that would not otherwise be supported by traditional institutions, Michael Govan, the group's director, said. The group's name comes from the Greek word for "through," he added, expressing Dia's role as a conduit for art.
Dia's endowment is $26 million, and since 1995 it has raised $75 million from individuals and foundations, which will help pay the $20 million cost of renovating the building in Beacon, Amy Weisser, a spokeswoman for Dia, said.
Many of the artists Dia has supported, including Andy Warhol, Joseph Beuys, Donald Judd, Dan Flavin and Richard Serra, have had individual shows at Dia's Manhattan headquarters, but the center's permanent collection has never before been viewed in one place.
Mr. Govan first discovered the Beacon site in the early spring of 1998. He was flying up the Hudson in his private plane with a curator and an architect for an aerial view of a building Dia then owned on the river's west bank. "We flew up another 10 miles, and I saw an empty factory," he said.
The group was looking for sites for its permanent collection, and had considered other options, including a collaboration with Mass Moca in North Adams, Mass.
Two weeks later, Mr. Govan drove up to take a look and was sold immediately. "I've often said that we couldn't build a museum that was better suited if we started from scratch," he said. A number of Dia's artists, including Richard Serra and Robert Irwin, are equally enthusiastic and are helping design galleries in the building, he added.
The scale of the building was important, Mr. Govan said. It needed to be large enough to house some of the larger works in Dia's collection, including Andy Warhol's 430-foot-long series of paintings, which will be housed in its own separate 7,000-square-foot room.
The building's location on a magnificent stretch of the Hudson River, a few steps from both the Metro-North train station and Denning's Point State Park, was another attraction.
Dia is also bringing its artistic vision to the renovation of the waterfront that lies beyond its new museum. Dia and Scenic Hudson jointly commissioned George Trakas, a Manhattan-based environmental sculptor, to produce a survey and some proposed designs for the waterfront.

HEADLINE: COPING;
Parsing the Ethics Of a City's Culture

BYLINE: By FELICIA R. LEE

 BODY:
SO here we are, our waistlines thickened by Thanksgiving dinner, our heads fat with ballot counting and legal challenges. And here is Khoren Arisian, who wants New Yorkers to consider the ethics of the election process, instead of the technicalities, and how to address real issues like economic inequality.
Mr. Arisian, 68, a son of Armenian immigrants and a retired Unitarian minister, is the new senior leader of the 125-year-old New York Society for Ethical Culture. 
 Ethical Culture calls itself a humanistic religious and educational movement founded on the belief that moral growth is the basis of spiritual life. Rather than talking about God and dogma, followers see spiritual life as growing out of a search for values and ethics.
Mr. Arisian is a proud agnostic (with a divinity degree). Many of the society's members are agnostics or atheists. As might be expected, members are big on separation of church and state. In place of ritual, they involve themselves in discussions and action on social issues and community events, which can range from marches for prison reform to seminars on the plight of the homeless.
"I don't know how I can get people in the middle class involved," Mr. Arisian said the other day in his book-lined office, considering the challenge of his new position. "People seem turned inward these days."
Call it election fatigue. Or call it recount fatigue. But whatever it is, Mr. Arisian said the real issue is where we are as a nation, given the increasing divisions of region and class. Practically, Mr. Arisian wonders how the country and New Yorkers plan to combat problems like homelessness and the ever-widening gap between rich and poor. He says issues like abortion rights and the death penalty can be seen as moral and ethical problems as much as political ones. And he asks if there are questions that we do not want politicians to decide, given separation of church and state.
These are the kinds of issues he hopes the once-lively Ethical Culture Society, woefully quiet in recent years, can tweak New Yorkers to ponder.
Mr. Arisian is looking at the city and its role in shaping democracy with a fresh eye.
He comes here from his post as co-founder and executive director of the Humanist Center of Minneapolis and emeritus minister of the First Unitarian Society in that city. He sees a New York that is cleaner and richer than when he was last here 20 years ago as a member of the society's board and director of its now-defunct School of Adult Education. He also sees the homeless people living among the newly prosperous, and the working poor who deliver groceries to a comfortable middle class. The old problems of bad schools, slow trains and income inequality are still here, he said.
Both wealth and poverty are more blatant in New York. A few blocks from a line of limousines are homeless people selling old magazines and stuffed animals. More difficult to quantify is how attitudes have hardened against the poor in a city where so many are thriving. This can mean that people fail to see the suffering right before their eyes, or rationalize that there must be some explanation that does not implicate them or society. Laziness? Drug addiction?
"The quest for money has taken over," Mr. Arisian said. "More here than anywhere else."
New York's subway series mentality -- the belief that everything begins and ends here -- can make it a difficult city to engage, Mr. Arisian said.
On the one hand, the city is more liberal, secular and culturally diverse than the rest of the country. It is, he said, the only place where the seemingly oxymoronic concept of a secular religion could have emerged.
On the other hand, because the city is so self-contained, New Yorkers can feel isolated from issues like the rise of the conservative right.
The society had its heyday in the 60's and 70's, when New Yorkers by the hundreds turned up at Ethical Culture events to talk about civil rights, the Vietnam War and impeaching Richard Nixon. There were about 1,500 members then, compared with about 350 now. In its 125 years, the society helped establish the first free kindergarten in the city and the first settlement house in the country, and it helped found organizations that became the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People, the American Civil Liberties Union and the Legal Aid Society.
Most recently, the society supported a march by restaurant workers. Most were immigrants who demanded better wages and working conditions.
Mr. Arisian said he had a sense of urgency about restoring some of the society's luster. He will begin, he said, by posing the hard questions: the meaning of democracy, the place of religion in politics and what an ethical society owes the least among us.

HEADLINE: POSTINGS: Neighborhood Entrepreneurs Program;
Renovating Bronx Homes

 BODY:
New York City officials and community leaders formally opened a low- and moderate-income building last week at 2001 University Avenue in the Morris Heights section of the Bronx. The building, the largest of six being rehabilitated in the immediate area, was formerly known as a haven for drug dealers. It contains 74 apartments ranging from studios to three-bedroom units.
The rehabilitation of the city-owned buildings is taking place under the Neighborhood Entrepreneurs Program. Administered by the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development with the aid of the New York City Housing Partnership, the program aims to return city-foreclosed properties to private ownership. The University Avenue building is to be managed by Peter Magistro of Bronx Pro Real Estate. Tenants are subject to limitations on income, which may not exceed $56,200 a year. 
 "Returning this building to responsible local management will add to community redevelopment, a goal we share with the residents of Morris Heights," said Jerilyn Perine, the city's housing commissioner.
The redevelopment cluster includes buildings at 1978 University Avenue, 1691 Davidson Avenue, 1668 Davidson Avenue, 1696 Nelson Avenue and 30 Buchanan Place. When completed next fall, the project will provide a total of 174 new units of housing.

HEADLINE: WEDDINGS;
Diana Schlesinger, Robert Nassau

 BODY:
Diana Laurel Schlesinger, the director of education at the Museum of American Folk Art in New York, and Robert Leon Nassau, a filmmaker, are to be married today. Rabbi David Adelson is to officiate at a house owned by the bridegroom's parents, Adrienne and Stephen Nassau, in Sherwood, Md.
Ms. Schlesinger, 31, is keeping her name. A graduate of Oberlin College, she received a master's degree in art from the University of Wisconsin and another in museum education from the Bank Street College of Education. 
 She is the daughter of Beverly and Murry Schlesinger of New York. The bride's father retired as an assistant principal of the Booker T. Washington School in New York. Her mother is a lawyer in New York for a legal services plan run by District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees.
Mr. Nassau, 28, has produced and edited a documentary about teenage girls with emotional or drug and alcohol problems who bicycled across the Continental Divide. His mother retired as a senior project manager and financial analyst for the World Bank in Washington. His father is a partner in O'Toole, Rothwell, Nassau & Steinbach, a Washington law firm.

HEADLINE: At Crossroads, a Dramatic Pause

BYLINE: By JILL P. CAPUZZO

DATELINE: NEW BRUNSWICK

 BODY:
THEATER season is in full swing here the State Theater's flashing marquee lists a packed schedule of music, dance and drama. Next door, the George Street Playhouse is finishing up its season-opening drama and getting ready for a musical.
But the yellow brick building at the end of the block is silent, except for the two-story banner that flaps in the wind, proclaiming Crossroads Theater's 1999 Tony Award for best regional theater. Inside the darkened lobby, dried leaves dance across the black marble floor, among the only things to have entered the theater since it closed its doors for the season at the end of September. The theater's 310 green felt seats remain flipped up, the director's booth strewn with posters from the theater's glory days. 
 With close to $2 million in debt, a halt in financing from public and private sources, internal disarray and an increasingly wary public, the board of trustees announced on Sept. 30 that Crossroads -- which many consider the country's premier African-American theater -- would not open for the 2000-2001 season in an effort to save it from extinction.
As difficult as the decision was, most now say it was inevitable. Indeed, many were surprised it did not happen sooner, since the theater was chronically in debt and notorious for operating in a perpetual crisis mode, particularly in the last 18 months. And yet, because of the theater's award-winning productions and unfailing commitment to original works, Crossroads has garnered not only the highest praise but also a wide network of support that over the years has been willing to extend resources and patience as the theater has tried to right itself again and again.
With no staff and an entirely new board, Crossroads hopes to ride a wave of sentimental outpouring and community goodwill to resurrect itself in time to put on another season next fall. At issue is $835,000 in state grants that are still being withheld, and a usually charitable community that is taking a wait-and-see attitude before opening its pockets to a theater that was once the envy of all other arts organizations for the lavish financial support it received.
"It was always a struggle," said Ricardo Khan, the co-founder and former artistic director of the Crossroads. "The main problem was working toward a $5 million vision but with only two or three million in cash."
To be sure, many circumstances contributed to Crossroads' downward spiral. Some -- like the growing pains of a successful company, or the inevitable conflict between artistic integrity and the bottom line -- are issues that face many arts organizations. But in this case, the challenges at times shifted from the philosophical to the personal, with egos and divided loyalties often getting in the way of good management and clear decision-making. Other factors, more closely related to the theater's sources of money, raised larger issues about the public's financing of the arts.
While some reasons for the failure of Crossroads to stand on its own are as clear as the voices in a Rogers and Hammerstein musical, others are as murky as the plot of a Pinter play. Indeed, the New Jersey State Council on the Arts more often than not tried to impede a reporter's examination of the theater's troubled history. Nina Stack, the council's communications director, denied repeated requests for grant applications, business plans and even independent audits of the theater.
From its start in 1978, Crossroads was a theater of great ambition, gracing its stage with such stars as Ossie Davis, Ruby Dee, Avery Brooks, Leslie Uggams and Anna Deveare Smith. It introduced works by such award-winning playrights as George C. Wolfe, Rita Dove, Leslie Lee and Ntozake Shange. The goal was always to provide a level of art otherwise unavailable in minority-based theater. Unfortunately, the lofty ambition was not always matched by the resources to back it up.
When Mr. Khan and his co-founder, L. Kenneth Richardson, hit upon the idea for Crossroads soon after graduating from the Mason Gross School of the Arts at Rutgers, the $2 million or $3 million budget that they would eventually work with seemed like a lot of money. Tired of being offered parts as pimps, drug dealers or convicts, the two sat at St. John's Restaurant here one afternoon and scratched out their mission statement on a napkin: "to create a professional environment for artists in black theater to develop and practice their craft" and to "present positive and honest images of who we are as African-Americans."
Under the sponsorship of the George Street Playhouse, Mr. Khan applied for and received a $225,000 federal grant to get started. They found an old sewing factory on Memorial Parkway in a somewhat seedy neighborhood that -- despite a daunting 30-step climb to enter and a Y-beam at center stage that had to be incorporated into every set -- provided a perfect home for the company for the next 13 years.
Within three years, Crossroads was staging original works, and over the years the 132-seat theater would present many world premieres, including Mr. Wolfe's "The Colored Museum," a play Mr. Richardson directed in 1986 that put Crossroads on the national map. With the success of "The Colored Museum," and the urging of the city to find a new home, Mr. Khan and Mr. Richardson began planning a capital campaign to raise funds for a larger theater in New Brunswick's Cultural Center.
But by the time ground was broken at the new Livingston Avenue site in late 1989, Mr. Richardson was already long gone from the company, forced out by the board in a departure that was painful to Mr. Khan and signaling the first pin-prick in the balloon. Mr. Khan and others close to the theater still decline to discuss the personal problems that led to Mr. Richardson's ouster.
Despite this setback, the late 80's was what Mr. Khan now calls "the golden era," when Crossroads introduced plays like Leslie Lee's "Black Eagles," about the Tuskeegee Airmen of World War II, which was later performed for President George Bush at Ford's Theater in Washington, and Mbongeni Ngema's "Sheila's Day," a collaborative effort with black South African and African-American actresses that went on to tour in South Africa.
With $1.5 million from the City of New Brunswick and hundreds of thousands more in corporate and private donations, building the new $4 million theater marched toward completion in 1991. That summer, a call came in from Bill Cosby, who offered to perform a series of benefit shows in October to provide Crossroads with a financial cushion during its first year at the new site. His appearances, sponsored by AT&T -- one show at Crossroads and four at the State Theater -- were a thrilling end to a weeklong celebration that included a parade through downtown, a school holiday and the preparation of a time capsule to be placed in the theater lobby.
But even though Mr. Cosby's performances allowed Crossroads to cut back that season from five plays to four -- thus saving tens of thousands in production costs -- it wasn't long before the theater found itself in financial straits. The new four-story building brought about $200,000 a year in added expenses, including the annual $65,000 rent to be paid to the Cultural Center. A larger company also meant a larger staff, higher salaries and new contractual obligations with the actors' union, which until then had gone easy on the fledgling equity company.
"In the old theater, we had a developmental contract with the union," said Mr. Khan. "They gave us certain concessions. Once we moved to the new theater, we were now the enemy, part of the collective bargaining against the union."
In addition, a recession gripped the state in 1991, and politicians halved the art council's budget. Subsequently, the state financing of all arts organizations supported by the council was trimmed. At Crossroads, the state grant in 1991 went to $302,000 from $604,000, remaining at that level for several years.
Many say the double whammy of unanticipated expenses together with the cut in state financing sent the Crossroads on the downward financial spiral from which it never recovered.
"They lost a huge amount of money from the state, and hadn't yet figured out what they needed to operate in the new space," said Keryl McCord, who would inherit the organization's financial woes when she took over as the theater's managing director in 1997. "It was like they just got a new boat, they haven't even gotten their sea legs yet, and all of a sudden, they've got 'The Perfect Storm.' "
A year after the move, the theater was already about $600,000 in debt; by the mid-1990's, that figure climbed to $1.7 million, close to the level it is today. In 1995, a stabilization campaign -- a combination of fund-raising along with the generosity of banks and vendors willing to excuse some of the debt -- reduced the burden by almost half, to $950,000.
But it was not long before the debt started creeping up again. Ms. McCord, who had previously served with the National Endowment of the Arts and the League of Chicago Theaters, said she was brought in by Mr. Khan in 1997 "to try to turn this huge ship around."
Today, she says she was shocked by what she found -- a $1 million debt, which included an outstanding $200,000 bank loan, $300,000 the theater had drawn down from its cash reserve endowment that had never been paid back, vendors' bills that were more than 90 days past due, as well as other surprisingly lax financial practices.
"In my entire career, I had never worked in a place where I had the ability to sign a check to any amount with no second signature required," said Ms. McCord, who shared that privilege with Mr. Khan.
Peter Harvey, a Morristown lawyer who was president of Crossroads' board of trustees from 1997 to 1999, also questioned many decisions that affected the theater's finances, from the plays that were chosen to how the theater was being marketed.
"It was always an issue of 'Will the financial resources drive the art, or will the art drive the money?' " said Mr. Harvey. "There has to be a balance between artistic merit and audience appeal. We had plays that were very profitable, but there were also years when various plays lost a lot of money. When you're so deeply in debt, you can't afford to have half your shows be losers."
Moreover, he questioned why the box office was not open during performances to sell tickets to future shows, and why audiences were not solicited at the end of a season to renew their subscriptions.
Yet once again, help was on the way. In 1996, Crossroads had been accepted into a grant and loan program called the Working Capital Fund for Minority Cultural Institutions. Financed by the Ford Foundation, which had been a longtime supporter of Crossroads, the Working Capital Fund was designed to create long-term financial stability for troubled minority arts organizations by teaching them how to manage their resources better. Over the next four years, the fund was to provide Crossroads with $220,000 in grants and $696,000 in loans, plus the expertise to develop a business plan to erase the theater's debt in five years.
The new business plan called for Crossroads to reduce its season from five plays to four, re-allocate its staff to focus more on marketing and sales personnel and to develop plays that realistically fit the budget. While the plan was viewed by some as the theater's best chance to get back on its feet, others saw it as a crass business move designed to wrest artistic control from the creative people on the staff. Not surprisingly, it got personal and it got ugly and it got there fast.
"There was a struggle over commitment to the plan," said Mr. Harvey. "It required that you make some very hard choices about the product -- the art -- and personnel. Some people were unable or unwilling to do that because of loss of control, weakening of power and egos."
Mr. Khan said the plan simply "didn't work," citing a lack of "synergy between the board, the production staff and the management staff."
"One of the decisions in the plan was to clean house, to professionalize our administration," he said. "On the one hand, you want to have skilled, trained people. No problem. But on the other side, you had people who loved the theater, who had been with us for 6 or 10 or 13 years, being replaced. Maybe this person didn't quite have the experience, but they worked hard. Many people worried that we were throwing the baby out with the bath water."
Mr. Khan put it this way: "We don't mind a team coming in and changing things, as long as they did a better job than we did. But I never saw that happen."
Yet while internal conflict raged, people outside the theater were optimistic about the business plan as well as the presence of Ms. McCord as business manager. The Geraldine R. Dodge Foundation -- which over the years had provided a total of $1.07 million to support Crossroads' season and its Genesis Festival for new plays -- in 1998 directed its $100,000 grant toward implementation of the business plan.
In a letter to the theater, the Dodge Foundation wrote: "Crossroads Theater is in a new place, and much of this has to do with Keryl McCord, who brings leadership to your team in taking the company back to its bare walls."
In addition, the PNC Bank, which had also been a longtime supporter of Crossroads, saw this as a possible turning point and provided capital and debt relief totaling close to $200,000 to help the theater over its financial hurdles.
"They were struggling," said Preston Pinkett, senior vice president of PNC's development bank. "There was very little in terms of financials, and we realized it would be difficult. But it was important to New Jersey, and we wanted to be of assistance. They had a plan, and we had a lot of faith in them and said, 'We'll do what we can to help.' "
Yet while a fresh flow of money was pledged to the theater, there were more ominous undercurrents offstage. In October 1998, the theater's founder, Mr. Khan, told the board he wanted to begin planning for his succession and requested a year's sabbatical, which would begin in late 1999.
"I was burned out and exhausted by 20 years at this, and I honestly did not know the answers anymore," he said recently. "With the tensions between the old staff and the new staff, I felt the company could find its way better without me in the middle."
Most of the staff members were not told of Mr. Khan's pending departure until January 1999, which predictably set into motion a period of anxiety. "Because we didn't have a plan in place, people started to panic," he said. "Everyone was scrambling for whatever they had. Uncertainty affected everything."
Through the spring of 1999, the discord between the business staff and the production staff grew worse, until it came to a head when the door to the business office was kicked in and the furniture was vandalized. In April, Ms. McCord resigned and took a job as vice president at the African Grove Institute, a research and development organization for black theaters. "I felt there was no way I could be as effective as I wanted to be," Ms. McCord said in a recent interview. "The company has a history of art first and my belief is not that art is second, but that you have to manage your resources. There were tough financial decisions that had to be made, and the company wasn't willing to bite the bullet."
At a board meeting a few weeks after Ms. McCord left, more troubling financial news surfaced: $9,000 in bank overdraft charges had accrued, leading the board's controller to institute a new system requiring two or three signatures, depending on the amount of the check. At the same meeting, the board also challenged Mr. Khan's request for a sabbatical in which he would continue to earn his $75,000 annual salary, and it refused to sign off on the 1999-2000 production season without a budget for the shows.
"I declined to consider the matter before we had the anticipated cost of the shows and a projection of the revenues coming from them," said Mr. Harvey, the board president. "It was paramount to act consistent with the business plan; otherwise, you're approving a season in the dark."
Anticipating trouble, some board members who had not attended several previous meetings were urged to show up, and by the end of the session, one of them, Dale Caldwell, was elected to replace Mr. Harvey as board president, leading Mr. Harvey and two other board members to resign.
But like so many other times in Crossroads' history of high-wire acts, the theater's art grabbed the spotlight, placing its financial woes temporarily in the shadows. Its production of Charles Bevel's "It Ain't Nothin' but the Blues" would be the theater's first play to move to Broadway in April, and on June 6, Crossroads Theater would make history as the first black theater to receive a Tony Award for outstanding regional theater in America.
The theater's staff, of course, hoped that the Tony Award would help attract new donors and new theatergoers. But because of its severe financial problems and personal discord, the theater was unable to capitalize on its achievement. In July, the Dodge Foundation said its usual $100,000 annual grant to Crossroads -- money the company had been counting on to promote the new season -- would not be forthcoming.
"Here we are with this Tony Award, but we have no money to market it," Mr. Khan said. "For the first time, we had no brochure for the upcoming season."
Janet Rodriguez, program officer for the Dodge Foundation, says today that her organization could not, in good faith, continue to pump money into the troubled organization.
"Between the deficit and the business manager leaving and the flurry of resignations from the board, we couldn't get behind the theater," Ms. Rodriguez said.
As Mr. Pinkett of PNC Bank put it, "That was the first kid screaming that the emperor was without clothes." His institution soon followed suit.
"We became less comfortable that things would work out," he said, "so we sought to remove ourselves from the situation and asked to have the loan returned in full."
Despite Crossroads' report of July 1999 to the state arts council that "the new board president is committed to the business plan, and has vowed to work closely with the other board members and the staff to reach all of the theater's financial goals," the debt continued to grow and the business plan developed with the Working Capital Fund, for all intents and purposes, was shelved. In fact, the theater still owes the fund $331,000 of the $696,000 that it borrowed.
Emmett Carson, president of the group that administers the Working Capital Fund, said the instability of Crossroads prevented the business plan from being viable. "They certainly didn't have the success we would have liked to have seen and they would have liked to have seen," Mr. Carson said. "They didn't get as much out of it as others did."
As summer turned to fall, Crossroads struggled to mount a new season, while keeping publicly quiet about Mr. Khan's imminent departure. Trying to cut corners on production expenses and save money, the staff delayed delivery of costumes for the musical "Play On!" so the first six performances had to be canceled. Then, midway into the run, the play's star, Stephanie Mills, refused to go onstage because of a disagreement over stockings. Because the theater could not even afford understudies, the performance was canceled.
In early December, Crossroads called a news conference to make two major announcements. On a hopeful note, it spoke of plans to consolidate some debt with a $500,000 loan from New Millenium Bank of New Brunswick, backed by a $250,000 guarantee from the New Jersey Redevelopment Authority. But it also disclosed publicly what the theater had been grappling with for more than a year: at the end of the month, Mr. Khan was to begin a yearlong sabbatical (during which he would continue to receive his salary, until the theater closed).
The next series of blows came the following spring -- the theater had begun missing payments on the new loan and the anticipated $335,000 from the New Jersey State Council on the Arts became uncertain.
Over the years, the council had given Crossroads close to $5 million. But by July -- after a less than satisfactory response to its request for more information on the theater's 2001 grant application -- the council did not include Crossroads in its $19 million distribution to arts organizations. After a consultant brought in by the secretary of state took one look at the troubles at Crossroads, another $500,000 in emergency state financing that had been approved by Governor Whitman was also withheld.
So while subscribers continued to send in money for the 2000-2001 season, theater personnel tried to piece together a plan that would allow them to continue despite what was now a $1.7 million debt. Finally, on Sept. 30, the board decided to cancel the season, and the entire staff was let go. As an exclamation point, Mr. Caldwell, who had been board president for little more than a year, announced his resignation the same day, citing personal reasons.
"'Everybody was trying everything possible to put on a traditional season," he said, "but to put on another season would have eliminated any credibility the board would have with funders and would have closed down the theater completely."
In recent weeks, the new board, under the leadership of a temporary president, Rhinold Lamar Ponder, has been scrambling once again, this time to meet the arts council's latest deadline for more information, including the 1999 audit. By last week, Mr. Ponder said that "most of the documents requested by the arts council have been submitted."
The board has also reorganized the theater's business structure, formed a search committee for a new artistic director and begun looking at ways of reducing the debt -- which with mounting interest is close to $2 million. In addition to the money owed to Working Capital Fund and New Millenium Bank, Crossroads owes vendors more than $400,000 and the Internal Revenue Service about $200,000 in unpaid taxes.
In the meantime, the theater's longtime sponsors are waiting to see what the state does before lending financial support again.
"Because they're in New Brunswick, we always felt a certain responsibility to help them," said Michael Bzdak, director of corporate contributions for Johnson & Johnson, which over the years has donated hundreds of thousands of dollars to Crossroads. "Right now we just want to get an accurate picture of what their financial situation is. There are a lot of people out there who want to help."
But some believe Crossroads' heavy reliance on government, corporate and foundation grants might have kept the theater from learning to better manage its finances. As Mr. Carson at Working Capital Fund said, "organizations cannot be viable long-term if their primary support is from grant dollars."
Mr. Khan, who these days is looking for work, admits: "If we'd never had grants, we'd be a different company. As we grew, we became accustomed to grants and people saying, 'We want to help.' The new building was built for us not because we were a mediocre company but because we were an excellent company.
"Sure, it would be easier for us to do 'Hello Dolly,' absolutely," he added. "But we wouldn't be serving our audience."

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY & CO.;
A Free Trade Battle, by the Turnpike

BYLINE: By JOHN SULLIVAN

 BODY:
A free trade zone is an area most associated with faraway places like the Panama Canal Zone, or at least an airport shopping concourse. But there have long been free trade zones in New Jersey, and a request for a new one along the turnpike has angered local businesses.
The company behind the application to the State Commerce and Economic Growth Commission, the Rockefeller Group Development Corporation, worked with the state to open the first free trade zone in New Jersey more than 20 years ago. The Rockefeller group is now the administrator (the state actually owns the license) for the Mount Olive Zone, a 684-acre development in Morris County and it wants another one in Cranbury, near Exit 8 on the turnpike. 
 Eugene A. Preston, a vice president with the Rockefeller Group, said the company applied to open an extension of its free trade zone in Cranbury because it was almost out of room for light industrial space and distribution space in the Mount Olive zone. He said Rockefeller planned to purchase 150 acres of a 300-acre parcel owned by the Carter Wallace Corporation. The proposed Free Trade Zone would include the 150-acre Carter Wallace land and the 150 acres developed by Rockefeller.
When Charles Klatskin, a longtime real estate developer and a partner in the Forsgate Industrial Complex, learned that the state had asked the federal government to establish a free trade zone near his own commercial warehouse, he was alarmed.
A free trade zone provides such benefits as decreased or delayed customs fees to companies, making it attractive to importers or to manufacturers that work with foreign-made components.
Worried that the zone could lure away their tenants, Mr. Klatskin and other landlords in the business district started a letter-writing campaign to derail the application, which he called "an artificial competitive advantage" for a newcomer to the area.
"I don't think the state realized what they were doing," Mr. Klatskin said. "It was completely an oversight where they were creating favoritism."
The opposition seems to have taken the State Commerce Commission by surprise. Used to viewing foreign trade zones as a positive if arcane boost to New Jersey's import businesses, the commission had sent the application to the federal Department of Commerce without consulting business owners in Cranbury. Last week, responding to letters from Mr. Klatskin and other landlords as well as from state and local politicians, the Commerce Commission asked the federal government to delay the application until it had the chance to review the opponents' positions. The state has received an extension of its application until early December.
"At the time of the application, we were not aware of this type of concern," said Charles E. Hance, the commission's chief executive. "We would like to figure a way this can be done without causing any damage to local businesses."
Mr. Preston said his company planned to build four structures on the site, which could be used for light manufacturing or shipping. He said the expansion was designed to attract new businesses to the state, and said it would be difficult to take tenants away from other landlords because of the way the trade zone works.
"It is not our intention to take tenants from owners that are down there already," he said.
Mr. Preston said nearby businesses could apply to the federal government as a formality, but mainly to the state, which is actually is the licenseholder of the trade zone, for permission to be included in the plan. He said the neighboring businesses would then have the same benefits as the businesses on the 300-acre parcel. The businesses would have to pay administrative costs, such as the application fee for the free trade zone.
"Any company that can use the free trade zone would be able to apply for a subzone," he said.
Foreign trade zones were established by Congress in the 1930's to help American companies compete with overseas firms. There are several ways companies can benefit from the zones, mainly from decreased customs taxes. For example, if a manufacturer in the zone imports parts to make a finished product, the company pays customs fees based on the finished goods rather than the individual components. These fees are usually considerably lower than the duties on parts. Also, companies do not have to pay customs duties until the imported goods enter the United States market, which means that if the goods are shipped from the zone to another foreign country, the company pays no customs fees at all.
There are 240 free trade zones in the country, five of which are in New Jersey. Typically, the zones are controlled by state or local government; sometimes authorities like the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey apply for them. The zone closest to Cranbury is at the Mercer County Airport in Ewing, and officials there have told the federal Commerce Department they would not oppose the Cranbury application
Both sides of the fight in Cranbury have political support. Opponents of the zone point to letters written to federal commerce officials by local politicians from neighboring towns and by state politicians. Joseph Azzolina, chairman of the Assembly commerce committee and a Republican from Middletown, wrote opposing the zone "unless it is expanded to include existing businesses in the area."
Supporters have their own letters. Representative Rush Holt and Alan Danser, the mayor of Cranbury, both wrote the federal Commerce Department in favor of the project, which Mr. Holt said would help "the retention of existing industries and the attraction of new industries into the Middlesex County area."
Harvey Moskowitz, the Cranbury town planner, said the town was treating the project like any other large business development. The area of town, on the eastern side of Cranbury, is zoned for light industrial use and the project meets requirements for zoning and land development, he said.
The section of Cranbury clustered around Exit 8A of the Turnpike has been a hot development area because it is relatively flat and well drained and offers easy access to New York and Philadelphia. Mr. Moskowitz said most of the available land in the area had already been developed or is owned by development companies. "The 8A area has largely been played out in terms of available land," he said.
Mr. Moskowitz said the question of whether a free trade zone would affect existing businesses was not something the town planners could consider when deciding whether to grant a permit. He said businesses typically opposed any new development if it posed a threat of new competition.
"Every time a new building comes on line, that is a concern of the existing owners," he said. "The question of whether or not it is going to take business away is not something we can consider."

HEADLINE: URBAN TACTICS;
What Do You Call the, Uh . . . ?

BYLINE: By SUSAN HODARA

 BODY:
WHEN Gabe Hakvaag was a graduate student in Boston a decade ago, he supported himself by what he called "nannying," working for a couple with a 6-year-old son. It was, he acknowledged, an unorthodox job for a 25-year-old man, and the arrangement was equally unprecedented for his employers, who never quite knew what to call him.
"When they introduced me," said Mr. Hakvaag, a theater director who lives in Inwood, "they'd say: 'This is Gabe. He helps around.' I'd have to interject, 'No, I'm the nanny.' " 
 As baby boomers settle into middle age, a language problem of the moment is not how to refer to a live-in boyfriend or a gay partner but what to call the person who cares for the children. The options are numerous, each with its own connotations: the teenage baby sitter, the Swedish au pair, the trusted housekeeper, the energetic mother's helper, the buttoned-up governess, and, most recently, the beloved nanny.
Increasingly, the term has come into wider use, especially in New York. A search of the archives of The New York Times revealed a significant increase in occurrences, from 11 in 1980 to 175 in 1999. Classified ads in The Irish Echo, a weekly newspaper that is a major source of information for New Yorkers seeking child care and child care positions, promise "the best nanny in the world," an assortment of "loving, reliable" nannies, "loyal' and "honest" nannies and a host of "wonderful" nannies.
The growing popularity of the term reveals a great deal about changing attitudes toward child care, both the people who use it and those who provide it.
The word goes back to 19th-century England, where Mr. Hakvaag would have been an unlikely candidate for the job. There, a nanny was invariably a woman, the Mary Poppins of our shared consciousness, who lived with the family and cared for the children. The term evokes a gamut of associations, drawn from English royalty and the memorable public television series "Upstairs, Downstairs."
"The word nanny is very English," said Helen Freedman, founder and editor of Big Apple Parent, a New York monthly newspaper. "It connotes a tradition and a level of professionalism that just doesn't exist in this country. There, if your daughter tells you, 'I want to be a nanny,' you'd be pleased. She'd enroll in a nanny school and get specialized training."
But starting in the mid-80's, several social and economic forces came together to make the word increasingly popular -- in some cases, almost required -- among New York families.
"First, more college-educated women were living in the city and working in full-time, professional jobs," said Denyse Kapelus, founder and director of Professional Nannies Institute, established in 1986 and now one of the leading child-care referral agencies in New York. "As they started families, they became very serious about what they called their caregiver, and 'baby sitter' sounded too generic, suggesting that anyone at all could care for their child. 'Nanny' signified a more professional, permanent and trained person."
Perhaps in response there was a surge in the number of nanny schools. A dash of elitism fueled the trend.
"These often high-profile working mothers needed to find a term that didn't sound like they were leaving their children with a custodial caregiver," Ms. Kapelus said. "They wanted a word that reflected a different kind of status.
Robin Kellner, founder of Robin Kellner Agency, a Manhattan agency that was established in 1986 as a nanny placement firm and now offers a broader range of services, added: "The term nanny is a more professional term for child care, whereas the term baby sitter suggests a passive watcher. 'Nanny' implies experience, a legitimate position with salary and benefits, as well a certain level of status. It is an upgrade, a way of legitimizing child care."
Predictably, parents come down firmly on both sides of the language divide.
When Stephanie Palewski, an editor at "60 Minutes," had two young children at home, she and her husband, Will Brumbach, an owner of an Internet company, employed a combination of child care people. But Rebecca Knights was their only full-time person, and the only one they called nanny.
"She was more than a baby sitter," said Ms. Palewski, who lives in the Flatiron district. "She was support staff for both the children and us. She was the mother, father, grandparent and any other relative who couldn't be there. She was a teacher, a shoulder, a chef, a housekeeper, an entertainer. An angel.
"I think the word nanny is a very respectful term reserved for the caregiver who is also a teacher and guide to the wonders of life," Ms. Palewski added. "I wouldn't think of calling any of our other caregivers nanny, only Rebecca."
For others, the word is an elitist term that sticks in the throat. To these parents, the term smacks of class consciousness, regardless of how high their child care expectations are.
"We need child care from breakfast till dinner, and we split the job between two people, neither of whom lives with us," said Richard Lyon, a composer and writer who lives on the Upper East Side with his wife, Valerie, a physician, and their two children.
 BOTH are loving, responsible, generous, intelligent, all the things you want in a nanny," Mr. Lyon said. "But we'd never think of calling them that. It seems somehow anachronistic and pretentious in 21st-century New York. I call them our child care people. Val calls them our baby sitters. We've tried to come up with a useful alternative, but we're stumped.
"When I was a kid," Mr. Lyon added, "my family had someone we called a governess. She was the antithesis of Julie Andrews, whom I still picture when I hear the word nanny. If we ever do hire someone with that perfect face and accent, who sings like a bird and can teach our kids to fly, then maybe I'll relent and call her our nanny."
The choice of terminology can also be a function of economics.
"When I think of a nanny," said Danielle Evans, a research assistant in a direct marketing agency, who lives in Inwood with her husband, Gregory, a freelance musician, and their two children, 4 and 2, "I think of an older, loving woman who would dedicate herself to my children.
"But I also think of someone we could never afford to hire," Ms. Evans added.
For now, she remains in "babysitterdom," using part-time child care to fill the gaps in her and her husband's schedules, the world of nannies still an elusive dream.
Caretakers also have their opinions.
For a year, Julie Pettinelli, an Upper East Sider who works in the human resources department at Time Inc., has agonized about what to call Pat Erwin, the woman who cares for her baby daughter. But for Ms. Erwin, the issue is simple. "I prefer to be called by my name," she said. "My name is Patricia, and I like to be called either Pat or Patty."
And what about the term nanny?
"That's O.K., too," Ms. Erwin said. "It's really not a big deal.'
Mavis Roberts has been working as a nanny in Manhattan and Westchester County for more than 16 years, though she doesn't refer to herself as such.
"I'm a caregiver," she said, "a child care giver." She is uncomfortable describing herself as a nanny.
"Somehow, a nanny is different," she said. "I am black, and to me, a nanny is white, so I wouldn't call myself that."
Meanwhile, the search continues for a more suitable job title.
"The term nanny gained popularity in this country about 15 years ago," said Joy Shelton, chairwoman of the American Council of Nanny Schools, a coalition of 15 accredited schools across the country. "But if we had it to do over again, we might have chosen a different name, one that reflects more what a nanny does here and now.
"We've tried 'in-home child care provider,' " she said. "But it's just too much of a mouthful."
