PUB-DATE: November 26, 2000

SECTION: Section 14; Page 1; Column 2; The City Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 2627 words

HEADLINE: The Unlit Stove

BYLINE: By DAVID KIRBY

 BODY:
NOT long ago, Debra Birnbaum came home to find an unusual letter tucked inside her utility bill. It was from Con Edison, wondering why she wasn't using any gas in her West Village apartment.
"They asked if anything was wrong," said Ms. Birnbaum, a senior editor at George magazine. "And they offered to send over a technician to check on the problem." 
 But there was no problem. Her gas worked perfectly.
"It was pretty humiliating," Ms. Birnbaum acknowledged. "Because the only thing I use my stove for is making popcorn. What was I going to do, call and say: 'Hi. I'm a single girl, and I don't cook?' "
Ms. Birnbaum is hardly alone. The home-cooked meal in New York houses and apartments is decidedly on the wane. This is not simply because New Yorkers eat out so much, but because so many are so reluctant to cook and, when they eat in, they are increasingly able to select from an array of epicurean options available for takeout or delivery to anyone with a phone, fax or modem.
There are no exact figures on how many, or how few, New Yorkers cook, and how much prepared food they consume. But there are telling indicators: On a good night a popular place like Tuscan Square in Rockefeller Center sells more than $400 in takeout dinners, and Tavern on the Green sells some $2,200 worth.
Those tabs may only get larger. In a feverish economy, more New Yorkers are working longer hours than ever, and their numbers include a growing population of mothers who used to stay home and tend the stove. Combine that with the soaring incomes, minuscule kitchens and a passion for all things quick and convenient that are distinctively New York, and it's easy to see why so many residents, especially in Manhattan, are addicted to home meal replacements, or H.M.R.'s, as they're called in the food service industry.
Seth Bookey, an Upper East Sider who edits a trade publication called Vision Monday, tells of the time an English friend came to visit and was shocked by the number of people lined up for takeout late at night.
"She said, 'Do all New Yorkers do this every night?' " Mr. Bookey remembered. "And I said: 'Of course not. Half the time we have food delivered.' "
Of course, some New Yorkers are passionate cooks. But while a certain segment of the population can be found fingering the whisks at Williams-Sonoma, prowling the produce aisles of Fairway, brushing up on Indonesian cooking and watching, enraptured, as the Iron Chef hacks away on the Food Network, a countertrend is at work.
"Here, cooking is a spectator sport," said Carol A. Butler, a psychotherapist whose downtown clientele includes many people more likely to stop at the gym than the supermarket after work. "It's something you watch and think you might someday try."
 
A No-Brainer: The Stove or the Phone
If New Yorkers are addicts, the city is the great enabler. Sure, you could always get Chinese and diner food to go, but in the last five years the options have become staggering, much like the explosion in dot-com start-ups, a related phenomenon. Pizzaman still delivers, but so does the chic French bistro on the corner. Nearly every ethnic restaurant will dispatch fresh, hot food to your door in minutes, and a surprisingly large number of upscale places, like the "21" Club, offer food to go.
The phenomenon has transformed the sights, sounds and smells of the city. Every evening at dinner time, the streets are flooded with delivery men on bicycles, heavy chains clanking in the night as the drivers lock their bikes (illegally) to trees. Halls and elevators of apartment buildings once suffused with homey aromas flowing from warm ovens become redolent with the pungent scent of kung-pao chicken and pizza topped with goat cheese.
"There's more food delivery options in New York than anywhere," said Paul Westra, a former analyst at Salomon Brothers, and now vice president of business development at food.com, a Web site that offers online ordering from 300 New York restaurants. "In New York, time is the most valuable component of a cooked-meal experience. And dining out means investing the whole evening, not just the money. So, many New Yorkers choose delivery.
"When it comes to ordering out," he added, "New York is 10 years ahead of the nation."
The proof is in the proverbial pudding, packaged, preferably, in a reheatable polyurethane container. New York may be the "biggest restaurant market on the planet," according to the 2000 Zagat Restaurant Survey, but it isn't the place people dine out the most. That honor belongs to Houston, where respondents ate out 4.9 times weekly, excluding breakfast, compared with just 3.2 meals a week for New Yorkers, who lagged behind the national average of 3.8.
In the realm of takeout, however, nobody can hold a warming bag to New Yorkers. Those responding to the Zagat survey ate restaurant food at home 3.8 times weekly, more often than in any of 32 cities studied and well above the national average. And New Yorkers cook less than anyone. Just 4.8 homemade meals weekly, compared with 5.6 nationwide and 6.3 in the biggest home-cooking town of all, Baltimore.
One explanation for this trend is the rise of the cyberclass, or, as Sam Sifton, a senior editor at Talk magazine, calls them, the "yetties" (young entrepreneurial technocrats).
"I spent a year among them, documenting their habits, including their food," said Mr. Sifton, whose book, "A Field Guide to the Yettie: America's Young, Entrepreneurial Technocrats," will be published this month by Talk-Miramax Books. "The yuppies as we understood them disappeared after the '87 stock market crash. But their ethos lived on, partly in the notion of nonstop corporate-card delivery and takeout."
In the lofts of Silicon Alley, still humming despite a slowing of the city's dot-com economy, "It's all about takeout," Mr. Sifton said.
"For the yettie, life is work and work is life," he added. "They're at the office 18 hours a day. At some point, the sushi man is going to come."
Sushi, sleek, elegant and low-cal, is mother's milk to higher-echelon techies. Those further down the electronic food chain, like programmers and coders, prefer copious quantities of Chinese takeout. "They are much more the General Tso's chicken type," Mr. Sifton said.
"I doubt many of them are interested in cooking," he continued. "But if Nasdaq rebounds, and there's a second wave of I.P.O.'s and instant gagillionaires, they're going to buy bigger places. And for the first time, some 30-year-old will be staring down the barrel of a Viking stove. And what do you do at that point? Well, you look at it for six months, and then maybe you boil an egg."
 
Guilt and the Mothers Of Convention
For all the "I can order anything any time" bravado, a sense of guilt is sometimes at work, a sneaking suspicion that, as plastic and foil containers pile up in the garbage, maybe all this takeout is taking its toll on home and hearth, not to mention pocketbook and waistline.
This sentiment is particularly prevalent among mothers, who sometimes, but not always, lament the fact that the fridge is stocked with little more than packets of ketchup and plum sauce. Is it possible, they wonder, not totally tongue in cheek, that they are producing a generation of children who will refuse to eat anything that does not come in a small white carton with an aluminum handle?
There is also a fear that delivered dinners feed isolationism in families, especially when each member orders an individual item, then retreats into a bedroom. Some parents put their foot down.
"We used to order from different restaurants," said one busy public relations executive. "But now I insist on one check." This woman, who lives on the Upper West Side, never cooks and says she knows no one who does.
But, she added: "At least I have friends in fast food. My mother had a close relationship with the butcher. I have a close relationship with the sushi place. I call to order, give them my phone number, and they say, 'Oh, it's the yellowtail scallion inside-out lady.' "
Delivered dinners can also offer an overlooked advantage. Patrons with enough charm, not to mention chutzpah, can often coax a delivery person into picking up a few extra items along the way.
Lisa Albanese, 29, a marketing executive for a clothing company, is a master of this strategy. Ms. Albanese and her husband, Augustin Jakovac, a producer at VH1, live in a "cozy" exposed-brick one-bedroom in an Upper West Side brownstone. The kitchen is little more than a counter, and they order out a lot.
"The restaurant guys always recognize her and say: 'Hey, Lisa! What's up?' " Mr. Jakovac said. "And she orders the bagels, or whatever, and then adds: 'I'm really kind of desperate. We don't have any toilet paper, and I know that two doors down from you there's a discount store. Do you think you could pick up a four-pack of Charmin for me and put it on the bill?' And they do."
They also bring newspapers, magazines and Tylenol.
"I can't believe she has the gall to even ask," Mr. Jakovac said. "But she asks very nicely: 'Do you think you could, perhaps, maybe? If I gave you a good tip?"' Ms. Albanese became so adept at this approach she even persuaded the pizza place near her old apartment to send up single slices.
 
How to Remove All Temptation to Cook
In some New York households, not only the home-cooked meal has disappeared. The appliance used to prepare the food has disappeared along with it. Isaac Kolhberg, until recently an administrator at New York University Medical Center, and his companion, Na'an Nadar, an interior designer, were so certain they would never cook in their Washington Square Village apartment, they had the oven and range removed. A woman comes once a week to stock the freezer with entrees that can be warmed in the microwave oven.
"I know we should probably be doing some cooking ourselves," Mr. Nadar said. "But we honestly can't figure out why."
Carol Zack, a calligrapher who lives on West 74th Street, went an entire year without a working oven. After it broke for the third time, she never bothered to have it fixed. When her daughter teased her about the situation, Ms. Zack pointed out that the family did own a microwave, although she admitted she had no idea how to use it.
She, too, has developed coping strategies. She adores the thin-crust pizza at a local parlor, but the pizzeria delivers only as far south as 75th Street. So, like Elaine on "Seinfeld," Mrs. Zack devised an ingenious solution.
"I called and ordered and gave them an address on 75th Street," she said. "Then I walked one block north, and when the man on a bicycle arrived, I paid him and took the pizza home." A doorman witnessing the transaction was so moved by her plight, he gave her the telephone number of the lobby, for use with future pies.
Bill Diggins and Jennifer Bandier Diggins, an Upper East Side couple who manage pop bands like TLC, found themselves in a situation so typically New York, it could have inspired an entire episode of "Seinfeld." Ms. Bandier Diggins orders in everything, even coffee. But one day, overcome with an odd feeling of boredom and curiosity, she decided to bake cookies.
"I became obsessed," she said, "and called the store and had $300 worth of ingredients sent over, put the oven on, and began mixing." A half-hour later, the apartment was filled with toxic fumes. It turned out the housekeeper had converted the oven into a closet for storing Tupperware, which melted into a single blob. "She never imagined it would be used for anything like baking," Ms. Bandier Diggins said.
 
Beyond Pizza and Chinese: Four-Star Delivery
The cognoscenti have always known how to have upscale cuisine delivered to their doorstep. Places like "21" have been doing it for decades. In "Rear Window," Grace Kelly ordered a four-star dinner for a wheelchair-bound Jimmy Stewart, complete with a waiter in tails. But the range of haute cuisine that can routinely be delivered grows more impressive by the week. Options, some for takeout but many for delivery, include Swedish gravlax from Aquavit, veal Romana from Campagnola and chicken Kiev from the Russian Tea Room, among countless choices.
New Yorkers who live near Peccavi, an Italian Mediterranean restaurant on East 26th Street, can enjoy dinner delivered the Old World way, arranged on fine bone china on big trays borne by uniformed waiters dodging traffic. (People living further away get a busboy with a beribboned, multicolored paper bag.)
"We didn't plan to do any delivery, but we started getting many calls for it," said Kana Brinckerhoff, an owner of the five-month-old restaurant. "And not just the occasional fried calamari. People want rib steak and pomegranate-glazed quail."
Because orders to be delivered can easily come to $80 or $90, she added an extra touch.
"I felt like we were cheating people out of a fine dining experience," Ms. Brinckerhoff said, "so we put everything in reusable Tupperware-like containers and a nice bag. We're trying to justify, saying: 'O.K., here's your $90 takeout. Isn't that a cute bag?' "
Another option is e-menus. These include sites like nydelivery.com, gotham2go.com and food.com, which connect mostly to pizza and fast-food places, as well as more specialized sites like tossed.com, which calls itself "the world's first online salad bar," and redwinewithfish.com, "preparing and delivering civilized dinners to civilized New Yorkers." Health fanatics may patronize a site called zonetoyourhome.com, which will send three meals daily, based on the Zone diet, for $24.95.
The almost-personal chef, once the preserve of the superrich, has become an increasingly familiar presence even in middle-class families. The many choices include Joan's Personal Chef Service, which will prepare a week's worth of dinners, including snacks, for a family of four, for $50 a dinner, and the Natural, which will deliver four complete meals with an emphasis on natural foods for $100. Thirty-one New York businesses are listed on the Web site of the United States Personal Chef Association.
 
Takeout Trend: Threat or Menace?
Is the decline of the home-cooked dinner yet another indication that civilization is teetering on the brink, or is it simply a not-so-sinister sign of the times?
Whatever the answer, it is clearly no deterrent when it comes to dinner parties.
"New Yorkers can never agree on anything," said Ms. Birnbaum, explaining why she gave a touch-tone dinner party not long ago. "Everyone has their own little rules. This one's vegetarian. That one won't eat Chinese. This one is gluten-intolerant." And so, having prepared her guests for an evening of takeout, she simply tossed some menus on the table alongside her credit card and announced: "Dinner is served. Pick one."
And while the image of a family devouring a meal prepared by loving hands is appealing, it is debatable whether intangible emotional benefits have been lost.
"I hear stories about family meals that were disastrous," said Ms. Butler, the psychotherapist. "Even as adults, plenty of folks dread going home for the family dinner experience. It wasn't a universally warm and fuzzy experience."
Others disagree.
"Nothing fixes somebody's bad day more than having their favorite dish made by someone they care about," said Sara Moulton, an executive chef at Gourmet magazine and host of the Food Network's "Cooking Live."
"It's like mom's meatballs. In New York, everyone's rushing around, talking into cell phones, barking at each other. Home cooking, though, is much more conducive to sitting down, talking.
"It may sound corny," Ms. Moulton added, "but there's something to sharing."

HEADLINE: BRIEFING: ECONOMY;
CONSTRUCTION OUTLOOK

BYLINE: By Karen Demasters

 BODY:
Construction in New Jersey should continue to boom during the next two years, according to an annual projection by the Alliance for Action released last week. The alliance predicts $20 billion in new construction during 2001 and 2002. This is a 24 percent increase over the prediction last year for the years 2000 and 2001 and the largest dollar amount predicted since the alliance began making its annual forecasts 16 years ago. The numbers are based on the plans of 25 public and private organizations involved in construction. Projected school construction of $3.1 billion over the next two years represented the largest single area. The State Transportation Trust Fund projects $975 million in construction spending over the two years. For the private sector, the New Jersey Chapter of the National Association of Industrial and Office Properties estimated $2.6 billion in new construction for the two years and New Jersey utility companies said $2.1 billion in construction work would be done. Last year the alliance predicted $16.7 billion in new construction in 2000 and 2001. Karen DeMasters

HEADLINE: BENEFITS

 BODY:

These Truths Are Portable
WEDNESDAY -- A dinner at the Waldorf-Astoria will raise money to display a 1776 copy of the Declaration of Independence at libraries and public schools across the country. The event is organized by People for the American Way, the civic, policy and lobbying group. Nickolas Davatzes, the president and chief executive of the A&E Television Networks, will be honored. Drinks at 6:30 will be followed by dinner at 7:30. Tickets, $600, from (212) 245-6570. 

For 'Greener' Buildings
WEDNESDAY -- A dinner at the Racquet and Tennis Club, 370 Park Avenue (52nd Street), will honor Ralph Gibson, a photographer, for his work on the New York urban environment. The evening, which begins with drinks and a silent auction at 7, will benefit the Design Trust for Public Space, which gives fellowships to architects, landscape artists and urban planners to encourage environmentally sound building projects and the development of playgrounds and other public open space. Bruce Ferguson, the dean of the Columbia University School of the Arts, will speak. Tickets, $250, from (212) 695-2432, ext. 10.
 
Angel Ball
THURSDAY -- Marc Anthony, Natalie Cole, Sister Sledge and 'N Sync will be among those performing at a dinner at the New York Marriott Marquis for the G&P Foundation for Cancer Research. The event will also honor Queen Noor of Jordan, Mikhail Gorbachev and Paul McCartney, each of whom has lost a spouse to cancer. The black-tie evening begins with drinks at 6:30; dinner and the show follow at 7:30. Tickets, $1,250, from (212) 614-0400.
 
A Century of Aid
THURSDAY -- The Xavier Society for the Blind, which celebrated its 100th anniversary this year, will raise money for its programs, including its publication, The Catholic Review, at a cocktail party at the Southgate Tower Suite Hotel, 371 Seventh Avenue (31st Street). The organization provides people who are blind or have limited vision with spiritual and inspirational reading materials and operates a free lending library of works in large print and Braille and on audiocassettes. The evening begins at 6. Tickets, $100, from (212) 473-7800.
 
Affordable New York
THURSDAY -- Edward I. Koch will be among those honored for their efforts in support of New York neighborhoods at a dinner at Cipriani 42nd Street, 110 East 42nd Street. The black-tie event will raise money for programs of the Phipps Community Development Corporation, which builds affordable housing in New York and provides social services to the residents. Jane Hanson, an anchor on WNBC-TV, will be the evening's host. Drinks at 6:30, and dinner at 7:30. Tickets, $600, from (212) 675-9474.
 
For Peace and Human Rights
THURSDAY -- The New Israel Fund will raise money for its social programs in Israel, which include efforts on behalf of civil and human rights, women, and Jewish-Arab equality and coexistence. It will hold a cocktail party, buffet dinner and dance at Irving Plaza, 17 Irving Place (East 15th Street). The evening begins at 8 with cocktails. Tickets, $125, from (212) 750-2333, or $150 at the door.
 
Reaching for the Sky
THURSDAY -- A cocktail party at the Four Seasons will raise money for Trees New York, an organization that teaches children and adults to plant and care for trees on the streets of the city. Among those to be honored are Representative Carolyn B. Maloney, a New York Democrat. The event begins at 5:30 p.m. Tickets, $125, from (212) 227-1887.
 
Fulbright Dinner
THURSDAY -- Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers will speak at a dinner at the Plaza to raise money for the Institute of International Education, which administers the Fulbright scholarship program. Mr. Summers will be honored for his work on global economic policy, and Douglas Daft, the chairman and chief executive of the Coca-Cola Company, will be honored for his company's work in in the countries where it operates. The black-tie evening begins at 6:30 with cocktails; dinner at 7:30. Tickets, $1,000, from (212) 685-1095.
 
Helping Build Schools
THURSDAY -- A cocktail reception at the Hudson Hotel, 356 East 58th Street, will raise money for the School House Foundation, which helps arrange financing for the construction of public schools in New York. The event begins at 6:30 p.m. Tickets, $2,500, from (212) 981-5281.
 
AIDS Concert
FRIDAY -- The B-52's, Cyndi Lauper, Rufus Wainwright and the Girls Choir of Harlem will be among the performers at a concert beginning at 8 p.m. at Carnegie Hall to raise money for the social service and education programs of Gay Men's Health Crisis, the AIDS service organization. Sandra Bernhard will be the host, and Phil Donahue will be among those honored. A reception with the honorees and the artists will be held at Brasserie 8 1/2, 9 West 57th Street, just after the concert. Tickets, $250, from (212) 367-1531, or $35 to $75 for the concert alone, from (212) 247-7800.
 
Dining to Gershwin

HEADLINE: THE BUSINESS WORLD;
Capitalism, One Student At a Time, In Vietnam

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS

DATELINE: HO CHI MINH CITY, Vietnam

 BODY:
OF all the radical free-market concepts that American teachers are pounding into their students at a small Harvard-run economics institute here, the most fundamental may be elbow grease.
"I hear the complaint, 'You make them work too hard; you don't give them time to contemplate,' " said Brian Quinn, until recently the institute's director, as he headed down a busy two-lane highway dotted with the small new factories that form the first ripple of Vietnam's slowly emerging market economy. 
 One Vietnamese economist, said Lora L. Sabin, the institute's academic director, told her quite seriously, "Vietnamese officials prefer to work in a low-intensity environment."
Changing that attitude is one of the goals of the Fulbright Economics Teaching Program, founded in 1994 and administered by the Center for Business and Government at Harvard's John F. Kennedy School of Government. At a cool and sun-dappled complex in the city center, the institute is working to prepare middle-level mangers in provincial governments and state and private enterprises to do battle in the fast-moving market economy that Vietnam must master if it is to compete in the world.
"Things now move so quickly that it is impossible for any group of bureaucrats to make the right decisions even half the time," Mr. Quinn said.
The institute's efforts have become more important as pressure on Vietnam has intensified to loosen its centrally planned economy. President Clinton repeatedly emphasized the themes of speedier and more open economic development in Vietnam during his three-day visit to the country that ended last Sunday. Many American corporations, like Boeing, Caterpillar, Corning, Motorola and Oracle, are eager to start or expand their presence here, but the business climate remains difficult.
IN its one-year programs, the institute drills an average of 200 or more students in basic economic concepts and analytic skills, offering courses on subjects like applied statistics, managerial accounting, principles of management and appraisal of development expenditures.
"I found something of a shock when I came here," said Hung Quan, 35, a student who is an official at the regulatory agency for Vietnam's new stock exchange. "When we learned about central planning we learned how to manage and control the economy following the plan. In the new economy we are learning here how to use scarce resources efficiently. It is a very, very different point of view. There is some difficulty in adapting our learning from our old way of life. We are learning how to change."
People of his generation in Vietnam -- the people who make the economy run on a day-to-day level -- were trained in the principles of a command economy where the ethos was a rote fulfillment of government plans and goals.
"Now I think we will see changes," said another student, Bich Ha, an import-export manager for a private company. "People realize that if they don't change, if they don't hurry up, they will be left behind."
The institute, sponsored by the United States Information Agency with the cooperation of the Vietnamese government, anticipates the direction of Vietnamese economic development by shifting the focus from the central government to the provincial and local level.
"Economic reform is really going to be a process of decentralization of decision-making authority," Mr. Quinn said. "More and more decisions are going to be devolved to the provincial level, and as this happens it is important for these people to have the ability to make rational decisions.
"They will be designing programs, designing tax structures, designing government policy. It's unfair to require them to make policy and not give them the skills they need to do that."
Heading south through the rice fields of the northern Mekong Delta, where small factories have expanded the outskirts of Vietnam's economic hub, Ho Chi Minh City, Mr. Quinn stopped at the Long An Textile Company, a struggling state-owned enterprise that produces synthetic fabrics.
A recent graduate, Le Van Bich, was given the job of deputy director in charge of business development here in the hope that his new expertise would help make the factory more competitive.
"This company was established under central planning and we are still trying to find a way into the market economy," said Mr. Bich, who is in his mid-40's. "We make a lot of mistakes, for example not taking into account the impact of exchange rates on the costs of equipment."
Some of the old guard resist his innovations of style, he said. "But mostly we don't have a problem with employees because they realize there is no other way to go," he added. "If you don't go this way you can't last."
A little farther down the highway, Mr. Quinn stopped to talk with another graduate, Nguyen Thi Thu, 43, vice chairwoman for the management of industrial zones in Long An province.
"Before, we trained with a Soviet model," she said. "Most of the officials here understand the words and basic ideas of the new system so they can say the right things, but they don't really understand them. So when I explain things, they listen to me."
Curiously, the concept of independent thinking seems to be catching on precisely because it is the new policy of the central government -- the new central plan.
"The government and the party have a new line now and this line is markets," she said. "So if you can use market reasoning people agree with you. They don't argue against you because they know it is the line."
She added: "We have a new way of working since I came back from the course that everyone has to adapt to. It is much faster and more rigorous. Before, we worked much more slowly."
One of the most useful skills she learned at the institute, she said, was project appraisal -- the analysis of economic and financial risk and the balancing of gains and losses involved in a proposed project.
"In Vietnam today, analysis of investments is usually more emotional than rational," Mr. Quinn said. " 'We need a steel plant because every industrialized country has a steel plant.' We want them to be able rationally to try to understand whether it is more urgent, with their scarce resources, to build a bridge, a school or a new office building. What's the return on investment, what are the risks, how do you identify the benefits and losses in a rational way?"
At the institute, each student is asked to bring a real example from his or her home province for analysis by the class. Should the province build one big bridge or two small ones? What is the projected growth of traffic flow?
"The old way of thinking was to say, 'Oh, we think it will grow 10 percent,' but it's very hard to establish demand," said Ms. Sabin, who teaches the project appraisal course. "We want to expose them to the way we think about problems. The real world is filled with uncertainty, and that's the world they'll be working in when they go back to their own provinces."
STUDENTS are also taught to think not only about the enterprise and its immediate profits but also about the consumer, the ultimate arbiter in a market economy.
"Under the old system, the growth of output of shoes was set by the government at a certain level," Mr. Quinn said. "But it turned out that Vietnam was an attractive place to produce shoes and demand grew, and the government plan ignored that."
One of the most pervasive problems that foreign investors have found in Vietnam is an us-versus-them mentality. The concept of a win-win outcome is rare here.
"Their usual assumption is, if you win, I lose," Ms. Sabin said, "and the only way to make sure I win is for you to lose. It's like a war strategy.
"But of course, if you keep doing this in business, your partners are not going to come back."
Mr. Quinn agreed, "That's one of the things they have to learn about trade: that everyone can win."

HEADLINE: THE AGE OF DISSONANCE;
Neurosis in the City: It Goes Way Back

BYLINE: By Bob Morris

 BODY:
DOES it mean you're on the ball or neurotic when you're the first summer renter to resurface in East Hampton?
Two weeks ago, before the weather had turned cold and even before holiday season anxieties had surfaced, my friend Steven and I revisited the place, which we hadn't seen since late August. Rental prices had jumped, according to our Sotheby's broker, and many properties had been sold during the economic blush of fall. 
 Still, with summer a mere seven or eight months away, it was time to begin the hunt. With the focus of sea gulls devouring delicacies from the trash, we plowed through several homes, dismissing them as too this or too that and turning up nothing that suited our Platonic Hamptonian ideal.
As I drove home, my agitation was exacerbated by traffic on the Southern State Parkway, almost as bad as traffic in August. The weird thing was that something about all this -- the traffic's making me late for the theater, the daunting cost of the distant summer and the challenge of finding a sufficient number of attractive, low-key friends able to afford such steep rent -- felt so familiar, so right.
I guess I'm just your average neurotic with Newyorkitis.
Newyorkitis is not a term of my own invention. It was coined in 1901, when one Dr. James Girdner published a book by the same name. According to Dr. Girdner, the symptoms of Newyorkitis include "haste, rudeness, restlessness, arrogance, contemptuousness, excitability, anxiety, pursuit of novelty and of grandeur, pretensions of omniscience, and therefore prescience, which of course undermines any pleasure taken in novelty."
Dr. Girdner wrote this book at a time when neurasthenia was big -- a disorder in mental functioning that, according to Freud, includes lassitude, irritability, worry and hypochondria. These days, neurasthenia and Newyorkitis may be arcane terms, but that doesn't mean neurosis doesn't thrive in the city that never sleeps, at least not without Ambien, melatonin or a discussion about which to take during upgraded air travel. "When you're neurotic in New York, it means you have a link to being an intellectual," said Sondra Farganis, a sociology professor at the New School for Social Research. "And it also often means there's a charm and depth to your feelings."
Indeed, this would help explain the appeal of Woody Allen, Jerry Seinfeld, Candace Bushnell and the Broadway comedy hit "The Allergist's Wife," not only to New Yorkers but to the rest of the country, too, including telephone solicitors, who view us as a kind of demographic viper pit.
Still, it takes a certain fortitude to be charmed by life at such a heightened pitch, where every move, outfit and accessory is stridently scrutinized. A friend who teaches kindergarten in TriBeCa, for instance, has to face germ-obsessed parents who want no child's hand to touch the floor, and who would also like a video camera installed in the bathroom to see that everyone washes properly.
She becomes neurotic hearing her students talk about their country houses and lavish vacations. I can relate. "Are you looking to rent or buy?" is all someone had to ask about my Hamptons house hunt at a cocktail party the other night and I collapsed into myself. Money! New York magazine's recent cover story about how much New Yorkers make suggested that if we were all more open about money, it would actually make us nicer.
I'm not sure knowing that Tommy Hilfiger made $26.9 million last year makes me any nicer. But it certainly makes me more knowing, for better or worse.
Are we all so knowing as a defense against all the things we can't know -- like if the Poland Springs water we just drank was tainted, or if that mosquito bite will infect us with the West Nile virus or if we'll ever get a cab without having to fight for it? I don't know. But in a quest for amusement as much as for an answer, I attended a recent New School seminar called "Neuroses and the Culture of the City."
It was packed. I learned a lot, too: that the moderator, first of all, used to be a heroin addict, and that the term "ambient fear" describes the low-level hum of anxiety we live with daily.
I also learned that long before Walt Whitman called New York a "passionate and mettlesome city," Horace and Juvenal deplored the clamor of ancient Rome; and that when the automobile first appeared in New York, it was considered dangerous because it was so much quieter than clattering carriages. Most surprising, I learned that two big polls taken in 1954 and 1969 showed that New Yorkers weren't more neurotic than other Americans, and that they actually had lower suicide rates and fewer psychiatric problems.
"Neurosis involves issues of style, not substance," declared Hillel Schwartz, a panelist, before a rapt crowd. "It's a kind of escapism for the well-off individualist."
Being neurotic, after all, at least in the way we think of it around here, is really a privilege, an affliction that lacks any real political or medical urgency. In fact, the neurosis seminar at the New School was nothing if not a crowd pleaser.
The Thanksgiving Day parade a week later was another matter. At a viewing party on a friend's balcony on Central Park West, we were all watching the parade in our own particular way. I, for instance, was pleased to see that none of the marching bands freezing their lips off were local, and wondered if the Mickey or Barney or Rugrats balloon floating past us in the wind at eye level might do the kind of damage that would result in more lawsuits by injured onlookers.
Nearby, two guests surveyed a shivering Texan flag team. "They should have color-coordinated their ski hats or at least done them in all neutral colors," said one. "I hate parades," said another, who then watched intently until the whole thing was over.
I should probably learn to hate parades, too, and the Hamptons as well.
But first, I've got to get a summer rental.

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Initial Offerings: Once Hot, but Now Hot Potatoes

BYLINE: By PATRICK McGEEHAN

 BODY:
IF your holiday wish list includes shares of the hottest new stock, you are likely to be disappointed. With the way things are going in the stock market, you might find more value in a Pets.com sock puppet or a William Shatner CD.
Several companies have had such dismal debuts that there may not be many more offerings this year -- hot, cold or lukewarm. 
 The heady days of February and early March, when the typical initial public offering doubled in price in its first day of trading, are a warm and increasingly fuzzy memory. Back then, I.P.O. seemed to stand for immediate profit opportunity. Now, investors appear to have a different interpretation: it's probably overpriced.
All four of last week's offerings stumbled out of the gate, losing as much as 18 percent in a day. Rigel Pharmaceuticals, a member of the fast-cooling biotechnology sector, reduced its rescheduled offering in size and price, then postponed it. Since Oct. 1, some 58 offerings have been withdrawn or postponed and 49 have been sold, said Richard Peterson, an analyst at Thomson Financial/ Securities Data in Newark.
Those are troubling results for the one set of products that Wall Street tries to underprice. To keep companies coming to market and investors eager to buy their stocks, investment bankers price initial offerings so that they will rise, at least a little. A quick gain of 10 percent to 15 percent was considered ideal before the Internet stock craze bubbled up and shares routinely doubled or tripled on Day One.
Prospects for new stocks have not looked this bleak since the fall of 1998, when the market skidded to a halt in the wake of the Russian financial crisis. Back then, some very profitable companies, including the Goldman Sachs Group, shelved their initial offerings while they waited for the market to recover. By late November, investors had resumed gobbling up new stocks, and that stall turned out to be a hiccup.
This one may require a Heimlich. "Normally at this time of year we should be getting rocked," said Irv DeGraw, a finance professor at Eckerd College in St. Petersburg, Fla., who studies the market for new stocks. "It's usually a pretty hectic market from Thanksgiving until mid-December."
Last year, there were about 45 initial offerings after Thanksgiving, Mr. Peterson said. This year, there may not be half that many, he said.
What went wrong? Mr. DeGraw, also research director at WorldFinanceNet.com, which tracks stock offerings, said that the initial-offering market had been sagging for several weeks but that its weakness was masked by enduring demand for biotechnology, optical networking and alternative energy stocks. "Unfortunately, all three of those pillars collapsed," Mr. DeGraw said.
Trapped in the rubble was Alliance Fiber Optic Products, a company that Merrill Lynch took public on Tuesday, after reducing the number of shares for sale by 28 percent. The shares, offered at $11, ended their first day down 87.5 cents, or 8 percent, and dropped on their second to $8.50.
That was not even the worst performance by a Merrill-led initial offering last week. Imagic TV fell from its $11 offering price to $6.75, or 39 percent, in its first two days of trading.
Surely, some of those who rushed to sell at a loss had been recipients of "friends and family" stock, shares doled out to people associated with executives or underwriters of the company being offered. Until the Nasdaq index crashed through the atmosphere last spring, insiders' shares in a technology company were equated with manna from heaven.
Now that investors have little appetite for new stocks, people with connections to the next hot offering may be left wondering: With friends and family like these, who needs enemies?

HEADLINE: ECONOMIC VIEW;
Argentina And Canada: Two Sides of Uncertainty

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA

 BODY:
'STABILITY might not be everything," Karl Schiller, the former West German economics minister, once remarked, "but without stability, everything is nothing."
The global landscape is littered with the debris of strong national economies that were undermined by the uncertainty of political crises, fiscal emergencies and other catalysts of instability. Right now, the currencies of two countries at opposite ends of the Americas -- Canada and Argentina -- show both the strength of certainty and the withering danger of uncertainty. 
 The exchange rate for the Canadian dollar is set by the market. Argentina's peso has been pegged one-to-one with the United States dollar since 1991. Although Canada's economy is far more developed than Argentina's, their populations are similar in size. Commodities are important to both economies, and both depend heavily on large, competitive neighbors.
When it comes to the currencies, however, Argentina's peso is strong, while its economy is weak. Canada's dollar is weak, but its economy is consistently strong.
The reasons for this seeming contradiction are complex, but they come down to stability, and the confidence that supports it.
Some economists blame the dollar peg for Argentina's recent problems, in part because a strong American dollar translates into a strong peso, which hurts Argentine exports. But the recent loss of investor confidence may be far more damaging, said Steve H. Hanke, a professor of applied economics at Johns Hopkins University. His defense of the dollar peg is not surprising since he helped Argentina adopt it. "The problems in Argentina have nothing to do with the currency board," which controls the dollar peg, he said. Argentina really needs to bolster investor confidence, he said.
Soon after taking over last December, the coalition government led by Fernando de la Rua tried to climb out of a recession set off by Brazil's currency devaluation in early 1999. The plan was to reduce the budget deficit by raising taxes. It backfired, raising fears of default or devaluation.
A few weeks ago, the government introduced austerity measures that appeared to satisfy some foreign investors and the International Monetary Fund. But the belt-tightening set off a huge general strike by three major trade unions -- a situation that can only further dampen investor confidence.
Guillermo A. Calvo, director of the Center for International Economics at the University of Maryland, and other economists worry that just forcing Argentina to shape up fiscally will not restore confidence.
Professor Hanke contends that Argentina should abandon the peso and adopt the American dollar as its legal currency. Professor Calvo says the I.M.F. should offer more than the $7 billion credit line it has set aside for Argentina. A larger contingency fund, he said, "would make a big difference in preventing a collapse in 2001."
But another devaluation in Brazil would cause havoc, leading Professor Calvo to suggest it may be time for Brazil and Argentina to consider a common currency.
Some academics say a similar moment may have arrived for Canada and the United States. The two economies are substantially integrated, and when the Federal Reserve Board decided recently against decreasing interest rates, the Canadian dollar -- sometimes called the loonie because of the water bird depicted on $1 coins -- was pummeled. It dropped to its lowest mark in two years, worth just 65 American cents.
Despite the limping loonie, Canada is enjoying "a continuing stream of good economic news," said Karim Basta, director of global debt strategy at Merrill Lynch. Taxes and unemployment are declining, job creation is vigorous and the Toronto Stock Exchange is outpacing United States markets.
Theoretically, Canada allows the value of its dollar to float, meaning that the market determines what a loonie is worth against the United States dollar. In reality, many economists say, the loonie is consistently undervalued. When it drops too low, Canada's central bank defends it by intervening in the market and buying the currency.
Professor Calvo said such interventions "prevent the currency from doing its job," which is to respond to whatever the market will bear. As a result, Canada has neither the responsiveness of a completely free floating exchange rate nor the predictability of a fixed rate set by a currency board.
But what does it matter if the nation remains stable? Canada's economy is cushioned against doubt, so much so that even with closely contested federal elections tomorrow, it is the character of the candidates, not the condition of the currency, that is likely to determine the outcome.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 9; Column 2; Money and Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: PERSONAL BUSINESS;
A Way Around Air Rage: The Charter Flight

BYLINE: By JANE L. LEVERE

 BODY:
JOHN MYERS, an advertising executive from Chicago, often dreaded business trips. He had to deal with traffic gridlock on the way to the airport, frequent flight delays or cancellations, and tight space once aboard the plane.
But Mr. Myers and a growing number of other disgruntled travelers, flying for both business and pleasure, have found an alternative in charter flights. Aside from having fewer delays, smaller crowds and often roomier aircraft, charters sometimes offer ticket prices that are competitive with those of commercial airlines. 
 Once the realm of Hollywood moguls or the corporate elite, charter flights are becoming more accessible for everyday travelers. Many air-charter services have sprung up in recent years, some with large corporate backing, and the Internet has made it easier to find and book a flight, either by chartering an entire plane or buying a seat on one. Charter-jet flight hours rose nearly 13 percent in the 12 months ended in July 2000, according to the Air Charter Guide of Cambridge, Mass., which tracks the industry.
"The kinds of people who were chartering planes in my first 10 or 12 years of business were from Fortune 50 or Fortune 100 companies; now we see companies we've never heard of," said Jane F. McBride, chairwoman of FlightTime.com, which has been in the air charter business for 15 years.
Mr. Myers, 49, president of the Trade Marketing Group, has become a frequent customer of Indigo, a unit of NewWorldAir Holdings of Chicago, which has offered charter flights on eight-seat Dassault Falcon 20 aircraft since late August. Like many other charter services, Indigo runs flights from smaller, less-crowded airports -- it now flies only between Midway in Chicago and Teterboro in New Jersey, about 10 miles from Manhattan. The company plans to expand service from Midway to airports in White Plains, Minneapolis, Washington and Atlanta next year.
One major reason that Mr. Myers turned to Indigo was the United Airlines labor dispute with its pilots over the summer. United, the nation's largest airline, was forced to cancel many flights, stranding thousands of travelers. "In the summer of discontent with United, it seemed to be an extremely viable option," he said.
Industry analysts predict that more passengers will choose charter services, especially as commercial business air fares continue to climb and as primary airports become more congested. Just this month, United raised its unrestricted business fares by more than 10 percent, and several other big airlines followed suit. Many industry experts say flight delays, which have risen nearly 50 percent over the last five years, will become worse. The Federal Aviation Administration predicts that by 2010, the number of airline passengers will rise almost 60 percent.
And because the economy remains strong, more companies can afford to charter jets, not only for top executives but also for middle-management teams. "As it takes longer and costs more to do business travel, businesses will increasingly look for viable alternatives to, or substitutes for, traditional scheduled service," said Samuel C. Buttrick, an airline analyst at UBS Warburg.
Sensing an opportunity, several big companies have decided in recent months to invest in charter businesses. American Express has an 11 percent stake in Indigo, while McKinsey & Company holds 1.5 percent. CSFB Private Equity, the private investment arm of the Credit Suisse Group, injected $10 million in eBizJets during the summer, while Bombardier, the Canadian aircraft maker, bought Skyjet.
With the exception of Indigo, which operates private jets through a sister company, AirServ, charter companies basically sell the services of a large group of air charter operators. Paul Takemoto, a spokesman for the F.A.A., says the agency regulates the operators, not the brokers.
The charter brokers with the longest track records are FlightTime, based in Waltham, Mass., and Air Partner P.L.C. of Britain, which has been selling charters for nearly two decades. Its American subsidiary, Air Partner Inc., is based in Fort Lauderdale, Fla.
Both companies arrange charter flights on demand; FlightTime offers them online. Also selling via the Internet are eBizJets and Skyjet, both three years old, and eJets.com and CharterHub.com, which have started in the last six months.
Other types of charters are variations on the concept of fractional ownership of corporate aircraft -- basically a time-share arrangement for those who want access to a corporate jet but do not wish to buy one. Instead, they buy shares of a plane and pay monthly maintenance fees, as well as hourly usage fees when they fly.
FlightTime and eBizJets each offer their own versions of fractional ownership. For a midsize business jet, like a Hawker 800 or Citation III, FlightTime's plan requires the purchase of at least 25 hours of flight time, at $58,000, plus a $1,650 hourly fee. At eBizJets, the midsize business jet plan requires the purchase of at least $100,000 in charter services, the equivalent of 40 hours of flying.
Some charter services capitalize on the fact that many charter planes fly home empty after delivering a customer to a destination. Skyjet, for example, lets charter operators post empty flights online, where travelers can bid for them. Alternatively, travelers can post their desired itineraries on the site and request prices from operators. MyJets.com, which started this month, permits travelers planning to use commercial airlines to post their itineraries on its Web site 72 hours before their departure. If a charter operator is flying an empty plane on any of the travelers' routes, MyJets can sell them seats.
PRICES can vary greatly. Indigo and MyJets have rates that essentially match or even undercut scheduled airlines' unrestricted full coach air fares. For example, Indigo's one-way Midway-Teterboro fare is $635, compared with United's unrestricted one-way coach fare of $879 from O'Hare International Airport in Chicago to La Guardia Airport in New York.
Other brokers' rates are based on the type of aircraft chartered, the number of hours flown and whether the itinerary is one-way or round trip. Air Partner charges $800 to $900 a flight hour for a King Air 200 turboprop, while Skyjet charges around $750. For a larger Gulfstream V, Air Partner charges $6,000 to $7,000 an hour, while Skyjet charges around $5,000 an hour.
For a large group traveling together, chartering a plane can still be cheaper than a last-minute trip on a scheduled commercial flight.
Some charter customers say the benefits far outweigh any extra expense. Marc Herring, 40, chief executive of the Herring Media Group, a marketing and advertising company in Sausalito, Calif., sees a charter jet as a traveling office. He has used FlightTime's services seven times this year to fly staff members and clients to conferences and meetings in the United States and overseas. The aircraft are configured to suit his communications needs; he uses telephones already on the planes and brings along his own networked computers, printers and other equipment.
John Thibault, 46, president of Convergent Networks, a telecommunications company in Lowell, Mass., uses eBizJets' services. He says chartering lets him meet his customers at their convenience. "If they're available at 3:15 and I can't find a commercial flight that accommodates their schedule, I can use a private aircraft," he said.
But charters are not used only for corporate travel. Michael Carpenter, 34, an account executive at Keynote Systems in San Mateo, Calif., along with 21 friends, chartered a plane from eJets in August to attend a weekend bachelor party in Las Vegas. "It was $600 per person, only a few hundred dollars more than if we had bought airline tickets at the last minute," he said.
David Catalano, 35, chief executive of Midwest Bankers, a commercial finance company based in Indianapolis, chartered a plane from Skyjet in July to take his family on vacation to Atlantic City. Although the charter cost $14,000 -- more than double what he would have paid had they traveled first class on a commercial airline -- he said it was worth every penny.
At the end of the vacation, Mr. Catalano became stuck in traffic on the way to the airport. If the family had had tickets on a commercial airline, "we would have been toast," he said. "But they were waiting for us," he added of the charter. "With chartering, you're basically buying your way out of stress."
 
Have Jet, Will Travel: Some of the Services

SECTION: Section 12; Page 1; Column 1; Automobiles

LENGTH: 1641 words

HEADLINE: BEHIND THE WHEEL/Chrysler PT Cruiser;
'Le Jimmy Cagney Car' Makes a Big Bang in France

BYLINE: By KEITH MARTIN

DATELINE: PARIS

 BODY:
JUST outside the cathedral at Mont-St.-Michel on the Normandy coast, a school bus pulled up. Elementary school children rushed en masse to the windows, pressed faces to the glass and shouted enthusiastically, "Le Pe-e-e-e Te-e-e-e! C'est magnifique!"
It wasn't the first time that happened during a family trip through France in a Chrysler PT Cruiser -- nor would it be the last. Attracting curious onlookers was a regular occurrence. 
 Americans tend to think of France as having good wines and bad cars, enjoying the Bordeaux and Burgundies, but viewing vintage Peugeots, Citroens and Renaults with a frisson of distaste. No new French cars have been sold in the United States since Peugeot left in 1991, undone by Gallic refusal to cater to Yankee whims and an insouciant approach to mechanical reliability.
Conversely, of course, the French profess disdain for many things American. We eat too much and dress too badly, and don't fully appreciate Mickey Rourke or Jerry Lewis. But the French love Detroit cars, providing they have tailfins and were built before 1961.
It was thus particularly gratifying to find, as an American in Paris, that such tough critics considered the PT Cruiser to be tres bon. Even amid demonstrations against McDonald's and attempts to strip English words from the French lexicon, most of the people we encountered embraced the PT's pugnacious design and its thoroughly American flair.
In the United States, the car is viewed by many as a cute, practical minivan, with little attention paid to its performance. "You're going to go to Europe to drive a rebodied Neon?" a friend who owns a Porsche said with a sniff, recycling a common but largely incorrect assumption about the car's origin. "Be careful you're not blown off the road by all the Mercedeses and BMW's."
True, most American cars fall flat on their tires on the Continent. They are too big for medieval streets, and, thanks to suspensions tuned for straight and level American freeways, lack the precise handling and agility that Europeans expect. And they gulp too much fuel in countries where gasoline costs up to $5 a gallon.
But over the course of 10 days and 1,500 miles, the PT Cruiser defied those stereotypes. Europeans greeted it enthusiastically, a good sign for DaimlerChrysler as it tries to sell the car overseas. While the PT was originally designed for both domestic and export markets, foreign distribution is being held back by a lack of production capacity in Mexico, where all PT's are built, coupled with strong demand in North America.
Our first test car was waiting at Charles de Gaulle airport; equipped with an automatic transmission, it lacked the verve that one would like in heavy Parisian traffic. Two days later, we got a second car that proved much more capable, an upscale Touring Edition with an engine that is not available in American PT's: a 2-liter, 140-horsepower, dual-overhead-cam four cylinder. (Cars sold in the United States come with a 162-horsepower, 2.4-liter engine.) The second car, with its five-speed manual transmission, held its own in Paris, where every roundabout becomes a Nascar-style circle-track event, cars bobbing and weaving for position.
The dark-green Touring Edition had four-wheel
antilock disc brakes, flashy alloy wheels and fat 16-inch tires. Dealer-installed accessories included a Becker navigation system ($2,150) and a parking sensor ($285).
The base price for the Touring Edition, in France, was 129,900 francs, about $16,600 at current exchange rates. With the options, the price of the test car was about $19,000.
I had not had much luck with navigation devices in the United States, finding them cumbersome to use. But the Becker system was a masterpiece of simplicity. It made finding our destinations in Europe, often an exercise in frustration, painless.
In addition, we had acquired a cellular phone compatible with European standards, so that if friends dialed our home number in Oregon, the phone rang in France. We learned to turn it off at night. ("Hello. Oh, you're in Paris and it's 3 a.m. there? Sorry.")
The navigation system and the cell phone proved useful from the start. In Paris, we plunged right into the helter-skelter of roundabouts and confusingly labeled streets, as we tried to find the home of friends on Avenue President Woodrow Wilson. Forty-five minutes after entering the street address into the computer, we arrived, having made only a few unnecessary passes around the Arc de Triomphe.
The PT Cruiser is the right size for Europeans, a midsized vehicle by their standards, although it seems huge next to the diminutive Smart cars that now visually punctuate the streets of European cities. Our friends had just bought a Smart, and insisted, in a manner appropriate for expatriates, that we have a drag race down the Champs-Elysees. (The PT won.)
From Paris we headed to the D-Day beaches of Normandy, following the A-13 Autoroute, a freeway on which the PT cruised comfortably at 140 to 160 kilometers an hour -- about 85 to 95 m.p.h. The posted limit was 130 k.p.h., but no one paid heed. Indeed, we saw the motorcycle-riding freeway police just once, a group of six near a toll booth. (Were they having, in lieu of coffee and doughnuts, their morning espresso and croissants?)
There is a pecking order on European highways. Cars that are tiny or tinny (or both), bearing names like Renault Clio, VW Lupo and Citroen 2CV, plod along in the slow lane; larger cars like the Audi A6 and Lancia Thema occupy the middle; and rarely seen German luxury cars zoom by on the left. The PT alternated between the middle and left lanes, motoring effortlessly at 90 m.p.h. but moving over when a rocket -- usually black and wearing BMW or Mercedes logos -- rushed up from behind with headlamps flashing.
The fuel economy was decent enough; we averaged 24 m.p.g. We added a quart of oil, expensive at $10.25. All told, gas and oil for the trip cost about $300.
The PT Cruiser's big tires and protruding nose gave the car a slightly menacing look; at the Grand Hotel in Dinard, when the doorman saw the car he immediately imitated a Prohibition-era gangster with a tommy gun, delightedly proclaiming: "Le Jimmy Cagney car! Bang bang bang bang." Then, he whipped out his cell phone and invited his friends over to take a look.
After Dinard, we made a stop in the walled city of St.-Malo. Parking the PT was no problem, as it fit easily into the small European spaces. But moving through the crowds that surrounded the car took some time.
Our next stop was a 15th-century chateau in Marcay, not far from Chinon (where Joan of Arc encouraged the dauphin, who later became King Charles VII, to reclaim France from the English). Leaving St.-Malo, we entered Marcay into the navigation system; four hours later, at the end of a winding, one-lane road, we arrived.
In a country that loves style, the PT was a hit. It was immediately recognized, having recently made the cover of several magazines. Everyone wanted to see inside and to sit behind the wheel. We were asked questions about its fuel economy and the size of its gas tank. (At 15 gallons, Europeans said it could be larger.)
The interior proved efficient. There were nooks and crannies everywhere to stick the odd bits of paraphernalia that one accumulates on vacation; brochures, unused maps, empty pop cans and so on. My 9-year-old daughter, Alexandra, a child of the cup holder generation (GenCup?) had some complaints: in the back seat, she couldn't reach her drink, resting in a holder in the back of the center console, without unbuckling her belt, and she had to operate the power window switches, also on the back of the console, with her toes.
When it comes to luggage, I, my daughter and my wife, Cindy, travel like Hannibal crossing the Alps. We arrived with four bags, each large enough for a week's worth of costumes for a member of the New York City Ballet on tour. That luggage fit snugly in the car, with the entire back seat still upright.
By the end of our two weeks, after we had stopped at the equivalent of Wal-Mart to buy two more suitcases, we had to fold down part of the rear seat to make room for them. Our bags swelled with necessary souvenirs like the bottle of 1989 Chateau Mouton-Rothschild that we found at a weekend market for $200.
The bellman who helped us load the car at Chateau de Marcay could not hide his amazement at the amount of luggage we had. I am sure he checked our room to make sure we weren't taking the bedding and a nightstand or two back to America.
On the last day, we made a 300-mile dash to Brussels. Again, we entered the destination, and six hours later arrived at the airport hotel, tired from the trip but not weary from trying to read foreign maps. The simple, straightforward navigation system simply removed one of the great challenges of driving abroad: figuring out where you are and where you are going.
The PT is a good car in America, and it is an even better car in Europe. The roads and traffic patterns allow it to make use of its extremely competent engine and suspension, cruising at speeds that would give you a lifetime of speeding tickets in the United States. The engine likes to be worked, and develops much of its power above 4,000 r.p.m. In America, most driving is done below that, as we putter around town or cruise at 65 m.p.h.
We had expected the PT's styling to create a small spectacle wherever we went -- and it did. But we hadn't expected its mechanical components to hold their own so well in Europe, where cars are driven aggressively, with their brakes and suspensions getting a serious workout every day. The competence of the PT Cruiser's underlying platform was a surprise. In this case, beauty was more than skin deep.
 
INSIDE TRACK: C'est Americain! C'est formidable!

SECTION: Section 4; Page 5; Column 1; Week in Review Desk

LENGTH: 1180 words

HEADLINE: The World;
How to Commit the Perfect Dictatorship

BYLINE: By BLAINE HARDEN

 BODY:
THE Southeast Asian country formerly known as Burma may not be a perfect dictatorship, but it's as close to perfection as is humanly (or perhaps inhumanly) possible at the beginning of the 21st century.
Myanmar is more insulated from outside pressure than North Korea because it can almost always feed its own people. It attracts far less international criticism than Iraq because it is not so anvil-headed as to invade its neighbors or go to war with a superpower. And it's much less likely than China to be destabilized from within by the growing pains of high-tech prosperity. That's because it allows almost none of its citizens to prosper and the Internet is against the law. 
 There are a number of useful, if ugly, lessons to be learned by examining four decades of repression in Myanmar -- a reign of fear, poverty and isolation that, so far, shows no signs of coming to an end.
Outstanding achievement in the field of dictatorial misrule demands more than merely a willingness to commit murder to stay in power. This isn't to suggest that the generals who run Myanmar shy away from murder. Their troops killed at least several hundred pro-democracy demonstrators in 1988. According to the State Department, soldiers have since executed people unwilling or unable to work in forced labor gangs.
What separates the Burmese generals from lesser (and in many cases, former or dead) dictators is their thoroughness and creativity.
"They have effectively destroyed civil society," said Robert Helvey, a retired United States Army colonel who since 1992 has run training seminars in nonviolent resistance along the Thai-Myanmar border. "Burmese society has been atomized. People cannot come together for any purpose because all the organizations have been destroyed."
Whether it's being a Boy Scout or allowing a foreigner into your home, sending an unauthorized e-mail or gathering outdoors in groups larger than five, it is illegal in Myanmar and punishable by a long prison term.
Yet the generals are exceptionally flexible when it helps them hang onto power or attract foreign currency into the country's anemic economy. They have made a series of sovereignty-sharing deals with armed ethnic minority groups on the country's borders. These cease-fires have ended decades of war and freed the regime to focus its repressive talents -- and most of its 400,000 troops -- on the unarmed majority.
Since making all this carefully calculated peace, the generals have welcomed investment capital from ethnic minority groups that specialize in the sale of heroin and amphetamines. Many of the buildings erected in the country in recent years have been built with laundered drug money, according to the American embassy there.
The proven fragility of authoritarian leaders -- most recently in Serbia, but going back to the henchmen of apartheid in South Africa and to Eastern Europe's Communist bosses -- suggests that even the most repressive governments are vulnerable to mass democratic opposition.
"In general, the rule of thumb is the more brutal, the more brittle," said Dr. Peter Ackerman, an expert on nonviolent resistance and the principal content advisor to "A Force More Powerful," a recent PBS series on the triumphs of nonviolence in the 20th century.
But that rule of thumb doesn't seem to matter in Myanmar, at least for moment. "You have to distribute resistance to all strata of society and the Burmese people have not yet figured out a way to do this," Dr. Ackerman said. "Nonviolent resistance is a strategy that doesn't always work. I think the Burmese dictatorship is doing a good job at doing a bad job."
For purposes of comparison, it's worth noting that the failed regime of Slobodan Milosevic did a bad job at doing a bad job. In the run-up to the October election that Mr. Milosevic lost and then failed to steal, his regime seem to fall asleep at the dictatorial switch. It allowed opposition groups to organize and distribute literature in the smallest towns and villages in Serbia.
IN a display of half-hearted authoritarianism that would never pass muster in Myanmar, Mr. Milosevic ignored the power of the Internet to mobilize his opponents. He tolerated independent media outlets in the Serbian capital and in many other cities. He allowed foreign journalists to roam all over, sniffing out news that filtered back into Serbia via the Internet and independent news media.
Burmese generals know better. If they don't control it, they ban it. And the foreign press is kept out (unless they sneak in as tourists, as I did).
The generals in Myanmar have perfected a style of governance that limits information, sows distrust and prevents private misery from snowballing into mass political action. "The individual who walks out of his house in Burma in the morning is confronted with the overpowering control of the state," said Mr. Helvey, who periodically trains Burmese to go back inside their country and try to organize nonviolent action against the government. "People do not have a place to go to talk to each other because they know that there are informers everywhere."
The country, of course, has an immensely popular, articulate and internationally acclaimed opposition leader. But that leader, Daw Aung San Suu Kyi, a winner of the Noble Peace Prize, is under house arrest, as she has been for more than 6 of the past 12 years. Nearly all the leaders of her opposition party are also locked up.
Locking up "the lady," as she is widely known in Myanmar, has all but paralyzed serious opposition to the generals, according to Mr. Helvey, who served as military attache in the 1980's at the American Embassy in Myanmar. Some of his current pro-democracy work is funded by the Albert Einstein Foundation, a Boston-based nonprofit group that supports nonviolent activism around the world. "Politics in Burma have always been personalized and Aung San Suu Kyi is the symbol of the entire pro-democracy movement," said Mr. Helvey. "Without her, the movement has not demonstrated the ability to take on a strategic struggle."
The dictators in Myanmar are also geographically lucky. Positioned between China and India, two huge and antagonistic neighbors, the regime has been able to play on their mutual suspicions to acquire cheap arms and make favorable trade deals.
More than anything else, though, it seems to be single-mindedness that allows the generals to remain at the top of their game.
In the mid-1990's, they flirted with the Chinese model of combining openness to technology and foreign investment with continued political repression. That experiment, though, gave substantial economic power to people who had no loyalty to the regime.
"The generals decided, 'Uh-oh, we can't tolerate this,' " said Mr. Helvey. "They seem to be going back to isolation. They aren't trying to have it both ways."
In the past two years, the military has taken over scores of profitable foreign-owned companies and hundreds of businessmen have fled the country. In a perfect dictatorship, things are getting back to normal.

LENGTH: 1014 words

HEADLINE: The Election;
Trying to Escape the Purgatory of Parity

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
BACK in headier times when George W. Bush was first stepping onto the stage as the Republican Party's putative savior from the southwest, Karl Rove, the Texas governor's chief strategist, liked to draw grand parallels with William McKinley and prophesy that the 2000 election would be as historically significant as the election of 1896.
Like Newt Gingrich before him, Mr. Rove, a history buff, was fascinated by how McKinley and the political king maker, Mark Hanna, produced a new and enduring Republican majority by recognizing that the Civil War issues that had dominated politics were no longer relevant to a rapidly industrializing nation entering a new century. 
 McKinley assembled a new political coalition that included both the new industrialists and their workers, the millions of immigrants pouring into the nation. As a result the Republican Party went on to dominate the Presidency for most of the next three decades. Similarly, Mr. Rove liked to say, his candidate would prove the McKinley of the new century, expanding the Republican Party base by drawing in Hispanics, suburbanites and independents.
Of course, as every poisonously partisan new day of the presidential standoff makes clear, Mr. Rove and Mr. Bush failed to create that commanding new majority. And in their failure they are in good company.
Eight years ago when he defeated Mr. Bush's father, President Clinton thought his New Democratic promises of a third-way politics, neither conservative nor liberal, could effect such a grand realignment. Two years later his party lost control of Congress and he was left scrambling further to the right.
Next came Mr. Gingrich, the man who brought House Republicans into power. He promoted his own brand of New Age conservatism, which he said would ensure American supremacy in the global economy by cutting government bureaucracy while upholding traditional cultural values. But he and his allies also overplayed their hand. Bill Clinton was re-elected. And Mr. Gingrich was repudiated in the fires of Mr. Clinton's impeachment.
Now, with both parties at this extraordinary impasse, pollsters, intellectuals and party professionals are engaged in a desperate search for a way to get beyond parity. The old order, they say, is crumbling.
"I think what happened is that we have come to the end of the politics of the industrial age," said Al From, the head of the Democratic Leadership Council, the incubator of Democratic centrism. "The old New Deal alignment is finally gone, but as we come into the information age the new lines are not yet settled."
Perhaps the new political order cannot be forged without the pain of cultural dislocation. "I think historians generally say it takes some great crisis of one kind or another to break a political impasse in the parties, the Civil War being one of them," said Eric Foner, a Columbia University historian who studies Reconstruction.
"The 1890's is another example," Mr. Foner said, referring to the McKinley era. "In 1893 there began a big economic depression which really shifted votes very markedly to the Republican Party." He also cited the Great Depression, when Franklin D. Roosevelt began an era of Democratic rule.
Indeed, some politicians believe that the new alignments of the information age will be formed in recession and the backlash it might cause against the global economy. "If you have a downturn, the New Economy will find out who its friends are," said Representative Thomas M. Davis III, the moderate Virginia Republican who heads the House Republicans' re-election committee.
MR. Davis argues that it is his party, with its emphasis on free trade, small government, laissez-faire economics and distrust of organized labor, that will win the allegiance of technology entrepreneurs, small businessmen of all races, and suburban wired workers to create a ruling majority. "You get a cataclysmic event when the lines get drawn and the Democrats will go with their labor base and their underclass," he said.
MR. FROM contends, rather, that it's the New Democrats, with their emphasis on social tolerance, activist but limited government, global economics and more portable benefits, that will become the ruling majority for a nation growing more affluent and better educated. "I really think Clintonism or the third way is the formula for the information age," he said, arguing that Mr. Clinton's ideas never got a fair test because of the Lewinsky scandal.
Then again, not everyone thinks the global economy will be the main agent of change. At the New America Foundation, a Washington group dedicated to bringing new ideas to politics, Michael Lind, a senior fellow, said that the fiscal strain on the Medicare and Social Security systems and the dividing lines over how to deal with it might turn out to be the crisis that changes the political order. "The big question is, What kind of social safety net do we have?" Mr. Lind said. "How is it financed? How much of the economy does it take up? And right now each of the two parties have bits and pieces of the answer. To see a real realignment on this issue there would be a showdown over the question and my guess is Social Security or Medicare will drive it."
With so much uncertainty in the air, the first order of business may be to settle who controls each major party. Those internecine brawls are just beginning. New Democrats accuse Mr. Gore of abandoning them for old-style populism unsuited to the times, while liberals continue to bristle at the New Democrats' fiscal conservatism.
Among Republicans, conservatives are arguing that Mr. Bush should have relied more on energizing the party's conservative base. But others, including Mr. Gingrich, say the party has to work harder to reach Latinos, African-Americans and the young.
Which side will seize the future, and through what issues? No one knows. After this dead-heat election, politicians and voters alike find themselves, uncomfortably, "Wandering between two worlds," as Matthew Arnold once wrote, "One dead, the other powerless to be born."
