HEADLINE: PORTFOLIOS, ETC.;
Two Views of a Marked-Down Market

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
THE silver lining in the market sell-off this fall is that those worrisome -- yet often ignored -- high stock valuations have come down sharply. But have they fallen enough to warrant tossing whatever cash you have back into stocks?
According to Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, the market, as measured by the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, has not been this cheap since either the end of 1998 or the beginning of 1999, depending on the valuation measure. That is enticing, given that the Nasdaq composite index, heavy with technology, is down to 2,904, 42 percent below its record high in March. 
 One valuation measure, designed by Peter J. Canelo, a United States investment strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, shows that valuations have not been this low since October 1998, when the S.& P. 500 hit bottom after Russia defaulted on its foreign debt and the giant Long-Term Capital Management hedge fund collapsed. The second gauge, designed by Byron R. Wien, Morgan Stanley's other United States investment strategist, shows that valuations have not been at this level since January 1999, although they were lower in October 1998.
"The market, ostensibly, looks very, very cheap," Mr. Canelo said. "Instead of selling, people should be buying."
But there are reasons for pause. Mr. Canelo's gauge says the stock market is now about 22 percent undervalued while Mr. Wien's measure says the market is still about 12 percent overvalued. In other words, one gauge says the market is much cheaper and attractive while the other says the market is cheaper but not that attractive.
The main reason for the difference is in the gauges' assumptions for operating earnings growth over the next five years for the S.& P. 500 companies. Mr. Canelo assumes that earnings growth will average 12.5 percent; Mr. Wien assumes 9.8 percent, just above the 10-year average of 8.8 percent for the stocks that have been in the index.
Mr. Canelo says he chose the higher growth rate because the nearly 200 major changes in the components of the S.& P. 500 index over the last several years have increased its earnings potential. Based on the current index, he said, the prospective five-year earnings growth rate would be 14.8 percent. But partly because the 10-year average earnings growth rate for the 500 stocks now in the S.& P. is only 11.2 percent, he cut his prospective five-year rate to 12.5 percent.
The earnings outlook right now is even slower. According to First Call/Thomson Financial, which collects Wall Street earnings forecasts, the projected fourth-quarter growth rate for the S.& P. 500 has plunged to 10.7 percent from 15.6 percent on Oct. 1. The forecast for technology earnings has dropped to 16 percent from 29 percent. For next year, the overall forecast is down to 11.8 percent from 14.8 percent. Morgan Stanley's 2001 projection is at 10.4 percent.
And the economic environment is not as positive as it was in October 1998. By Nov. 17, 1998, the Federal Reserve, trying to ward off a financial crisis, had cut its benchmark interest rate by three-quarters of a percentage point, to 4.75 percent. The price of crude oil was below $12 a barrel and the Consumer Price Index was on a pace to climb just 1.6 percent that year.
Today, crude oil costs more than $35 a barrel. And the Fed, at its November policy meeting, indicated that rate cuts would wait until it was assured that inflation, at 3.4 percent in the last 12 months, was in check.
INVESTORS also have more reason to worry that the economy is in for a jarring hard landing, instead of the gentle one they want. Last week, the government reported that the trade deficit widened to a record in September -- a signal that the economy grew much more slowly than expected in the third quarter, according to Robert J. Barbera, chief economist at Hoenig & Company. Mr. Barbera expects third-quarter growth to be reduced to 1.7 percent, at an annual rate, from the 2.7 percent reported initially.
So the earnings outlook is likely to worsen, and the best valuation reading is between Mr. Canelo's and Mr. Wien's.

HEADLINE: PRIVATE SECTOR;
Bittersweet Tasks in Cereal City

BYLINE: By DAVID BARBOZA

DATELINE: BATTLE CREEK, Mich.

 BODY:
CARLOS M. GUTIERREZ, the chief executive of the Kellogg Company, knows something about risk.
His family fled Cuba in 1960 during the Communist revolution. He got early business training during an economic crisis in Mexico. And one of his first tasks as the top man at Kellogg early last year was to shut down a plant here in "Cereal City." 
 His biggest risk, however, may have come in orchestrating Kellogg's decision last month to spend $3.8 billion in cash to acquire the snack maker Keebler Foods in the hopes of remaking Kellogg as a diversified food company.
Mr. Gutierrez, who is trying to strengthen a company stung in recent years by slumping cereal sales and an eroding market share, said investors had to expect such risky ventures during difficult times.
"I don't think we have a choice," he said in an interview at his headquarters office here. "We have to find growth and we have to find it quickly, and that almost has to entail risk."
So Mr. Gutierrez, who took office 18 months ago, is bulking up Kellogg's snack and convenience food business, reshuffling management and reaching out to consumers by, among other things, stalking supermarket aisles.
"He goes to the grocery store to see how people shop," said Tim O'Day, a longtime friend and an executive at the Leo Burnett Company, Kellogg's advertising agency. "He wants to know what's happening in stores, what people are responding to."
Indeed, Wall Street analysts say Mr. Gutierrez, 47, has brought a fresh and open approach to Kellogg that had been lacking at the company.
"During the 1990's, Kellogg may have been the worst-managed packaged foods company I followed," said John McMillin, who studies the food industry for Prudential Securities. "I think Gutierrez is a major uptick from past management."
Friends and colleagues, who describe Mr. Gutierrez as quiet and reflective, said his management style was to soothe rather than to scold.
He is unpretentious, they say. During an interview at his office a few weeks ago, Mr. Gutierrez was dressed casually and spoke in even tones about returning to Cuba someday, about one of his corporate heroes (the late Robert C. Goizueta, the Coca-Cola chief and another Cuban refugee) and about the future of Kellogg.
Calling the Keebler deal "transformational," he discussed plans for innovative new products like Snack 'Ums, cereal-flavored snacks in canisters, and efforts to use the Internet to promote more traditional brands, like Frosted Flakes, Raisin Bran and Pop Tarts.
Mr. Gutierrez was born into a wealthy Havana family.
In the 1950's, his father owned a flourishing pineapple export company. But in 1960, after Fidel Castro came to power, everything changed. His father was jailed after arguing with Che Guevara, Mr. Castro's minister of industries.
"I recall the day he was taken," Mr. Gutierrez said. "I'll never forget the olive-green uniforms coming to the door and taking my father to the car."
Several hours later, after his father had been released, the family was ordered out of Cuba. They fled with $8,000 and 25 suitcases for Miami Beach, where, Mr. Gutierrez said, he began to learn English from a hotel bellhop.
"At the time, my father thought it would be over in a matter of weeks," he said.
But the Gutierrez family never returned to Cuba. His father steered the family through Florida, New York, Mexico and Florida again, all by the time Carlos was 17. "My father worked for 20 to 25 years trying to recreate Cuba and recreate his business there, and he was never able to do it," Mr. Gutierrez said.
In the early 1970's, when his father went to Mexico to start a business, he went along and taught English before finding a job at Kellogg's Mexican unit. He took business courses at the Monterrey Institute of Technology and married a young Mexican named Edilla. (They now have three children.) He was transferred to Battle Creek, where he began his corporate ascent.
William Lamothe, a former chief executive at Kellogg, recalled how Mr. Gutierrez, in his 30's, returned to Mexico in the mid-1980's and helped turn around a struggling plant.
"He was a young man when we took a chance," Mr. Lamothe said. "Basically we didn't have anyone else to put in Mexico. The products there were rated low," he added. "But he really turned that situation around."
Mr. Gutierrez was named executive vice president of Kellogg's Asian-Pacific operations in 1994, just weeks after his father died. Mr. Gutierrez said his father would have been proud.
"He was my confidant throughout my life," Mr. Gutierrez said.
He returned to Battle Creek in 1996 and was named president and chief operating officer in 1998. Six months later, with the shares of Kellogg plummeting, the company's board called a special meeting to appoint Mr. Gutierrez as chief executive, succeeding Arnold G. Langbo, who stepped down under shareholder pressure.
Rising to the top spot was bittersweet, Mr. Gutierrez said, because one of his first orders of business was to shutter some Kellogg factories and refocus its energies in a downsizing effort.
Kellogg had been upset by slumping cereal sales and evidence that consumers had turned toward private-label cereals and convenient breakfast foods like bagels and muffins.
Shares of Kellogg shot up, though, after Mr. Gutierrez was named chief executive. Many see him as approachable and accessible. (He does not play golf and is "a rabid Yankees fan," one colleague said.)
Of course, some Wall Street analysts say the honeymoon is now over, and they wonder about profits that remain lackluster.
"He's very personable and he understands foreign markets," Mr. McMillin said. "But he hasn't yet knocked the cover off the ball; they still haven't stopped losing global market share."
Mr. Gutierrez, though, points to the acquisitions of Worthington Foods, a maker of vegetarian fare, and Keebler, the No. 2 snack maker in the United States behind Nabisco, as bold moves. Cereal now accounts for 75 percent of the company's revenue; the goal is to get that down to 40 percent by 2004.
"Keebler gets us there," Mr. Gutierrez said.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 7; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1260 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING;
I.B.M. Hews to Its Vision, Even as Growth Slows

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER

 BODY:
THERE was a special guest star at I.B.M.'s annual fall meeting with analysts early this month in New York -- none other than Louis V. Gerstner Jr., the company's chairman and chief executive.
Mr. Gerstner, who usually meets with analysts only each spring, said he wanted to steal a few minutes to support his new senior management team before turning over the meeting to Samuel J. Palmisano, whom he promoted to president and chief operating officer -- and heir apparent -- in a shake-up last July. 
 Analysts figured that Mr. Gerstner knew he had some explaining to do. In the first six years after Mr. Gerstner took over, in the spring of 1993, I.B.M.'s stock was stellar, climbing from a low of $10.25 that summer to a peak of $137.88 in July 1999. Since then, though, disappointments had accumulated, on sales growth most of all.
When third-quarter revenue fell short of expectations this year, the stock slid below $100, closing at $99.75 Friday. I.B.M. was in the embarrassing position of reporting that net sales of $62.8 billion for the first nine months were actually down $600 million, or 1 percent, from the period in 1999.
It was not enough that I.B.M. continued its string of double-digit earnings gains in the quarter, despite its sales problems. No one questions that Mr. Gerstner, a former McKinsey consultant, has been a brilliant financial engineer since he arrived on the scene to pull I.B.M. back from the brink of collapse and disintegration.
But improving efficiency and spending billions on stock buybacks do little to convince Wall Street that I.B.M. has a sustainable strategy for exploiting its technological prowess and marketing power.
"He hasn't been able to move the revenue up and at the end of the day, that's what you've got to do," said Ulric Weil, a technology strategist at Friedman, Billings, Ramsey & Company who has followed I.B.M. for 25 years. "If you can't grow revenue at double digits in the technology sector, investors worry about the future."
MR. GERSTNER'S few minutes at the meeting stretched out to more than 20, and he later participated in the question period. Combative as ever, he argued that I.B.M. was well on its way to global leadership in a huge, fast-growing e-business world. Only shortsighted analysts would pay much attention to the revenue shortfalls, he said, adding, "In my opinion, it has been a very good year for I.B.M."
For contrast, Mr. Gerstner turned to the recent trend of crashing dot-com companies. In a quick, I-told-you-so recap, he reminded analysts that 18 months ago, he described high-flying dot-com ventures as "fireflies before the storm."
He said I.B.M., meanwhile, had continued introducing products, expanding services and adding production capacity as part of a transition that began in 1993 to exploit the $1 trillion market for e-business software, hardware and services. Inevitably, he said, the e-business winners would be established institutions and companies that had long been I.B.M.'s core customers. "There is no new economy," he said of the Internet. "The wars haven't changed; it's just that somebody's invented gunpowder, and the one who uses it first will win the next battle."
Perhaps Mr. Gerstner's fervor was heightened by knowing that I.B.M.'s slide would soon cost him millions of dollars. The week after the meeting, he sold 700,000 shares, or about 9 percent of his holdings, for a total of $59.6 million as part of what I.B.M. called a regular program of tax planning and diversification.
Most analysts agree with much of Mr. Gerstner's assessment of where the company is headed and the steps it must take. Some doubt that I.B.M.'s lineup of servers for the Internet market is as strong as I.B.M. contends, in comparison with companies like Sun Microsystems and Hewlett-Packard. But they say the challenges are not comparable to those faced by giants like AT&T and WorldCom, which are talking about radically transforming themselves through divestitures and spin-offs -- or by I.B.M. itself in 1993, when John F. Akers, Mr. Gerstner's predecessor, hired Morgan Stanley to study similar steps.
"There's no question about the direction," said John B. Jones Jr., who follows I.B.M. at Salomon Smith Barney. "Pace is the issue."
The pace of growth is almost certain to pick up in the near term, if only because sales and earnings in future quarters will be compared to the sluggish results of this year. Mr. Gerstner is projecting "high single digits" revenue growth, not far under the overall information technology average of 10 percent to 11 percent. Some analysts expect the stock to bounce back to the $120-to-$130 range in 12 to 18 months.
It is not a unanimous view, of course. There are firm skeptics, like Bob Djurdjevic of Annex Research in Phoenix, who calls I.B.M.'s belief that customers will value its plumbing -- new hardware and software -- over that of competitors a major mistake. "They still think of themselves as a technology company, but no one cares about technology today," Mr. Djurdjevic said.
All that customers want, Mr. Djurdjevic contended, are the services that technology makes possible, like better handling of production and sales data. In his view, although I.B.M. is the world's largest computer services company, its culture has diverted it from a more focused, full-throttle attack on that market and has clouded its future. While slower-growing hardware and software businesses at I.B.M. generate the cash that allows Mr. Gerstner to keep buying shares and finance capital investment, the effort to maintain them is a threat to I.B.M.'s prosperity, Mr. Djurdjevic said.
Even those who endorse Mr. Gerstner's basic strategy worry that growth will falter. They point to I.B.M.'s serious problems in recent years in translating its strategy into business. "Poor execution has been upsetting Lou's projections too regularly," said Gary Helmig, an analyst at Wit SoundView.
I.B.M. has had unanticipated delays in closing software sales and pushed back by nine months its target for introducing features needed to make its new Shark data storage products useful for networked computers. Turning even good news into a mishap, it underestimated demand for its innovative microchips so badly that it ended up well short of capacity to meet orders. As a result, I.B.M.'s server group has had to wait in line behind some outside customers to get components needed to ship its newest large server computers, the zSeries 900.
"All the nails were committed to building other people's houses," Mr. Helmig said.
Henceforth, the primary responsibility for cutting down on such execution errors will be falling on Mr. Palmisano, 48. Stephen Dube, who follows the company for Wasserstein Perella Securities, said Mr. Palmisano's promotion by Mr. Gerstner, 58, had been 75 percent a matter of getting operations under control and 25 percent a selection of a potential successor.
Without shedding most of its traditional businesses, I.B.M. is too big to ever grow at a 20 percent clip again. But analysts say revenue growth can still creep past the industry average, into the low teens, without undermining earnings, assuming that Mr. Palmisano meets his mandate.
"We should be grateful for the consistency of the bottom line," said Laura Conigliaro of Goldman Sachs, adding that reliable profits made I.B.M. the closest thing to a good defensive stock in the technology sector now. "It's not broken. But the company has to show us revenue growth if it is going to be important in the future."

HEADLINE: New Retail Chiefs Face Ghosts of Profits Past

BYLINE: By LESLIE KAUFMAN

 BODY:
IT'S never easy being the new kid on the block. If it is Christmas and you are a retail executive, it's worse. Every move you make is scrutinized, tallied, weighed against your future.
Whether selling gold-sequined pants, plain microwaves or hot new MP3 phones, the nation's merchants have always seen the holidays season as a do-or-die time. The six weeks from Thanksgiving to Christmas bring in some 50 percent of most stores' revenue, and as much as 60 percent of profits, according to Jeffrey Feiner, a retail stock analyst at Lehman Brothers. 
 The 2000 holiday shopping season is shaping up to be the biggest pressure cooker in a decade. With the American economy flying high for the last decade, the holiday season has been all rum punch and mistletoe for retailers: sales have grown at 4 to 5 percent a year since the mid-1990's. But the dawn of the millennium has been rough: the stock market is volatile, gasoline prices have soared, interest rates are higher and consumers' appetite seems largely sated by earlier buying binges. And concern about the undecided presidential election is distracting shoppers just as the holiday season starts to peak.
Dan Barry, the chief retail economist at Merrill Lynch, is predicting "the worst Christmas for sales and profits since 1995" and the worst Christmas sales since at least 1990. In his latest holiday report, Mr. Barry predicted that comparable store sales at major retailing chains this Christmas season would rise only "a paltry 2.5 percent," half as much as they did last year.
Ouch. In recent months, the largest national retailers -- from Target to Gap Inc. to even the usually invincible Wal-Mart Stores -- warned that sales growth was slowing going into the fourth quarter. Consumers could scent a whiff of desperation in the air as some stores, trying to rev up spending, put up holiday decorations as early as September or offered early, aggressive promotions through coupons or other discounts.
In this turbulent year of all years, executive turnover at the top of the nation's retailers has been particularly high. Among those appointing new chief executives or chief operating officers were Wal-Mart, the country's largest retailer, and Wal-Mart.com; Sears, Roebuck, the largest department store chain; Kmart, the second-largest discounter; J. C. Penney, the second-largest department store chain; Toys "R" Us; Nordstrom; Lord & Taylor, a division of the May Company; Polo Ralph Lauren; and the Victoria's Secret catalog unit, a division of Intimate Brands.
"It is really unusual to have so many major, major broadline retailers with new C.E.O.'s," said George Strachan, retail analyst at Goldman Sachs.
Each new boss faces a very different task. H. Lee Scott Jr.'s responsibility, to stay on top of the well-oiled Wal-Mart colossus, is hardly the staggering challenge facing Charles C. Conaway as he moves to halt a slow sinking at Kmart. Each new executive brings a different range of experience and confidence to the task, and each has had a different amount of time to try to shape the holiday season.
Alan J. Lacy was a six-year veteran of Sears who had already tackled some of the company's worst financial messes before moving up to become chief executive on Oct. 1, but Roger Farah arrived at Polo Ralph Lauren after spending six years hawking sportswear at the former Woolworth, now Venator. Jeanne Jackson left Gap Inc. to become the chief at Wal-Mart.com last April, but Allen Questrom, formerly of Barneys, took the helm at Penney only in mid-September.
Whatever their backgrounds, the executives face a common challenge: Whether the expectation is fair or not, they have to make this Christmas a success.
"The financial market has a very short memory in terms of how long people are in their jobs," said Robert Kerson, president of the global retail practice at Korn Ferry, who conducted the recent searches that led to the hiring of Jane Elfers at Lord & Taylor and John Eyler at Toys "R" Us. "They've got to show improvement. They've got to build some momentum. They are going to be judged to some degree by the performance of this last quarter."
The difficulty is this: Everyone in the business knows that a new chief executive can do very little to improve sales at Christmas, especially if she or he arrives just weeks or months earlier. The vast bulk of merchandise is bought months in advance. The merchandising strategies, the advertising buys and the schedule for putting key products on sale are already choreographed.
They are all dealing with "inherited plans," said Herbert Mines, chairman of Herbert Mines Associates, an executive recruiting firm that specializes in retailing. "So the trick with Christmas is to make sure that the stores are well manned, that day-to-day advertising is good, that the merchandise gets into the stores. It's the execution part of the job."
Still, it would be folly not to take some sort of stand. So when four of these newly initiated executives were asked about their plans for making a difference this holiday season, they had answers. Surprisingly, perhaps, they were almost completely different.
 
Nordstrom
Reinvigorating Customer Service
In September, at a moment of crisis, Blake Nordstrom stepped to the presidency of the company that bears his name. Despite the boom in retailing, the company, based in Seattle and once a model of merchandising and service, has been struggling with disappointing sales and earnings. Its previous management team, the first to contain no family members, had taken many steps toward refashioning the stores -- from modernizing the clothing assortment to updating the computer system to allowing for centralized inventory and purchasing.
But the rapid moves created unrest among the chain's rank-and-file, who felt left out of the decisions in a system that increasingly concentrated decisions at headquarters.
While Mr. Nordstrom, representing the fourth generation of his family to run the business, thought that the company had perhaps gone too far out on fashion statements, alienating longtime customers, he also felt that re-energizing the sales force should be his No. 1 task.
In the days after his ascension, Mr. Nordstrom toured stores, meeting with some 4,000 employees in 10 days. Nordstrom's longstanding reputation -- for service and the personal relationship that its clerks are encouraged to develop with customers -- is a key point of differentiation in the market, he explained.
"If our people don't feel positive, customers are going to feel it immediately," he said. "You can't edict a smile or excellent service."
Mr. Nordstrom had been one of six company co-presidents until February, then president of the Nordstrom rack group, a major division. In his short tenure as chief, he has enacted or is considering changes both large and small, many of them in response to suggestions from his 10-day staff tour. He said, for example, that he intends to introduce soon a package that could increase compensation as well as incentives for sales associates.
Beyond that, he said, he is using the resources of headquarters "to remove barriers, real or perceived, between sales people and customers."
In response to complaints by employees that the worst way to hurt a customer relationship is to be out of stock on a desired item, Nordstrom is testing an Intranet service in two regions. It will allow sales staff at one store to post needed items on a central computerized list that all stores can check -- eliminating the need to call stores one at a time. "This is a Band-Aid to help out sales people until we have a system that is more appropriate," Mr. Nordstrom said. In contrast to the practice of previous years, clerks receive additional compensation when they find the product in another store and make a sale.
In another change, customers no longer have to pay delivery charges when items are out of stock in one store and available for purchase from another.
Last month, the stores also made consumer-friendly changes to a credit certificate program. Originally, customers could accumulate points by making purchases through the Nordstrom credit card, eventually earning a gift certificate that could be redeemed only for merchandise. Now, the customer can use the certificates against a credit-card account balance. "These are all small changes," Mr. Nordstrom said, "but when you add them they signal a 100 percent commitment to the customer."
 
Wal-Mart.com
It All Comes Down To Fundamentals
The leviathan of the retail world has not led a charmed life online. Started in 1996, Wal-Mart.com lagged behind other virtual powerhouses like Amazon.com and eToys.com in attracting customers and building a significant revenue stream. In the summer of 1999, Wal-Mart.com announced that it was undergoing a major overhaul for the holidays; in fact, it did not get its revamped version up and running until January 2000. But soon after, Wal-Mart, determined to tackle the problem more systematically, spun off the site into an independent entity with venture capital backing. In April, it brought in Ms. Jackson, a well-respected retailer, who most recently had been running Banana Republic and Gap Online, to be chief executive of its online arm.
Ms. Jackson found that some of her long-term goals were at odds with her holiday wish list. In October, she shut down Wal-Mart.com for almost a month while it switched to a new technology platform; the timing of the move caused worried murmuring among analysts.
Acknowledging that it was "risky" to close up shop for a month during the run-up to the holidays, she said that the costs of operating the old Web site while she worked to start the new one were prohibitive. "It was just better business to devote 100 percent of staff effort to the new system," she said.
But knowing full well the importance of the Christmas season, she set out to improve her site accordingly. Last holiday season, on-time delivery and customer responsiveness had been a trip wire for the site. Yet while Wal-Mart.com broke ground on a new fully automated fulfillment center this summer, it will not be nearly ready this December. So Ms. Jackson hired a team to work with the third-party contractors that handled the order-taking and package delivery.
"We have to make sure that when the customer orders something, that order gets sent to the distribution center immediately," she said. "And we had to make sure the mechanisms were in place that, in case that did not happen, we caught it."
Another big push involved visibility. "I do not think we hit the customer's radar screen," she said of previous holiday seasons. To remedy that, she focused on promoting the hot products, like scooters, that she knew consumers would want this year. The scooters have been featured prominently in store circulars, television advertising and online marketing programs.
Wal-Mart's November circular, distributed to roughly 90 million people, advertised that certain key products were available at Wal-Mart.com as well as in the stores. The move paid off when pool tables, for example, priced at $188, sold out quickly at the stores but were still available online.
"This is all nuts and bolts," Ms. Jackson said, "but there was not enough focus on it last year."
 
Sears, Roebuck
An Energy Boost At a Fashion Show
A week ago Wednesday, barely six weeks into the job, Alan J. Lacy stood before 3,500 Sears employees and received an unexpectedly thunderous ovation. For the last five years, Sears had invited its employees to preview its Christmas advertising campaign. But Mr. Lacy, who took the helm of the enormous chain on Oct. 1, wanted to make a splashier effort to rally the troops.
So he staged a 45-minute runway show to the throbbing beat of Christina Aguilera, the teen pop sensation. To showcase the products that the company expects to be hot, Sears hired 18 model-dancers to prance down the aisle, displaying everything from negligees and boxer shorts to Craftsman screwdrivers -- the company expects to sell some 15 million of the tools in the fourth quarter. The audience went wild, showing an energy that Sears executives said they had not seen in years.
In many ways, Mr. Lacy faces the toughest job among the new retailing chief executives. For years, the venerable Sears has been slowly losing market share to aggressive discounters like Target and to fast-growing merchants like Kohl's. His predecessor, Arthur C. Martinez, started to clean up the company's dowdy fashion image, moved deeper into the hot home furnishings and appliance market and took several other obvious steps to help put the retailer back on track.
It is up to Mr. Lacy, who made his reputation by stopping losses in Sears's credit card business, to engineer new ways to make the vast chain leaner and more cost-efficient.
Stepping up to the chief's job as late as he did, Mr. Lacy said he knew he could only tinker around the edges with the programs in place, which he said pleased him.
That he has done. In response to the decision by Circuit City last summer to pull out of the appliances market, Mr. Lacy ordered new commercials to emphasize that Sears, which makes the popular Kenmore brand, should be the choice for shoppers.
Mr. Lacy also says he has been focusing on keeping the wheels of the Sears operation well-greased. "We have to make sure we really execute very well programs in place," he said. To do that, he is keeping a close eye on plans to keep the sales floors well-staffed, always a huge concern during the bustling holiday season, and the seasonal help well-trained.
Then there was that fashion show/ pep rally. "People are working day and night at headquarters to make sure things happen properly," he said. "We wanted to get people fully engaged in what is going to happen at the store level and also to go back to their desks happy."
 
Lord & Taylor
Making Big Bets On Best Sellers
From the moment that Jane Elfers became president of Lord & Taylor on June 1, "the holiday season was my first and foremost focus." she said. Despite the short time frame, she added, "you can have, I think I did have, an effect."
Lord & Taylor, the venerable chain with headquarters on Fifth Avenue in Manhattan, has been struggling to remain hip and relevant in an overcrowded retail environment. While its peers boomed in recent years, Lord & Taylor saw its sales per square foot drop from $237 in 1997 to $218 in 1999.
Ms. Elfers is determined to turn around the trend quickly. As a veteran merchant, she has chosen to put all of her energies into the goods on the store shelves.
She assembled her team of buyers early last summer and asked them to predict what products would be big for the holidays. Then she bet big. When everyone agreed that leather was going to be a runaway hit for the season, she went department to department, asking how her stores could be bigger than ever in leather goods.
The answer was not only to expand coats and boots into color, but also to offer leather hats, leather scarves and leather accessories.
Animal hide was not exactly a secret this season, but Ms. Elfers said her team got behind other merchandise that other stores will not receive until spring, like flower pins and chain belts.
"Many manufacturers were not on the trend," she recalled of the shiny belts. "It is not like they were sitting around with this stuff. And we said, 'What can you do for us?' We placed huge orders, and it is doing wonderfully for us on the accessory floor."
Ms. Elfers said that once the key products were selected, the store followed up with promotions. "We made a lot more out of big items instead of doing a lot on many little items," she said. Leather is featured on the cover of the seasonal catalog and has been displayed prominently in direct mail promotions and print advertising.
"Every store chooses key products," she conceded. "But this year the difference for us was the intenseness of the focus. We were very, very intense." In a telephone interview, in fact, she sounded very, very intense -- and hopeful.
"I am very optimistic about the season," she said. "We are already seeing some nice results."

HEADLINE: INVESTING WITH: Reed G. Bender;
Bender Growth Fund

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD

 BODY:
WHAT'S a growth manager to do when the market favors value stocks? Stick to his guns, said Reed G. Bender, lead manager of the $78.7 million Bender Growth fund.
"Managers get in trouble when they try to shift investing styles to take advantage of market momentum," Mr. Bender said from his office in Pasadena, Calif. As for the current value slant of the market, he added: "I don't think that the rotation will last. When the market calms down, it will come back to technology because that's where the growth is." 
 About 50 percent of the fund's holdings are in technology, a relatively steady stake since the fund began in 1996. The fund is part of the Santa Barbara Group of Mutual Funds.
The approach has paid off. The fund's Class C shares, which carry no upfront charges but relatively high expenses, returned 49 percent a year, on average, for the three years through Nov. 21, compared with 21.2 percent for its peers in the midcap growth category, according to Morningstar Inc. Since the Nasdaq peaked on March 10, the fund dropped 25 percent, compared with 27.8 percent for the group.
In trolling for growth among the 10,000 companies whose stocks are traded in the United States, Mr. Bender, 32, receives advice from his father, Robert L. Bender, 63, who owns the investment advisory firm that steers the fund. The list is trimmed to 2,000 by zeroing in on companies that the younger Mr. Bender expects to have revenue and earnings-per-share growth of at least 20 percent a year over the next five to seven years.
Next, he looks for the fastest-growing areas of the economy. His two current themes are corporate outsourcing and Internet infrastructure, including networking, data storage and online security.
"We think the existing network is inadequate for data transmission, and will not scale to the greater speeds that businesses will demand in the future," Mr. Bender said. "Selected companies involved in the buildout have a large opportunity ahead as they replace the network."
He spends the rest of his time looking for the two best companies -- generally, the market leaders in sales and profitability -- in each high-growth industry. Information is gleaned by visiting companies, attending growth-stock conferences and speaking to company managers, competitors and suppliers.
High valuations do not scare him away. "Because most of the industries we look at are growing 40 to 50 percent a year," he said, "we think the market leaders should sell at a premium" on a price-to-earnings basis. Over all, the companies in the portfolio sell for about 40 times projected 12-month earnings, compared with a multiple of 26 for the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index.
He aims to hold stocks for five to seven years, an eternity for many highflying technology issues. Turnover averages a measly 12 percent a year, versus more than 100 percent, on average, for diversified United States stock funds.
The long holding periods give him an edge, he added. "Wall Street has had a hard time valuing companies that grow that quickly for that long," Mr. Bender said, explaining that analysts tend to undervalue potential earning power. "I think that's the case for many high-quality technology companies in the market today."
Even so, he stays away from companies that he says are "priced for perfection," with price-to-earnings multiples that are not justified by their growth. "If earnings per share slip at all in the next couple of years, you'll see the multiple compress significantly," he said.
Mr. Bender may sell a stock when he figures that the company cannot consistently grow 20 percent annually. That may be because of an eroding market for its products, industrywide problems or the company's own deteriorating financial performance.
AMONG Internet infrastructure companies, one of his favorites is Check Point Software Technologies, of Redwood City, Calif., which designs and makes Internet security software. "We think Internet security will become a bigger issue as more companies do business online," he said. He expects the company to grow 50 percent a year in the next three years.
The company is a market leader in firewalls, software that prevents unauthorized computer access, and in virtual private networking, which lets companies connect remote locations securely. Checkpoint is also a bet on the growth of Internet connections like D.S.L. and cable modems, which are more vulnerable because users are always connected to the Internet. The fund bought shares in December 1996 at a split-adjusted price of $5.21. On Friday, they closed at $116.63.
Mr. Bender expects another Internet infrastructure holding, Qualcomm, to have annual growth of 50 percent over three years, driven partly by its proprietary C.D.M.A. technology, for code division multiple access, used by wireless telecommunications companies. "We believe C.D.M.A. will eventually become the dominant standard for transmitting wireless data," he said.
The fund bought Qualcomm stock at a split-adjusted price of $38.86 in August 1999; it is now at $84.50.
Mr. Bender also likes outsourcing investments, like Paychex of Rochester. He figures that the company, which provides payroll services like check-writing and administration, can grow 28 percent a year over the next three years.
"We're in an environment where companies have very little room to raise prices, so they're looking to become more productive by outsourcing," he said. As for Paychex, he added, "they're the leader in the business, and they're using their strong cash flow to cross-sell services to their existing customers." His fund bought shares in January 1997 at a split-adjusted price of $10.67. On Friday, the stock closed at $58.94.
The fund's expenses are relatively high -- 2.98 percent a year for Class C shares that carry no upfront sales charge, or load, but have a 1 percent charge for sales within a year. Class A shares, with an upfront charge of 5.75 percent, have annual expenses of 1.85 percent. Mr. Bender says the fund's expenses are falling as it grows.

HEADLINE: Help Us on Tuition

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Universities have reaped numerous benefits by investing their endowments (news article, Nov. 19). The enormous stock returns of the last few years have allowed the purchase of new equipment, the hiring of faculties and the construction of buildings at schools around the country. At the same time, however, the cost of college tuition has jumped at a rate greater than that of general inflation. 
 Has it ever occurred to these colleges to pass some of their new-found billions directly on to the students they are there to serve? Students appreciate the expensive programs and equipment, but what we really could use is a financial break.
 
LINDSAY ENDERS
Richmond, Ind., Nov. 20, 2000 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: Who Pays the Payroll Tax?

 BODY:

To the Editor:
In "A Retirement Plan That Wall Street Likes" (Economic View, Nov. 12), Louis Uchitelle suggested that an increase in the payroll tax -- now paid by employers and employees in equal amounts -- could help keep Social Security solvent in its current form for 75 years. Employers could pick up most of the increase, he said, if low-wage workers could not afford the extra burden. 
 However, economists across the spectrum believe that most if not all of the employer-paid portion of the payroll tax is shifted backward to labor, in the form of lower wages. So while a tax increase addresses the revenue side of the problem, it does so at the risk of exacerbating rather than redressing distortions and inequities found in the present system.
 
JULIUS CHANG
New York City, Nov. 13 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: Agencies Preparing Now To Fight Rural Wildfires

BYLINE: By The New York Times

DATELINE: BOISE, Idaho

 BODY:
Flush with $1.8 billion in new financing approved by Congress, five federal agencies are making efforts to better protect rural communities from wildfires like the ones that devastated Western states this summer.
"This is the first time in many years, perhaps ever, that the federal firefighting agencies have been fully funded," said Don Smurthwaite, fire information specialist at the National Interagency Fire Center here. 
 The money will allow the five agencies, including the Forest Service, the Bureau of Land Management and the National Park Service, to buy more fire engines, helicopters and tanker planes, and hire more firefighters, smoke jumpers and support workers.
Money will also be used to increase prescribed burning and tree thinning to reduce hazardous fuels around rural communities in the West. Some of the money will be spent on environmental restoration. And local fire departments will be able to apply for federal grants to buy more engines, hoses and radios to improve firefighting efforts in remote areas.
"There were huge concerns in the West last summer when fires were burning really close to communities and we lost a lot of structures," said Jack Sept, chief of external affairs for the Bureau of Land Management at the fire center. "Our people are trained to fight wildland fire and they end up trying to save structures, and the local fire departments are trained to protect structures and they're out there trying to fight wildfire."
Under the new plan, federal fire officials will form partnerships with small communities that are deemed by state fire marshals to be the most threatened by wildfires. Federal authorities will help train local firefighters and help fire districts apply for grants to buy extra equipment.
"There's a large need for those funds," said Brian Shiplett, Idaho's chief of fire management. "Some of those small fire departments have to raise money through a bake sale to buy a new fire hose."
Stan Davis, the mayor of Salmon, Idaho, said he was relieved to see more money for firefighting. "It was a miserable summer," said Mr. Davis, whose community endured 53 days of heavy smoke, with the threat of the nation's largest wildfire, the 195,000-acre Clear Creek blaze, burning in the mountains above town.
"I will feel safer, and the community will feel safer, knowing there will be more resources available for fighting fire," he said. "The economic side of it could be positive, too, if they provide new jobs."
Mr. Sept said the bureau would add 500 firefighters, converting 400 temporary fire-season jobs to full-time year-round jobs, hiring new 20-person crews in Colorado, Idaho, Nevada and Utah, and adding 20 new smoke jumpers at the fire center.
The Forest Service will add more crews as well, but the exact numbers were not available.
New fire crews and equipment will help ease the strain on the nation's firefighting resources during heavy fire seasons, Mr. Sept said. Last summer, all federal fire workers -- about 25,000 people -- were assigned to fires in August, and there were times when new fires erupted but no extra crews were available.

HEADLINE: As Closets Bulge, Americans' Taste in Gifts Often Turns Toward the Taste Buds

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN

DATELINE: TYSON'S CORNER, Va., Nov. 25

 BODY:
Ask the holiday shoppers who are already pouring through the malls that serve prosperity-enriched America what they want for gifts, and many just groan. Their closets, drawers, attics and garages are bursting with the bounty of affluence, they say, and except for the young, they have just one locale left. That is their stomachs.
"What are you going to do?" asked Julie Biggs, 45, a legal secretary who was pausing for coffee at the Tyson's Corner Center mall, the largest serving the nation's capital and its thriving suburbs in Maryland and Northern Virginia. "Not give you presents because you've got too much stuff?" Food might work, she said, adding: "My husband wants to know what I want. I don't know. I've already got a stack of handbags." 
 The economy may finally have slowed, the stock market may have plunged, and more than 11 percent of the population remains mired in poverty. But with wages still rising and nearly all who want to work working, most consumers have met their basic needs and have something extra to spend. In areas like this, many in the largest group of consumers, the baby boomers, have amassed all they can use, so they ask for gifts they can use up, like candles, flowers, lotions and, above all, food.
Danny Doyle, 47, a plumbing supply salesman from Staunton, Va., was sipping a cup of designer coffee at the mall while his wife and 21-year-old daughter browsed through Nordstrom's. "I don't need shirts," he said. "I don't need sweaters. I don't need shoes. Teas, coffee, a bottle of wine, a gift certificate to eat out. Something you can use, get a little enjoyment out of it and get rid of it."
At an adjacent table, Peter Kolar, a retired Foreign Service officer, said he was baffled about what to give and wary of what he would get. "I have a drawer full of wallets," he said. "I have 15 sweaters and two I wear." He likes books, but after looking around the mall's new specialty food stores, he said, "maybe one of those baskets with some cheese and some sausage."
There is no way to measure how much of the nation's food, candy, wine and liquor is bought for gifts, but there is little doubt of a holiday-driven surge in demand. "Many people, if certainly not all, have more disposable income," said David Hodess, president and chief executive of Cooking.com, a 2-year-old Internet company in Santa Monica, Calif., that sells kitchenware and 1,500 food items, including truffle oil and saffron.
"People have a lot of stuff already, and they are looking for an alternative," Mr. Hodess said.
Lori Iventosch-James, economic research director for Harris Interactive, a market research company that conducts the Harris Poll, said that after a rise in Internet food and wine sales last year, "I think we'll see a substantial increase again this year."
To respond, many malls' newest stores, like the 90 that Harry & David, the old fruit-of-the month catalog company of Medford, Ore., has opened in recent years, carry vastly expanded lines of food.
Karen Ponciano, manager of the company's 3-month-old outlet at Tyson's Corner, said her best sellers were five-pound boxes of pears, for $24.95, and boxes of 24 chocolate truffles, for $19.95. "In this area," she said, "there's quite a lot of expendable income and not a lot of price resistance."
Through catalogs, stores and the Internet, 83-year-old Omaha Steaks, long a supplier of nothing but beef, now carries crab cakes, chicken and lamb. More lines have meant more sales, from $180 million in 1997 to $254 million last year, said Sharon Bargas, a company spokeswoman. "We're for those who are hard to buy for, as well as those who have everything," she said.
Many other companies, most identified with other goods, have started or expanded sidelines of food. Most of a wall of the new two-story L.L. Bean outlet at Tyson's Corner is stocked with food, like garlic tortilla chips and blueberry sour cream coffee cake, in addition to old standbys like pancake mix and maple syrup.
Crate and Barrel, the home furnishings chain with outlets at other malls, now sells pesto, apple pie filling, salad dressing and balsamic vinegar. Plow and Hearth, a catalog company in Madison, Va., has introduced a $79.95 mushroom kit, which produces three months of shimeji, shiitake and oyster mushrooms.
Williams-Sonoma, which has long carried a sideline of food with its pots and pans, toasters and blenders, has renamed some of its biggest, newest stores Williams-Sonoma Grand Cuisine. In them, food products occupy as much floor space as the gear used to prepare them.
Crabtree & Evelyn, the British supplier of lotions and soap, has introduced apricot-orange relish and chocolate-covered ginger shortbread. Routinely, said Karen Paone, a supervisor of the company's outlet at Tyson's, customers complain that the people they shop for have all they need. "We always say," she said, " 'Well, have you thought about giving them tea or cookies?' "
The Internet is fanning the trend. Older stores and catalog companies have opened Web sites, joining an explosion of exclusively Internet retailers like Wine.com, Cooking .com, Gourmet Market, Gourmet Resource and Fultonstreet.com. Wine.com in Napa, Calif., packs $20 to $60 holiday baskets of wine with cheese, fruit and stress-relief bath salts.
Chestnut Direct, a catalog and Internet debutante in Essex Junction, Vt., sells fully cooked, irresistible-looking meals under a Home Bistro label, most for prices below those of big-city restaurants.
An 18-ounce meal of New York strip steak with ginger-garlic sauce, sliced red potatoes and carrot puree is $15.95, and a 13-ounce meal of poached salmon fillet with asparagus, spinach fettuccine and a red pepper sauce is $11.95.
Chestnut Direct has staked its prospects on breakthroughs in packaging technology: it ships the meals in vacuum-sealed, flash-frozen packages, wrapped in dry ice, that the customer heats in simmering water. Most customers, said David Thompson, president of Chestnut Direct, order eight meals at a time, and 25 percent of the orders are gifts.
Other retailers have emerged to exploit the market for people who have everything, including the high toll of calories. For $25.95, Blessed Nutrition.com sells a 200-capsule bottle of an antidote called a "super fat burner." For $2.59, or 60 for $143.40, CarbSmart Inc. sells a two-ounce sack of "lean protein sour cream 'n' onion zero carb" chips.
For companies like Williams-Sonoma and Cooking.com, the business of selling food is a natural extension of selling pots and pans to increasingly affluent customers.
"People get more specialized," said Mr. Hodess of Cooking.com. "They might not have a fajita pan. They might have their basic cutlery, but nothing does the job on a tomato like a tomato knife."
At the extreme, there's Neiman Marcus, the upscale apparel chain that has long carried a sideline of holiday snacks. To serve the 250 grams of beluga caviar it sells for $139, it offers a $1,500 "presentoir." This is a 10-inch-diameter silver-plated bowl "supported," according to the catalog, "by a base of graceful sturgeons."

HEADLINE: Ruth Dyk, Champion of Women's Suffrage, Dies at 99

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN

 BODY:
Ruth Belcher Dyk, one of the last surviving participants in the women's suffrage movement, died in her apartment in Rochester on Nov. 18. She was 99.
In a life that included working with delinquent girls, writing three books on psychology and producing anthropological studies of Navajo Indians with her husband, she never lost her fierce commitment to equality. 
 Only the protests of family members prevented her from going door to door in her wheelchair to canvass for Hillary Rodham Clinton's senatorial campaign in New York this fall. "If I have learned one thing, New York needs a woman's touch," she said at rally for Mrs. Clinton on Aug. 22.
"My mother was a suffragette," she said in the opening line of the Ken Burns documentary on the suffragists, "Not for Ourselves Alone: The Story of Elizabeth Cady Stanton and Susan B. Anthony," which was shown last year on the Public Broadcasting System. Her choice of word was surprising, said her daughter, Penelope Carter, of Rochester.
"The word suffragette reminded her of the Rockettes," Ms. Carter said. "She was a suffragist."
Mrs. Dyk's deepest inspiration was her mother, Annie Manson Belcher. She was one of the first women admitted to Tufts Medical School in the late 19th century, but school officials made her drop out when she married, Ms. Carter said. The free spirit of Mrs. Belcher, who by then was married to Arthur Fuller Belcher, a lawyer, is suggested by a family story of borrowing a minister's house for a Maine vacation. When a a house of ill repute next door burned down, Mrs. Belcher graciously invited the prostitutes to stay in the minister's house, engendering a local scandal.
Mrs. Dyk and her mother walked side by side down Beacon Avenue in Boston to demand the vote. Both were overjoyed when the 19th Amendment became part of the Constitution on Aug. 26, 1920.
"We had worked so long," Mrs. Dyk said in the documentary. "If you had worked years to do it, if you believed in it, if you thought great things were going to come out of it -- great in terms of the position of women in our culture, position of women in jobs, position of women everywhere -- and this was going to improve that . . . I mean lives would be changed."
Later in the documentary, she said she feared that women's lives "haven't changed as much as we hoped for." She told friends and family members that if women had voted as a bloc, they could have effected more change. "I don't know if we did enough with it," she said.
Mrs. Clinton, however, lauded her contributions in a statement after her death. "Her devotion to her family, her profession and her work in both the local and global community is an example for women everywhere," the first lady said.
Mrs. Dyk was born in Portland, Me., on March 25, 1901. She was proud to say that her pedigree included several families from the Mayflower, including Myles Standish. She grew up in Newton Center, Mass., and, like her mother, attended Wellesley College in Wellesley, Mass. She earned a master's degree in economics from Simmons College in Boston, and did further graduate work at the University of Wisconsin and the University of California at Berkeley.
She worked as a psychiatric social worker with delinquent girls in upstate New York, where, among other things, she taught her clients to play pool. She worked as a researcher at what was then called the Downstate Medical Center of the State University of New York in Brooklyn, and was co-author of three books: "Anxiety in Pregnancy and Childbirth" (Hoeber, 1950); "Psychological Differentiation" (Wiley, 1962); and "Left Handed" (Columbia University Press, 1980).
She completed the last volume with her husband, Walter Dyk, an anthropologist who studied Navajo Indians. His principal work, called "a revelation of the real life of the tribe" by The New Yorker, was in the form of an autobiography of one man, "Son of Old Man Hat," (Harcourt Brace, 1938).
After his death in 1972, Mrs. Dyk took transcripts of taped conversations and other research and wrote "Left Handed," a continuation of the first book.
Ms. Carter said her mother's strong will was evident when Mr. Dyk was suffering from Parkinson's disease and cancer and she was juggling two jobs and raising two children in the 1950's and 1960's. "What choice did I have? I did what I had to do," she said.
But when she had choices, she took an unconventional approach. "One of my earliest memories was her looking me in the eye and saying with just a bit of venom, 'Don't be so good,' " Ms. Carter said. "She meant you've to have some spirit, go against the grain."
Mrs. Dyk emphasized to her daughter that it was not necessary to marry, and that it was important to get the education that would allow her to live independently. When Ms. Carter began smoking, Mrs. Dyk said it was fine with her. With nothing to rebel against, the girl quit the next day.
In addition to Ms. Carter, Mrs. Dyk is survived by her son, Timothy, of Washington, a judge on the United States Court of Appeals for the Federal Circuit; five grandchildren; and two great-grandchildren.
Mrs. Dyk smoked into her 80's, drove into her 90's and voted in the recent election. She recalled how she felt when she was among the first women to vote.
"I was terribly frightened that I would push the wrong lever," she said. "I still go into that booth with the same feeling -- what if I voted Republican?"

HEADLINE: The Neediest Cases;
A Month's Rent and a Bridge Out of Crisis

BYLINE: By AARON DONOVAN

 BODY:
It was 21 degrees on the morning of Dec. 28, 1999. Pierre Blain, 26, turned on Channel 4 to check the temperature, then put on two pairs of socks and thermal boots to help stave off the cold. He thought he had the flu, but he reported to his job at a Jersey City furniture warehouse anyway because he needed the $67 that he would make that day.
That evening, as Mr. Blain walked to the bus stop to catch a bus home, he collapsed on the frigid sidewalk. 
 Mr. Blain assumes a passer-by stopped to see if he needed help. He remembers only that he woke up briefly at the Jersey City Medical Center, where a doctor told him that he had severe frostbite in his feet, and that both would have to be amputated. When he awoke the next morning, his feet were gone.
Almost a year later, Mr. Blain is virtually confined to his one-bedroom apartment near Prospect Park in Brooklyn. He has only his sister, Careen, 25, and his turtle, Tobias, to help him pass the days.
Mr. Blain is one of thousands of people a year who are helped by The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund, which provides money to seven of New York's oldest and most respected social service agencies. The fund was created in 1912 by Adolph S. Ochs, then publisher of The Times, after he was inspired by a chance encounter with a shabby beggar on Christmas Day 1911.
Mr. Ochs gave the man a few dollars and offered him a job at the newspaper. He was left with a keen awareness of the needy, and wondered if shining a light on the problems of the poor would inspire a city's good will. The next year, he sent a reporter to the city's orphanages and poorhouses to chronicle those most in need, and the Neediest Cases Fund, which today begins its 89th annual appeal for donations, was born.
"Giving to the Neediest Cases Fund is a profound statement at a time when many of us want to support our own institutions, our church, our synagogue, our school," said John Ruskay, chief executive of UJA-Federation of New York, one of the seven local charities the fund supports. "It also says we will support those that are left behind, those who may not be in view, but for whom we have a responsibility."
Mr. Blain depends on $818 a month in Social Security Disability Income, which covers his $735 monthly rent but little else, and on the income his sister gets from working as a baby sitter. But Mr. Blain did not have medical insurance, and the mounting medical costs associated with his legs have caused him to pay his utility bills every other month and lag behind in his rent payments.
"My landlord, he's a businessman," he said. "People have to make their money; I don't hate him for that."
When he was on the verge of being evicted from his apartment, Mr. Blain turned to the Catholic Charities of the Diocese of Brooklyn and Queens for help. Catholic Charities wrote a check for a month's rent and gave him $193.12 for a Con Edison bill. Catholic Charities, one of the seven local charities supported by the Neediest Cases Fund, used money from the fund to pay for those expenses.
"It saved my tail," Mr. Blain said. "I was getting drained. My rent is $735, and I've got the phone bill -- I've got to have a phone. And, I've got to eat."
The use of Neediest Cases Fund money to keep a man from being evicted is an example of what Thomas A. DeStefano, executive director of Catholic Charities of the Diocese of Brooklyn and Queens, calls bridge money -- money that comes to an individual or family at a time of desperate need and helps them back to stability.
"All resources are limited," Mr. DeStefano said. "We use the Neediest Cases money for people who have had the opportunity to be independent, who basically run into a crisis. At other times family is able to step in, but when you work with families who have had limited resources over the years, they're not able to provide that bridge."
Mr. Blain is part of a population of poor people that is expanding despite the continuing economic boom. "The number of New Yorkers living at or within 25 percent of the federal poverty level are growing at an alarming rate," said Dr. Ruskay, who first contributed to the fund by organizing a class donation when he was in grade school in Cedarhurst, N.Y. "Are we going to turn our backs on these people? The Times campaign today, as it has for 89 years, answers with a resounding 'No, they, too, are part of our community and our responsibility.' "
According to a recent study by the Economic Policy Institute in Washington, the poverty rate for New York State increased to 16.6 percent in 1997-98, from 13 percent nine years earlier, while the United States poverty rate remained nearly constant over the same period. The study also says that New York State has one of the greatest disparities of income between the rich and the poor in the country, and the disparity is growing.
The Times does not solicit contributions to the fund; rather, it publishes articles from November to February about those who have been helped through the agencies.
"Poverty, sickness, despair and isolation are still rampant in our city, even if we don't see it in our homes and offices, or on the street," Dr. Ruskay said. "These are New Yorkers who are invisible, but for three months a year, The New York Times holds these New Yorkers in front of us."
Because The Times pays for the fund's administrative costs, every dollar donated goes to those in need.
Catholic Charities continues to work with Mr. Blain to ensure that he is getting the full Medicaid benefits to which he is entitled, and helps him keep to a minimum the number of medical bills he has to pay himself.
"When I was feeling down they said I had to see a shrink," Mr. Blain said. "I said, O.K., but I had to cough up $70."
When Betsy Smith, director of Catholic Charity's Brooklyn Family Center, learned of that, she told him to speak to the social worker who is assigned to his case, Isobella Markorskaya. "Before you pay any of these fees, talk to Isobella about it," Ms. Smith said. "I don't want you paying anything out of pocket."
Mr. Blain said he had applied for health insurance at several companies since the amputations, but had been turned down.
He has a $30,000 pair of prostheses that attach to his legs, which were removed below the knee. The prostheses were largely paid for by the hospital, but he had to contribute more than $5,000 from his own savings for them.
The prostheses allow him to walk for brief periods, but are painful and cause his legs to bleed, Mr. Blain said. He would like a new pair, and expects to hear next month whether Medicaid will help him pay for them.
Ms. Blain used to run a day care center out of her apartment. Now she spends 12 hours a day caring for her brother, and to provide for the two of them, she looks after five children who come to his apartment each day.
"I stay here because he is my brother," said Ms. Blain, who arrives at the apartment between 6 and 7 a.m. each day. "He could get a home health aide, but that's not family."
Family may be the tie that binds, but it is not always the tie that can help a relative pay for sudden, great expenses. Mr. DeStefano said his agency was increasingly depending on Neediest Cases donations because families were finding it increasingly difficult to cope with emergencies like the one Mr. Blain faced.
"As we work with more and more people who have made that jump into independence," Mr. DeStefano said, "we're going to see that more and more individuals will come to us."
