HEADLINE: The State of the City: Packed Like Sardines;
Many See Crowds as Progress; Others Wish Prosperity Hadn't Brought Company

BYLINE: By JANNY SCOTT

 BODY:
Maybe you're thinking the city just seems more crowded but really isn't. Maybe you imagine that it's just the tourists or that you're just cranky or that it's that time of year. Maybe you figure it's just the No. 6 train or the cheese department at Fairway. Or you think New York City has always been like this.
Think again. If New York feels more crowded these days, it's because it is. There are more people living here, more people working here, more people coming to visit. Just try to hail a cab, rent an office, get a phone line, get a child into school, get into a restaurant, drive across town, park, walk a few blocks. 
 It's not just a matter of numbers; affluence intensifies the impression. More people plus more disposable income means many things: more construction, more scaffolding, more renovation, more noise, more double strollers, more car services, more sidewalk cafes, more hot neighborhoods, more delays.
Many New Yorkers now leave a half-hour earlier than they used to just to get to work. They steer clear of Midtown at midday, SoHo on weekends, Main Street in Flushing anytime. They jaywalk madly; they spill into intersections. They see movies at odd times and avoid the fish counter at Zabar's on Saturday mornings. They plan ahead.
Their feelings about it all range from irritation to exhilaration. Many complain about the subways, the traffic, the tourists in Times Square. They compare themselves to termites, cattle, sardines. "I often feel like a bat who's lost its sonar," said Francine Prose, a writer in Greenwich Village, who said she had grown more used to bumping into people.
Chris Lopes, 24, an electrician from Flushing, Queens, said: "I hate it, I hate it, I just hate it. I don't like breathing other people's breath. I like to kiss my wife when I get home, not the guy sitting next to me on the train at 5:30 in the morning. It's disgusting."
Abigail A. Frankfurt, 26, a Columbia University student who lives in Chelsea, said: "Last month they opened a Starbucks on the corner. I was like, 'Oh my God. There goes the neighborhood. O.K., so now we're part of Manhattan's main plan to attract the right kind of people to the little alternative neighborhoods.' And that's what has happened."
Others see the crowds as an index of urban well-being.
"Sure, down in SoHo the streets are always full, especially on weekends," said Jason Epstein, a former editorial director for Random House who lives in Lower Manhattan. "But that's thrilling. You can go for blocks and not hear a word of English. New York has always been like that; it's not like other American cities that tend to be exclusive. It's anything but. So I think it's in the full flower of its cosmopolitanism."
Robert. D. Yaro, executive director of the Regional Plan Association, a civic group that works to improve the economy and quality of life in the New York region, said: "It's a sign of success. We should enjoy it. If you want peace and quiet, move to Scarsdale or someplace. But if you want the real thing, the razzmatazz that only comes with New York, well, here it is."
 
Statistics: More Everything
The statistics are striking. Start with population. The number of people living in Manhattan increased by about 64,000 from 1990 to 1999, said Mark Perry, a demographer with the Census Bureau. In Queens, the population grew by 49,000, and in Staten Island, by about 34,000, while the populations of Brooklyn and the Bronx dropped slightly, Mr. Perry said.
Employment in Manhattan is at at least a 30-year high, said Regina Armstrong, a principal of Urban omics, a group of urban and regional economists based in Manhattan who work with public agencies. Employment in the borough rose by 190,000 jobs from 1993 to 1998 and probably by at least 52,000 jobs from 1998 to 1999, according to federal and state statistics.
Tourism is breaking all records. The number of tourists and other visitors increased to 36.6 million in 1999 from 22.8 million in 1991, according to NYC & Company, the convention and visitors bureau. The number of passengers using the region's three major airports rose by 11.7 million from 1994 to 1999, according to the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey.
Subway and bus ridership are way up. From 1992 to 1999, the average weekday subway ridership rose by 867,000 and bus ridership by 476,000, according to New York City Transit. "We're carrying a million more people today on our subways and buses than we were carrying in 1997," said Al O'Leary, a transit spokesman.
Traffic, too, has worsened. The City Department of Transportation found that the number of vehicles entering and leaving Manhattan daily increased to 1.93 million in 1999 from 1.76 million in 1990. The Port Authority said that traffic on its two tunnels and four bridges heading into New York from New Jersey increased to 123.6 million vehicles in 1999 from 112.3 million in 1990.
The number of cars registered in the city rose by nearly 97,000 from 1996 to 1999, including an increase of 16,000 cars registered in Manhattan, State Department of Motor Vehicles statistics show. In the early 1990's, the number of Manhattan registrations changed relatively little, but it leaped by nearly 8,000 from 1998 to 1999 alone.
And it's not just cars that New Yorkers are buying. The Department of City Planning approved the construction of 12,421 housing units in 1999, up from 5,135 in 1995. In Manhattan, the number rose to 3,791 from 1,129. The number of permits issued by the Department of Buildings for scaffolding, sidewalk sheds and improvements has risen by several thousand a year over the last few years.
Restaurant seats in the city are up by 17,000 since 1996, said E. Charles Hunt, executive vice president of the Greater New York Chapter of the New York State Restaurant Association. Sidewalk cafe permits issued by the City Department of Consumer Affairs rose to 573 in 2000 from 318 in 1996.
One number has barely changed: the number of medallion yellow cabs, which is set by the city government. It rose by 400 from 1996 to 1997, to 12,187, and has remained fixed since. A recent study by Bruce Schaller, a consultant on taxicab regulatory issues, concluded that a record 241 million passengers rode in yellow cabs in 1999 and that taxis had become harder to hail on the streets.
"It's this combination of population growth, employment growth and income growth which is really fueling this hyperactive sense that we're getting about New York today," said Ms. Armstrong of Urbanomics. She said the personal income of Manhattan residents had risen significantly over the last eight years. "People not only want to be here and live here and work here. They're spending their money here.
"It's the kind of activity we haven't experienced for probably well over 30 years," Ms. Armstrong said. "It reminds me of what New York was like in the late 60's, but in some cases it's even more intense today than then. You can see it in the price increases in housing, for instance. You can see it in the sheer numbers of people out there walking around."
And it is about to get worse. The weeks between Thanksgiving and New Year's Day are the busiest of the year.
 
Gridlock as a Way of Life
One morning recently, a river of commuters cascaded down the Lexington Avenue sidewalks, then merged in a stubborn bottleneck at the 86th Street subway station, which serves some of the system's most crowded lines. Tramping and shuffling, the crowd descended into a roiling pit of bodies and surged onto the platforms where trains were arriving, already stuffed.
And no one comes empty-handed. New Yorkers travel with briefcases, backpacks, laptop computers, gym bags, duffel bags, garment bags, diaper bags, strollers -- freight that the transit system's designers never anticipated when the system was conceived, suggests Tony Hiss, who wrote "The Experience of Place" (Vintage Books, 1991) and is writing a book on transportation.
Nancy C. Jones, a computer operator who commutes from East Flatbush, Brooklyn, to Midtown, said: "I just literally want to scream, 'Don't touch me! Everybody, just let me sit or stand in enough human space not to be mauled for an hour.' All joking aside, part of the reason why the city is so hostile is because you're treated like an animal twice a day."
At lunch hour in Midtown recently, a half-dozen delivery trucks were double-parked on West 46th Street between Seventh Avenue and Avenue of the Americas. Traffic was at a standstill the length of the block. On Fifth Avenue, the width of the sidewalk near West 49th Street had been halved by a cordoned-off cluster of ladders and workers repairing a storefront.
Crowds were massing near the curb, waiting to cross Fifth Avenue, then sallying forth as the light changed only to pull up short halfway across, confronted by an oncoming wall of more people. Near the New York Public Library at Fifth Avenue and 42nd Street, an ambulance idled in traffic, siren blaring, for three minutes before finally inching forward.
"You remember the night that all the international heads of state were in town and there was that dinner at the Metropolitan and Clinton was the host?" the writer Tom Wolfe asked recently. "The town was absolutely tied up. Nothing moved. And I walked 41 blocks to dinner. I said, 'Well, this is an exceptional occasion, of course.' But it's been that way ever since."
In midafternoon in Flushing recently at the intersection of Main Street and Roosevelt Avenue, buses lined the curbs in every direction. Heading south on Main Street, men, women and children maneuvered past tables laden with fruit, battery-operated barking dogs, Pokemon clocks, miniature boom boxes, toothpaste, socks, live crabs.
"You have to literally buckle your shoulders and play rough tackle and knock them down just to come down the block," said Tosha Bright, 24, a customer service representative from Bedford-Stuyvesant, Brooklyn, who works in Flushing. "They'll stop in front of you and hold a conversation. People run you over with shopping carts, strollers. It's crazy over here. I'm like, Brooklyn ain't got nothing on these people out here."
 
Nostalgic for Rougher Times
One evening recently, Krin and Paula Gabbard headed to a fashionable downtown restaurant with a reservation for an unfashionable Tuesday at 8 p.m. -- made after they gave up trying to go out on weekends because every restaurant, movie and jazz club always seemed to be booked.
When they arrived, the restaurant was packed and their table in use. They were offered drinks on the house, but bodies were three deep around the bar. They were finally seated 70 minutes late (and found a $175 bottle of wine on the table, a consolation prize from management).
"I long for the days when people thought of New York as a place that was dangerous," said Mr. Gabbard, a professor of comparative literature at the State University of New York at Stony Brook, who lives in Morningside Heights, Manhattan. "Because then I'd be able to get a table more easily."
There are, of course, cities like Hong Kong that are more densely populated than New York. And there are faster-growing cities, and subway systems that carry more people in fewer hours. There are even neighborhoods in New York City that have thinned out lately, and there are places, like the Lower East Side, that were once much more densely populated than they are now.
But it has been many years since contemporary New Yorkers experienced crowding in the city at current levels. Many were not here in the late 1960's when sidewalk traffic became so acute that the Regional Plan Association recommended widening the sidewalks of Lexington and Fifth Avenues and turning Madison Avenue into a pedestrian mall.
Later, recession and the crime rate began to drain the city. Like many who moved to New York in the 1970's, Luc Sante, a journalist and an author, feels a certain affection for those years when he could decide that $100 a month was too much to pay for an apartment on the Lower East Side and could make up his mind at the last minute to go out to a concert or a movie.
 
A Sense of Something Lost
Thomas Beller, a writer who grew up on the Upper West Side and recently started a Web site that collects stories about Manhattan (www.mrbellersneighborhood.com), remembers many "neighborhoods that were sort of legendarily barren and haunted -- strange places of ghostly refuge where you felt the city was some kind of abandoned monster."
"Density is the drug that has always kind of captivated people about New York," Mr. Beller said. Not simply density, he said, but "these high-pressure, low-pressure weather changes in the density levels. It's the contrast that you thrive on. You can get into those different weather systems of energy really fast." Those contrasts are still there, he said, but they are less pronounced.
Parks were other low-pressure zones. Now some of the best-known, like Prospect Park or Union Square, have been restored and are thronged with people. In the words of Fred Kent, president of the Project for Public Spaces, a nonprofit planning and design organization, Bryant Park has gone from "nine groups of people dealing drugs" to "3,000 people having a ball." Central Park draws some 20 million visits a year, the City Parks Department said.
Emily Lloyd, a former New York City sanitation commissioner and former Boston traffic and parking commissioner, said: "We are making our streets and squares and external spaces more inviting, investing more in them as places where we want to spend time. Which I think is a wonderful change. Because the street is where we learn to live with each other, where we learn to get along, where we really get comfortable with our fellow inhabitants of the city."
Or not.
Marshall Blonsky, a New York University professor who teaches semiotics, described a visit to Citarella on Broadway at 75th Street: "Getting out of that awful subway, fighting my way across the street, walking past the book hawkers, past the pet-givers-away, past the doormen-you-don't-want-to-be-doormen holding your bank door open."
And inside the market, "beautiful people in jogging outfits buying Cornish hens and pointing to lobsters." Certain urban settings, Mr. Blonsky said, "send the message, 'Come out and show off your wealth.' In the form of a tanned face in February or a huge mastiff that clearly you don't have room for in your apartment but that you want to show off."
 
Many Ideas for Solutions
While the city has succeeded in recent years in making itself more attractive, critics say it has done little to accommodate the superabundance of humans that results. They would like to see sidewalks widened, street space reclaimed, traffic limited, transit capacity increased, delivery schedules changed, high-rise buildings reconceived.
Many want sidewalks widened along critical stretches of Fifth and Lexington Avenues and in Times Square, where one such widening is planned. Some want the street signaling system modified to take into account pedestrians' needs, and certain curbside parking spaces eliminated so hot dog vendors can move off sidewalks into designated spots by the curb.
To cut down on cars, some transportation experts want tolls on the East River bridges, limited access to Midtown and the closing of certain streets to traffic. Many also suggest congestion pricing for parking, under which vehicles would be equipped with a device like an E-ZPass transponder that could be scanned so that drivers could be billed based on where and when they parked.
In addition, many say subway capacity must be expanded with new and extended lines, stations should be made less oppressive, and bus lanes should be cleared to keep the vehicles for which they were intended moving. Elevators in certain new high-rise buildings, some say, should be required to connect to the subway so employees could come and go without taking up limited sidewalk space.
But will it happen?
"You can find someone to object to almost any of these things," said Richard T. Anderson, president of the New York Building Congress, a coalition of the design, construction and real estate industries. "And there's no organized constituency for the pedestrians who are being crowded out."
For some, what has been lost, for now, is a feeling. Krin Gabbard, the professor from Morningside Heights, remembers how the city felt to him when he arrived in 1981. He could wander the streets, a flaneur, observing the place with a degree of freedom and detachment that is impossible when one is preoccupied with negotiating traffic.
One thing he remembers were the construction workers' catcalls to passing women, something he had once thought vulgar, but that at that moment struck him as executed with rare panache. That state of mind is denied the pedestrian on a packed street. Mr. Gabbard said recently, "I feel as if, sometimes, the crowds have taken away some of the romance."

HEADLINE: In Philippines, The President Could Evade An Ouster Yet

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS

DATELINE: BANGKOK, Nov. 25

 BODY:
President Joseph Estrada of the Philippines may not be a political scientist, but as a former movie hearthtrob he knows his scripts.
"It's just like in the movies," he said recently as his allies turned against him and protesters shouted in the streets for his resignation. "The hero gets beaten up in the beginning but still wins in the end." 
 Right now, Mr. Estrada is at the pummeling part. But his final defeat seems a little less certain now than it did at the start of the month.
Two weeks ago he became the first Philippine president impeached by the House of Representatives, and the Senate is soon to begin a trial that could result in his removal.
The charges against him involve a staple of Philippine politics: corruption. Mr. Estrada has been accused by one of his cronies of accepting nearly $11 million in illegal gambling profits and provincial tobacco taxes.
He denies the charges, but when asked about them in detail at a recent forum, he seemed flustered and uncertain. His accusers say they are ready to present the Senate with a mountain of evidence.
Political winds in the Philippines blow with particular force, and in recent weeks Mr. Estrada has been deserted by leading allies in Congress, by members of his cabinet and by the powerful business sector. Vice President Gloria Macapagal Arroyo, who is not a member of his party, resigned from the cabinet -- though not from the vice presidency -- and has become one of his most vocal opponents.
The chief figures in the "people power" movement that brought down former President Ferdinand E. Marcos have also come together again, and the powerful Roman Catholic Church has turned against him. "He is really losing ground, objectively speaking," said Amando Doronila, a political commentator.
But Mr. Estrada has not caved in as his opponents had hoped, and political experts say the battle could last for weeks or even months.
The 22-member Senate needs a two-thirds majority to remove him, and as of now Mr. Estrada appears to have just barely enough votes to hold on to office. His lawyers are raising legal obstacles, including a challenge to the impeachment itself, which passed in the House of Representatives without a formal vote. Time is on their side.
Congress will break for three weeks at Christmas and then for four months in February as the Philippines prepares for midterm elections in May. Experts say that if the impeachment motion has not been voted on before the long recess, it must be refiled one year after its initial filing, in October.
Such a scenario is not an appealing one to many Filipinos and appears to be particularly discouraging to the business sector.
There are also concerns that if the Senate vote is delayed -- or if the vote fails to remove him -- the street protests could grow, further hampering the country's political and economic life. "The number of people in the streets is bound to grow," Mr. Doronila said, "and that is not something to be dismissed lightly."

HEADLINE: Russia Backs European Plan For New Force

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: BERLIN, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Russia is ready to cooperate with the new military force being drawn up by the European Union, Foreign Minister Igor Ivanov said today.
"We consider it completely natural, the effort by Europe with their own forces to provide for their own security," Mr. Ivanov told leading European policy makers and analysts at a forum on Europe. "And in a crisis situation, we are ready for constructive cooperation." 
 European countries announced this week that they would form their own rapid reaction force outside NATO, which depends heavily on American military might and formed the West's primary cold war buffer against the former Soviet Union.
The 15 member countries of the European Union said they would create a force of up to 60,000 ground troops from among member armies by 2003 to deal with regional conflicts and humanitarian crises.
"The possibility of a Russian contribution in the conduct of European Union operations in regulating crises will be studied," Mr. Ivanov said. "I am sure that this will open good possibilities for our joint contribution to strengthening stability and security in Europe."
Mr. Ivanov's remarks could raise American fears of losing influence in European peacekeeping operations, and his remarks sought to highlight cases where American and European interests have diverged.
For example, Mr. Ivanov mentioned Europe's differences with Washington over the possible development by the United States of a national defense against ballistic missiles. "We very much appreciate that a whole series of leading European governments have come out with us and the overwhelming majority of the world in defense of strategic stability," he said. "One would like to believe that the series of supporters on our continent for the preservation of the ABM treaty will expand," he added, referring to the 1972 Antiballistic Missile Treaty between Moscow and Washington that does not currently permit deployment of the kind of system the American military envisages.
Warning that a failure to follow international norms would lead to chaos, Mr. Ivanov cited the 1999 American-led NATO air war against Yugoslavia as an example of NATO gone astray.
"Unfortunately, the well-known events in the Balkans in the spring of 1999 are evidence that such an alternative cannot be excluded," he said. "After the NATO bombing of Yugoslavia, there was again talk in the world that maintaining security was only possible by military means, including through the acquisition of weapons of mass destruction.
"I am sure that such a negative development of events is not in the interests of a single European government."
A day after President Vladimir V. Putin of Russia received the Palestinian leader, Yasir Arafat, in Moscow, Mr. Ivanov also called for the European Union to be more involved in Middle East peace talks.
"Russia, as a cosponsor of the Middle East peace process, believes that the European Union should take a more active role in its international mediation," he said. "The very proximity and approaches on these questions between Moscow and Brussels allows us to act in tandem and if necessary, in a joint effort."

HEADLINE: The Tree Trap

BYLINE: By ANDREW C. REVKIN

DATELINE: THE HAGUE, Nov. 25

 BODY:
In the end, the negotiators got lost in the trees.
After 11 days of draining and unwieldy bargaining by 170 countries over the rules for a proposed treaty to fight global warming, by this morning all the issues had been narrowed to just this one: How much credit should big forested countries get for all that photosynthesis? 
 In that natural chemical process, trees and other plants draw carbon dioxide, the main greenhouse gas, out of the air and stash the carbon in the ground or in wood, forming what experts have called carbon "sinks" and helping to cool the climate.
The United States, the world's largest producer of greenhouse gases and the country that potentially stands to face the greatest cost under any treaty, had originally said it might try to meet half its emission-cutting goal in just that way.
But other industrialized nations with fewer open spaces suspected the Americans of trying to find a way out of actually taking the hard steps of reducing their use of fossil fuels and of trying to get something for nothing -- in effect, playing a "get out of jail free" card.
Despite a report issued during the conference from the Energy Department saying that the United States could reduce its fuel use though fairly simple, inexpensive changes -- with almost no harm to the economy -- conservation measures have long been resisted in a country where big cars, low gasoline prices and economic growth have come to be considered nearly inalienable rights.
But for Europeans, accustomed to high fuel taxes and Green politics, the primary goal of the treaty was to cut emissions at the source, not sop them up after the fact.
Through the final week of the conference, the United States sharply whittled down its original proposals. By dawn today, everyone later agreed, it was very close. There was a palpable sense of optimism that -- after a decade of debate -- the world was ready to take a step toward a cooperative, but potentially costly, effort to cut the flow of gases that scientists have linked to the warming climate.
Through the night and early this morning, environmental groups, particularly the World Wildlife Fund, helped the European delegation dissect each complicated new formula for carbon tons and trees, said Kevin R. Gurney, a climate and forest expert at Colorado State University who spent hours crunching sets of numbers provided by European negotiators to the wildlife group -- one after another.
He said the final analysis came down to a 20-million-ton difference between the two sides, a minuscule amount of carbon dioxide in a world spewing 6 billion tons a year into the air.
"I think things came as close as they could come," he said. "I think they were tired and not able to translate some of that into the bottom line, and I think that did freak some of them out."
One result was that a pioneering climate deal sealed with handshakes by a few diplomats deep in the night came unglued when numbers lay under the fluorescent glare of the day.
Tonight, as he somewhat wistfully recalled that fleeting electric moment when an agreement was at hand, Jan Pronk, the president of the two-week climate meeting, compared it to the American presidential election. "At 6 o'clock this morning," he said, "it was too close to call."
People who were intimately involved with that critical moment when the tide turned say it appears that domestic political pressures, exhaustion, an exceptionally tight relationship between the Europeans and environmental groups and simply too much data to sort through in too little time all collided to destroy any environmental detente, at least for now.
In his post-game analysis tonight, Mr. Pronk said he lamented that fundamental problem with technical negotiations.
"When experts come to the table, they tend to stay too long," he said. "People leave with more questions than they had before."
On other heated issues, resolution seemed closer still. Delegates had fought over whether nuclear plants should be banned or simply not encouraged in a world combatting global warming. The technology produced no greenhouse gases, after all, but came with a load of other environmental concerns.
The compromise was the gentle verb "refrain."
There was a third bloc, the developing countries, that had its own demands, but those seemed achievable if the wealthy nations could speak with one voice, and in the end they could not.
Mr. Pronk said tonight he was disappointed, but not devastated. "I'm a professional, and I'm a believer," he said, "a professional believer."
But even as he spoke, workers dismantled the trappings of the conference, including blue and green panels bearing the logo he himself had chosen: "Work It Out."

HEADLINE: Irish Unions, Pay Lagging, Stage Protests

BYLINE: By BRIAN LAVERY

DATELINE: DUBLIN, Nov. 25

 BODY:
On Wednesday, trains across Ireland sat still in their stations. On Thursday, high school classrooms were empty. And this weekend, newsrooms are eerily quiet. After more than a decade of cordial industrial relations, Irish labor unions have returned to the picket lines with a series of intermittent one-day strikes by workers on the railroads and in hospitals, by airline pilots and journalists.
In October last year, 30,000 nurses staged a drawn-out strike. Over the summer, train drivers carried out a work stoppage. But the sporadic unrest orchestrated this month by 16,000 high school teachers is considered by some experts to pose the most serious threat so far to Ireland's 13-year-old "social partnership" model of industrial relations. 
 "We're going through a difficult period," said Tom Wall, assistant general secretary of the Irish Congress of Trade Unions, an umbrella group to which the teachers belong. "We're in transition in the protected areas of the economy."
The teachers' union, the Association of Secondary Teachers, Ireland, is one of more than 100 labor and employers' organizations that signed a national agreement called the Program for Prosperity and Fairness, which sets an annual level of wage increases for three years. Now the teachers' union is threatening that entire structure, joining a growing number of unions that warn that they will pull out of the agreement if demands for more pay are not met.
The unions contend that rising prices -- Ireland's inflation rate is currently nearly 7 percent, more than double the European average -- have made the 5.5 percent wage increases meaningless. The government insists that the terms of the agreement are not negotiable. "As usual, they keep their head down and hope it goes away," said Martin Frawley, an editor at the trade magazine Industrial Relations News.
The social partnership process dates to the economically stagnant Ireland of 1987, when the aptly named Program for National Recovery guaranteed gradual and controlled wage increases. By sticking to the periodic agreements, organized labor earned significant credit for Ireland's transformation into the fastest-growing economy in Europe, with gross domestic product expected to grow by 10.5 percent this year.
But while social partnership helped to maintain stability during the difficult times, it has outstayed its welcome, said Dermot O'Brien, chief economist at NCB stockbrokers in Dublin. He uses language similar to that of many Irish officials anxious to privatize old state companies and introduce full competition. "We're getting in the way of market forces," he said. "The supply-and-demand balance has shifted."
Thanks to the influence of American corporations like Dell Computer and Intel, the balance of jobs has shifted toward flexible and highly paid private sector jobs.
The Irish work force is almost 1.7 million people, of which the umbrella union group claims a membership of 500,000. Its share of the work force is falling because most new jobs are in nonunionized areas, like financial services, retail trade and hotels.
Now that the labor movement has become more focused on the public sector, the wage agreements help union members catch up with employees in the higher-income businesses associated with Ireland's economic turnaround. "A lot of employers, particularly in the high-tech sector, are throwing money at recruitment," Mr. Frawley said. "There's a wave of rising expectations that aren't grounded in reality, but it's the public perception that matters."
The young Irish men and women taking up those jobs are not conscious of the sacrifices made by the generation before them, Mr. Wall contends. "There's a lack of appreciation of what they call 'the grim yardstick of the past,' " he said. With an aging membership in industries that are not growing, unions face an uphill battle to keep their current high public profile.
The high school teachers, who resume their strike for two days next week, are in the slightly awkward position of battling an agreement that, while they consider it financially unfair, nonetheless helps maintain their status as an important minority in the work force. "The social partnership process has kept unions near the seat of power," Mr. O'Brien said. "If we didn't have the national plans, the unions wouldn't have nearly the same influence."

HEADLINE: One Battle More -- the Last? -- for Canada's Old Warrior

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE

DATELINE: TORONTO Nov. 25

 BODY:
Canada's leader, Jean Chretien, used to boast that he entered Parliament when John F. Kennedy was in the White House. But that boast has faded now that he is locked in a fight for political survival with Stockwell Day, a smooth-faced opponent who looks half the age of the craggy 66-year-old prime minister.
With Canadians to vote on Monday in federal elections, Mr. Chretien desperately wants to go down in history as the first prime minister since World War II to form three consecutive majority governments. In a high-stakes gamble, he called elections last month, ignoring the advice of Liberal Party leaders. If the Liberals fare poorly on Monday, they will probably depose him, ending a decade of his party leadership and seven years as prime minister. 
 What the press has billed as "Chretien's last campaign" is stumbling to an inglorious end, far from the sunny days of early fall when he surfed on a wave of national nostalgia after the death of Pierre Trudeau. Polls then gave the Liberals a 30 percentage point advantage over the Canadian Alliance, the new conservative party of Mr. Day.
With the gap narrowing to about 12 points, Mr. Chretien has been reduced to hinting that if he wins a majority government, he will hand over power to a younger Liberal leader halfway through his five-year term. Swallowing his pride, he started to campaign jointly with Finance Minister Paul Martin, his younger and more popular Liberal rival. Bowing to polls indicating that he is less popular than his party, Mr. Chretien offered "a great team" and reminded them that "government is not a one-man show."
With many Canadians telling pollsters that they see no reason to hold an election now, Joe Clark, leader of the Progressive Conservatives, snapped at Mr. Chretien in a television debate, "You're a prime minister without a plan, except to stay in office."
But Mr. Clark wrote Mr. Chretien off as "yesterday's man" once before -- on the eve of the 1993 election that swept the Liberal Party to power. In the last four decades, few politicians have prospered by underestimating the wiles and combativeness of Mr. Chetien, who came into the world at the height of the Depression as the 18th child of a Quebec millworker, a baby born with a deaf right ear and a paralysis that distorted his mouth.
Proud of his blue-collar roots in Shawinigan, an industrial town 50 miles northeast of Montreal, Mr. Chretien recently complained of the isolation of office, saying: "You don't have a lot of time to go into a beer parlor and have a beer with the boys. This is unfortunately over."
Like many Quebecois who support a unified Canada, Mr. Chretien is reviled by the separatist Bloc Quebecois party as a traitor to Quebec. Gilles Duceppe, the Bloc leader, who believes he will gain seats from the Liberals on Monday, routinely dismisses his French Canadian rival as "the little guy from Ottawa who disguises himself as the little guy from Shawinigan every four years."
A political mechanic, Mr. Chretien worked his way up under three Liberal prime ministers, speaking fractured French and English, but displaying flawless electoral instincts. As prime minister, he governs in an autocratic way that, critics say, reduces the role of his cabinet to that of a focus group.
"He decided this election all alone," said Thomas S. Axworthy, a Chretien supporter who teaches public policy part time at Harvard. "He was afraid of the economy turning down. He did not want to give the Alliance time to grow."
Much as Al Gore often did not get credit for the boom years of the Clinton administration, much of the credit for Canada's economic recovery go to the finance minister, Mr. Martin, a 62-year-old millionaire from Montreal. The difference between the two rivals is the difference between a patriarch and a patrician.
"The crowd surges toward the figure whose gray hair catches the television lights and seems to draw the adoring like a magnet," the newspaper The Globe and Mail said last week in an effusive front-page article headlined, "Crowds flock to Martin."
Inheriting huge budget deficits in 1993, the Chretien administration radically reversed Canada's fiscal fortunes. This year will be the third consecutive year of budget surpluses, a feat not seen here since the 1950's. Unemployment, while high by American standards, has dropped from 9 percent in the early 1990's to 6.9 percent today, the lowest level in a quarter century.
A few days before the election, the latest surplus allowed Mr. Martin to announce a $65 billion tax cut, spread over five years. But conservatives question whether Mr. Chretien, an apostle of government activism, has his heart in tax cuts. Mr. Day, who instituted a flat provincial income tax as treasurer of Alberta, warned, "Canadians have a right to be skeptical about a Liberal government promising tax cuts."
Almost playing to this distrust, Mr. Chretien unveiled a new $4 billion spending program this month, telling voters, "There is more to life than taxes and taxpayers."
Trying to defuse critics on the left, he signed a deal in September with the 10 provincial leaders to restore $14.3 billion on the national health care system.
In a candidates' television debate, Alexa McDonough, leader of the New Democratic Party, a socialist party, said of the prime minister's campaign-season turnabout on health care spending, "Thirty-seven days of Dr. Jekyll don't make up for seven years of Mr. Hyde." Health care routinely tops voter worry lists.
Although Mr. Chretien campaigned in 1993 against free trade with the United States, he quietly jettisoned that policy, allowing bilateral trade to nearly double, to $1 billion a day. In September, the United States monthly trade deficit with Canada, its largest trading partner, hit a record $4.7 billion.
Dragged forward by the American economic locomotive, Canada's economy grew by 4.5 percent last year. Still, Canada's huge national debt, equaling 365 billion American dollars, remains a political liability.
On the campaign trail, Mr. Day routinely picks up a wailing baby and says: "See? He knows that when he was born his share of the national debt was $18,000."
But Mr. Chretien remains a firm believer in patronage and economic interventionism, and he has announced multimillion-dollar grants to build bridges in Montreal and the creation of a state-run Internet portal.
Mr. Day has painted the slogan "A Time for a Change" on the side of his campaign bus. Alluding to his adversary's age, he often extends such backhanded compliments as, "I respect the 37 years of service that the prime minister has given."
Although Mr. Chretien recently said "there is no more room on my political body for any more scars," his aging political physique seems to have picked up a few more in recent weeks with allegations of corruption.
The Royal Canadian Mounted Police are conducting five inquiries into possible misuses of government job creation grants in Shawinigan, which Mr. Chretien flooded with federal grants and loans after it voted 55 percent in favor of Quebec independence in a 1995 referendum.
Last week, the prime minister acknowledged that he had repeatedly telephoned the president of a federal bank, the Business Development Bank of Canada, in an ultimately successful lobbying effort for a $400,000 loan to a man who had bought a Shawinigan hotel partly owned by Mr. Chretien.
"You call who you know," said Mr. Chretien, who depicted the lobbying effort as fighting for his constituents. Although The Vancouver Sun, British Columbia's largest newspaper, called for the prime minister's resignation, he counterattacked with a report clearing him, prepared by Howard Wilson, the parliamentary ethics counselor.
Conservative critics noted that Mr. Wilson was appointed by Mr. Chretien and reports to him. A Day spokesman, Phil von Finckenstein, said, "We need an ethics watchdog, not an ethics lapdog."
The charges have only added to the wariness of an already tentative campaign. In contrast to Mr. Day, who has run from one public rally to the next, Mr. Chretien has had events before safe audiences -- at local campaign headquarters or at factories, research institutes and job training centers that have received federal grants and subsidies.
But Margaret Wente, a columnist for The Globe and Mail, was not persuaded that even Mr. Chretien's waning personal appeal would be decisive in affecting the outcome of the election.
"So what if the leader can no longer fill a pool hall with people who actually like him?" she wrote last week. "He'll survive. The street-fighting instinct's still working."

LENGTH: 1316 words

HEADLINE: TREATY TALKS FAIL TO FIND CONSENSUS IN GLOBAL WARMING

BYLINE: By ANDREW C. REVKIN

DATELINE: THE HAGUE, Nov. 25

 BODY:
High-stakes negotiations aimed at finishing a treaty to curb global warming collapsed today after a tense all-night bargaining session foundered on last-minute disputes between European and American negotiators.
The breakdown, after two weeks of intensive talks here, stunned many participants, environmental groups and observers, even though they had recognized from the start the enormous task of finding common ground on ways to cut the greenhouse gases emitted by every smokestack and tailpipe from Boston to Brisbane. 
 "I'm gutted," John Prescott, Britain's deputy prime minister, said as he left the hall this morning after failing to negotiate a compromise primarily between the United States -- by far the world's largest producer of greenhouse gases -- and the European Union.
But Jan Pronk, the conference president and Dutch environment minister, did not concede defeat, and instead proposed that the meeting be suspended, with another session perhaps as soon as May.
The top American negotiator, Frank E. Loy, visibly tired and rubbing his forehead and eyes, agreed that the effort should continue, even when he and the Clinton administration would be handing over the task to a new administration.
"We will not give up," Mr. Loy said. "The stakes are too high, the science too decisive, and our planet and our children too precious."
From the outset the talks were riven by conflicting agendas as they aimed to fill in the fine print of a 1997 treaty, called the Kyoto Protocol, drafted by more than 170 countries.
Poor countries sought billions of dollars to help them adapt to climate change, while rich nations aimed to blunt the economic impact of the treaty by finding the least costly ways to cut their emissions of warming gases.
But today the failure came down to persistent disagreement between industrial powers on opposite sides of the Atlantic over the role of trees and properly managed farmland in acting as "sinks" to absorb carbon dioxide, the dominant greenhouse gas.
The lack of understanding on that issue was a key to the breakdown of the talks, participants on both sides said. European negotiators ultimately rejected a compromise proposal -- which early this morning seemed to have sealed a deal -- as too harmful to the environment and too favorable to the United States.
If enacted, the treaty the negotiators had hoped to complete would commit three dozen industrialized countries to cut their greenhouse gas emissions by 2012 to at least 5 percent below emissions in 1990.
So far, however, no industrialized country has ratified the pact, and as negotiations have dragged on in the intervening years, the emissions of most of the world's leading producers of greenhouse gases have only continued to rise, a trend that helped add new urgency to the current round of talks.
When the treaty was originally drafted in Japan three years ago, the United States, Canada and other large countries said they would seek credit toward their emissions targets for forested areas, but those talks never settled on an amount. Negotiators at the current conference had hoped to arrive at one.
But ultimately no agreement could be reached. Around 3 a.m., Mr. Prescott, from Britain, and representatives of two other European nations met with the United States team over a compromise proposal. Members of both delegations said agreement was reached in that room.
"We physically shooks hands," Mr. Loy said. "I asked, 'Are we now in full agreement, is this a deal?' I was pointing to a piece of paper. They said, 'Yes.' "
But when that set of proposals was brought back to the rest of the European delegation, they rejected it.
"We had a hard and fast political deal," said a member of the British delegation. But that deal could not hold up to the subsequent analysis before dawn by other European countries.
"I think it is fair to say that was a pretty important opportunity that was not cashed in on," Mr. Loy said.
Moments after word escaped that the agreement had broken down, representatives of competing private environmental groups sprinted around the halls, decrying the failure and saying simply, "It's over." They then scrambled to hand out hastily drafted press releases and to hold briefings for the media shouting to be heard over the din and confusion.
Many environmental groups argued that the United States had underestimated the strength of the European Green movement and its determination to reduce the use of fossil fuels drastically. A British official said that many European countries realized the significance of the idea of carbon "sinks" only during the current meeting -- and that it was far too late.
Jennifer Morgan, climate campaign director for the World Wildlife Fund, which joined a mostly European cluster of environmental groups, blamed the United States.
"The United States pushed too hard and too far," she said. "They didn't leave the time or trust to get a deal in the end."
Part of the problem was a cultural rift, negotiators on both sides said. The European Union, where Green Party politics is a driving force, never found a way to compromise with the United States, where the environmental movement increasingly works with industries to bring change, instead of fighting for strict top-down regulations.
"It's extremely difficult to negotiate between groups where political cultures are so different," Dominique Voynet, the French environment minister and a Green Party member, told the full sessions.
Earlier in the week, Jurgen Trittin, the German environment minister, explained that the opposition to forest credits in his country was deeply rooted, and that there was a clear sense at the conference that the United States and its partners were trying to get something for nothing.
"We have strong interest groups in German society," he said. "What shall I tell them if the United States makes a fire road in a forest and flies airplanes over it and says that is an emissions project? They'd say you're ridiculous."
But some environmental groups that tend to work more closely with industry defended the American position, which focused on building a political consensus back in the United States by making sure that the agreement satisfied powerful industry and farming lobbies.
"In the long-term fight against global warming, we need every tool at our disposal," said Eileen Claussen, president of the Pew Center on Global Climate Change, a Washington group. Ms. Claussen was formerly part of a Clinton administration team that negotiated agreements leading up to the Kyoto Protocol.
"If we take carbon sequestration and market mechanisms out of the equation, or bog them down with such overly restrictive rules that nobody uses them," she said, "then we are limiting our ability to meet our environmental objectives."
Sani Daura, Nigeria's environmental minister and a spokesman for a bloc of developing nations called the Group of 77 and China -- whose exemption from emissions targets had long been seen as the most likely source of an impasse -- said the conference had failed largely because of competing economic interests in wealthy countries.
"I hope all the parties have learned their lessons," he said. "The breakdown came from selfishness and lack of political will, in particular from the North."
Staff members for two American senators, one Democrat and one Republican, said all was not lost, however, despite the substantial setback. The Senate had strong reservations about the Kyoto Protocol even before it was negotiated in 1997. And now there was the prospect of a new push in Washington to curb greenhouse gases, they said.
"Regardless of the outcome here, the stage is set in Congress next year to consider addressing this issue in a way that makes economic and environmental sense in a coordinated fashion," one staff member said.

LENGTH: 1831 words

HEADLINE: A History of Himself

BYLINE: By Max Frankel; Max Frankel, former executive editor of The New York Times, is the author of "The Times of My Life and My Life With The Times."

 BODY:

 A LIFE IN THE
 TWENTIETH CENTURY
 Innocent Beginnings, 1917-1950.
 By Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.
 Illustrated. 557 pp. Boston:
 Houghton Mifflin Company. $28.95. 
 The question occurs the moment you spot the subtitle of Volume I of Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.'s memoir, "A Life in the Twentieth Century." Why would even a popular historian think his "Innocent Beginnings, 1917-1950" were worth 557 pages?
My answers, in order of importance:
Schlesinger's beginnings were hardly innocent. His youth was extraordinarily privileged, a combination of first-class genes and parents, brilliant tutors, enviable social connections and comfortable roosts from which to observe the sufferings and traumas of America's greatest depression and war. That elite breeding pond, almost dry now, is worth memorializing.
Moreover, the historian's first 33 years were no mere beginnings. They produced his Pulitzer-worthy "Age of Jackson," which propelled him into the top ranks of popular scholars and gave lifelong weight to his celebrations of the New Deal and American liberalism. Schlesinger observes that we have all done most of our reading by the age of 25. Maybe our best living, too.
And finally, the young Schlesinger's experiences, opinions, travelogues and press notices were all lovingly preserved in journals, manuscripts and multiple caches of correspondence -- all of which the old man has been loath to abbreviate.
Schlesinger dutifully describes his rich inheritances without, alas, fully reflecting on the sense of entitlement they conferred. A congenital Democrat and precocious reader, he early on replaced his maternal middle initial with his father's so as to make himself a Junior and glided through Exeter and Harvard into Dad's profession and philosophical shoes. All that brought easy affiliation with the Ivy establishment of scholarly activists who lusted for the excitements and satisfactions of Washington power games.
By 33, Schlesinger has collected a huge roster of friends, good friends and very good friends that glitters with names like DeVoto, Frankfurter, MacLeish, Bundy, Bohlen, Lippmann, Alsop, Graham, Morgenthau, Rockefeller and on and on -- most of them linked to pretty, lovely, beautiful, beauteous, stunning, even bewitching wives and loves. Small wonder that he sets out (and will by Volume II or III have succeeded for a time) to emulate the 19th-century life of a presumed ancestor, George Bancroft, the first major American historian, who attended the same Exeter and Harvard and also became a Democratic politician, White House ghostwriter and diplomat.
Still, the indispensable intellectual as well as social influence in Junior's life was obviously Arthur Senior, a path-breaking historian whose fame long preceded Junior's. He not only charted the course of his son's research and proofed his early books; he also passed on his devotion to liberalism, contempt for Communism and yearning to broaden historical perspectives. It seems quaint now to recall that the Schlesingers, pere et fils, were so recently struggling to liberate historical narrative from its once exclusive preoccupation with party politics, war and diplomacy. They were prominent among the innovators who related those events to society's workaday concerns and cultural patterns.
It was also Arthur Senior who set the stage for Junior's greatest achievements, his books on Andrew Jackson and the age of Franklin D. Roosevelt, by insisting that American democracy owed as much to the strivings of urban labor as to the wild frontier championed by Frederick Jackson Turner. Arthur Junior's memoir reads best when it revisits such major intellectual shifts and demonstrates how history is always contemporary history -- in his case an obvious effort to explain and justify the innovations of the New Deal. And his book is most instructive in proving that such revisionist argument is both inevitable and constructive, producing endless debates about liberal society's endless yearnings for reform.
SCHLESINGER'S history of himself is rich with other historical insights, candid (though not particularly introspective) about his own misjudgments, disappointingly stingy with his domestic and inner lives and downright tiresome in his excavations of journal trivia, press clips and encomiums. Fortunately, the excesses are relieved by generous doses of gossip, bursts of self-mockery and rewarding riffs on a great many subjects.
For example: "The vanity of historians is to suppose that we understand better than the people who were there what the shouting was all about. . . . But 20-20 hindsight can be carried too far. There is always the risk of Chip Bohlen's 20-20 hind-myopia. Too often we suggest that those poor chaps in the past may have thought they were acting for one set of reasons; but we, so much wiser, know that they were acting for quite other reasons. . . . The assertion that people in the past did not really know why they were doing what they did leads to the conclusion that we do not really know why we are doing what we do today."
Schlesinger offers the swiftest strokes to dispose of some of the century's fiercest controversies. Since F.D.R.'s obvious priority in World War II was the defeat of Germany, he notes, "the charge is absurd that he somehow connived in the Japanese attack" on Pearl Harbor. And: "The decision to drop the bomb was the most tragic decision in American history. Yet . . . the conventional attack on the decision suffers from grave deficiencies. There is neglect of the Japanese sources, which show that even after the bomb fell on Nagasaki, military opposition to surrender reached almost the point of revolt against the emperor."
And consider this superb summary of 40 years of cold war, offered without implying any "moral equivalence" of the Soviet and American superpowers: "Each undertook for what it honestly saw as defensive reasons actions that the other honestly saw as unacceptably threatening and requiring countermeasures. Each succumbed to the propensity to perceive local conflicts in global terms, political conflicts in moral terms and relative differences in absolute terms."
Also noteworthy are bursts of cultural history. Schlesinger's recurrent and romantic tributes to the films of the 30's illuminate a brief but vital decade of American experience. Just as Arthur Senior had listed the 598 books he'd read by the age of 14, the list that shimmers in Junior's diary is of the 482 movies he saw between 1931 and 1936. "We went to the movies for entertainment, of course, but almost as much for instruction
in techniques of self-presentation. . . . Young men sauntered insolently down the street like James Cagney, wisecracked like William Powell, cursed (expletives deleted by censors) like Humphrey Bogart and wooed like Clark Gable. . . . The style to which I vainly aspired was the witty, urbane, nonchalant, unruffled type -- Powell, especially as Hammett's Nick Charles, Fred Astaire, Robert Montgomery, Rex Harrison." What often came out instead, he concedes, was a snotty certainty, verbal aggression and deplorable manners. But the American public, riven by economic disparities and ethnic discriminations, gained "a common dream life, a common fund of reference and fantasy."
Equally instructive are Schlesinger's recollections of the battles to rescue liberalism from the suffocating embrace of Communism and the slow passage of high-minded intellectuals from isolationism to interventionism in World War II. He names names in both debates, including his own as too late a convert to the view that Hitler was no mere imperialist fighting for markets and colonies. "The Second World War led historians of my generation to the reluctant conclusion, founded on our own experience, that there were some problems -- some logjams -- that only war could solve." Grounded in that same wartime experience, he contends, was his contested claim that a similar logjam -- the North's moral disgust over slavery -- made the Civil War necessary and inevitable.
Recognizing that "the present persistently and inevitably recreates the past," Schlesinger critiques his own youthful masterpiece about Andrew Jackson from the perspective of contemporary politics. He did less than justice, he now believes, to Alexander Hamilton, John Quincy Adams and Henry Clay, who served business interests but nonetheless had a sounder conception of the role of government than existed in the antistatist theories of Jacksonians.
SIMILARLY, Schlesinger realizes now that "the predicament of women, of blacks, of Indians . . . was shamefully out of mind." Yet while he develops an elaborate defense for having neglected the evidence of the slaughter of Europe's Jews, he makes little effort to examine the insulation that closed his eyes and those of so many Americans to the horrors of Jim Crow. Nor does he reflect much on his slowness to grasp "our tumble into the nuclear age," his quick and classbound contempt for Truman or his brief but ignorant enthusiasm for Eisenhower. These misjudgments are duly recorded, but only dimly evaluated.
It may be too much to ask that the scholars who enriched our understanding of history by exhuming and composing social and cultural chronicles also satisfy the new interest in narratives of emotional and psychological development. Schlesinger is brilliantly succinct in recognizing the roots of liberalism in the Augustinian tradition as rendered by Reinhold Niebuhr, who taught him that "man's pretensions to reason and virtue . . . are ineradicably tainted by self-interest and self-love." Original sin thus struck him as an incisive metaphor for human frailty, undermining the pretensions of absolutists and requiring "the democratization of authority."
Yet even 557 pages are insufficient to reach the inner frailties of the young Schlesinger. He records his "long pursuit" of Marian Cannon, an artist and author of children's books, who, he notes, was "quite right" that they were not well suited to each other. But that is the last word about the unhappiness of his first marriage. Indeed, there is no glimpse at all of home life until the last page, where the scholar boasts that he could write undistracted by the clamor of his children. He quotes his journals to excess, but only hints at deep emotion in samples of bad, unpublished fiction -- about the guilt he felt for taking up with a "Paris girl" in wartime and the cushy life he led while brother Tom saw combat.
Schlesinger says that by nature or nurture he has always felt the historian's basic impulse "to find out what the past was really like. . . . I cannot walk down Fifth Avenue without wondering how the street and the people on it would have looked a hundred years ago." By nature or nurture, it is one contemporary reader's impulse to wonder what the memoirist was really like, what this distinguished intellectual really thought in the privacy of his dreams.
 
http://www.nytimes.com

SECTION: Section 7; Page 28; Column 3; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1142 words

HEADLINE: Spelling Trouble

BYLINE: By David J. Dent; David J. Dent is the author of "In Search of Black America: Discovering the African-American Dream." He teaches journalism at New York University.

 BODY:

 Losing The Race
 Self-Sabotage in Black America.
 By John H. McWhorter.
 285 pp. New York: The Free Press. $24. 
 John H. McWhorter shares what he calls "the very first memory of my life" in his book "Losing the Race." It happened in that turbulent year, 1968: "A group of black kids, none older than 8, asked me how to spell 'concrete.' I spelled it, only to have the 8-year-old bring his little sister to me and have her smack me repeatedly as the rest of the kids laughed and egged her on. From then on, I was often teased in the neighborhood for being 'smart.' "
McWhorter, who now teaches linguistics at the University of California, Berkeley, says this incident did not occur in one of those low-income neighborhoods often perceived to be the factory producing black youngsters wanting to be so unwhite that they reject and scorn the kind of intelligence that makes for good spelling. McWhorter says he was smacked for being smart in the comfortable black middle-class haven of Mount Airy, a Philadelphia suburb.
That it happened there forms the base of McWhorter's argument that the black middle and upper middle classes are destroying the possibilities and dreams of the civil rights movement. He says a deep anti-intellectual current -- often considered characteristic of the underclass -- has swept into the culture of black privilege and is complemented by an identity clouded in separatism and victimization. In McWhorter's view, this sense of victimization produces a misguided moralism that makes anything black, like O. J. Simpson, always right. He argues that this anti-intellectual strain is largely responsible for the test-score gap between black and white middle-class children. He says this destructive force in black life must end, along with any reliance on affirmative action, before blacks reach true equality.
McWhorter relates several encounters with irresponsible black middle-class students at Berkeley; these, along with other personal experiences like the Mount Airy episode, form the core of the limited original evidence he offers to support his contentions. He often leaps to broad generalizations from personal experiences and observations that are, most often, inadequately explored. For example, he mentions seeing a television program, featuring the actress Nell Carter, in which her character expresses an intense interest in Russian history: "It struck me as a false moment, and I wondered whether the script had originally been written with a white actress in mind. I couldn't help thinking of how very few black people I have ever met who were so passionately interested in a subject that had nothing to do with being black." McWhorter breezes by without considering whether his response reflects his own limited exposure to African-American culture and his inability to see black life in America without polemical lenses.
Too often McWhorter does what many blacks accuse whites of doing -- he draws a conclusion from any negative encounter he has experienced with another black person and assumes it is the norm; whatever violates it is the exception. "I once met an aspiring black linguist who had spent two years in China without learning Chinese beyond what he needed to buy food at the market," he writes. "Most people who spend two years in a foreign country come back speaking the language. . . . This was the only linguist I have ever met who spent two years abroad without becoming bilingual, and it is not likely to be accidental that he was black. Separatism has a way of discouraging black Americans from learning foreign languages other than French and Spanish . . . and Swahili." He produces nothing empirical to support this.
He rightly defines the black majority as middle class, but never imagines that population beyond the confines of the argument against affirmative action. It sometimes seems as if that horrifying day in Mount Airy eternally looms over his view of black Americans. He too often appears simplistically to divide the race into two camps -- those under spells of victimology and separatism and those like himself. "I am not alone," he writes. "An increasing number of black people are questioning the cognitive dissonance between the vast potential of their lives . . . and the insistence of so many blacks around them that America remains a racist purgatory."
His argument remains captive in a closet of his own experiences, with scant assessments of academic studies, and data on the test-score gap that are never explored with the rigor they deserve. Yet the book's shining moments are tied to that shortcoming. McWhorter's accounts of personal encounters are riveting even when they do not fully carry his arguments. For example, he shares some details of his successful climb to tenure that, in his view, did not escape the suspicion of affirmative action. He firmly believes his colleagues, despite his stellar record of publication, can never get beyond a reductive thought: "It was perfectly obvious that in the back of most minds was 'Of course he got tenure -- they wouldn't dare deny tenure to a black person unless he was hopeless.' "(He does not offer any data to show that black professors have an easier route to tenure than others and barely addresses the many stories that suggest otherwise.) In McWhorter's view, all the blame goes to affirmative action -- none to the troubling visions of colleagues who cannot see his success without thinking "affirmative action." And should affirmative action be abolished to appease the limitations of those professors?
McWhorter often celebrates the economic advancement of blacks as evidence that racism in the United States is not as severe as many claim. One is left wondering why he or anyone should celebrate the rise of the black middle class if the majority of those well-off blacks are locked in the cults of victimology and separatism -- and choked by anti-intellectualism -- as he contends. That thought underscores the great frustration in reading "Losing the Race." Even when I find myself disagreeing with McWhorter, I could not help thinking of more solid, substantial and constructive ways to state his argument.
There is documentation that finds some blacks rejecting academic success for fear of being called white and in order to be "cool." One might argue this is complemented by the American disdain of the nerd that looms far beyond black culture and did not begin there. Yet the test-score gap, though not sufficiently explored in "Losing the Race," is real. McWhorter at least brings attention to its possible connection to the anti-intellectual strain that he confronted in Mount Airy and that he says he finds in his black students. This issue is not served when many blacks avoid it. McWhorter stirs the waters. Unfortunately, he does not clarify much in the process. 
http://www.nytimes.com

CORRECTION-DATE: December 24, 2000, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
A review on Nov. 26 about "Losing the Race: Self-Sabotage in Black America," by John H. McWhorter, referred incorrectly to the author's childhood neighborhood, Mount Airy. It is part of Philadelphia, not a suburb.

HEADLINE: Argentina and the I.M.F.

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Paul Krugman, in his Nov. 22 column, "Shadow of Debt," rightly cites Argentina's currency board as the source of the country's economic malaise. Tying the peso to the strong American dollar, the board imported the Federal Reserve's dollar deflation and now has sky-high unemployment and interest rates. 
 The currency board did end Argentina's hyperinflation when the price of gold was stable at roughly $350 an ounce, between 1985 and 1997 -- but as gold has fallen, the peso and Argentina's economy have deflated. In response, the International Monetary Fund gave Argentina a loan but required it to raise taxes, putting the country deeper into the ditch. Now the I.M.F. is back with another loan and more austerity.
Mr. Krugman notes that fans of the currency board say it is the "next best thing" to a gold standard. Indeed, Argentina would be much better off if it dissolved the board, rolled back the tax increases and pegged the peso to gold. It would soon have the strongest economy in Latin America, instead of being a crippled ward of the I.M.F.
 
JUDE WANNISKI
Morristown, N.J., Nov. 22, 2000
 
The writer is an economist.

SECTION: Section C; Page 6; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1131 words

HEADLINE: Group for Cooperatives Seeks to Make Credibility a Lure of Its New .Coop Domain

BYLINE: By CHRIS GAITHER

 BODY:
When the panel that governs the Internet's naming system decided this month to add seven new suffixes, it ended months of discussion and argument over how to relieve the pressure on the crowded .com domain.
But it did not solve the issue of whether the seven entities that won the rights to administer the new domains would truly create viable alternatives for all the forces seeking their own new piece of cyberspace. 
 Still, one of them, the National Cooperative Business Association, has set a lofty goal with its new .coop domain: to make it "easier for consumers, producers and others to find the businesses they trust in the global electronic marketplace," according to its Web site.
"What a cooperative can do is address chaos in the marketplace," said Paul Hazen, president of the association, a decades-old group based in Washington that the names panel chose from nearly 50 applicants.
Meeting in Marina del Rey, Calif., on Nov. 16, the Internet Corporation for Assigned Names and Numbers, known as Icann, chose the seven new dots -- aero, biz, coop, info, museum, name and pro -- making the largest structural change to the Internet since the late 1980's.
Though existing domains like .com, .org and .net were created to differentiate Internet addresses, the distinctions have largely been lost. Critics also say the .com domain is overcrowded, with more than 20 million names.
"There's only so many common-word names, and if you only have three suffixes, 'thefarmerinthedell' can be used up pretty quickly," said Jeffrey Moser, director of economic and co-op development for the National Farmers Union, a Denver-based trade association that supported .coop.
To begin the process, Mr. Hazen's group paid the $50,000 application fee to Icann and brought in Poptel, a British Internet service provider run as a co-op, to provide technical support. The association also received a strong endorsement from the International Co-operative Alliance, a worldwide trade association based in Geneva that claims 750,000 member cooperatives.
The groups selected to run domains will negotiate contracts with Icann during the next few months. Icann must then receive approval from the Commerce Department, which began handing over administrative functions to Icann in 1998. The new domains are expected to appear next summer.
The winners included some more straightforward business proposals, like one from Afilias, a consortium of name registrars that hopes to sign up businesses to its .info domain, and one from JVTeam, a new venture that will run the .biz domain. But the cooperative association is seeking to tie its domain to a larger mission.
Since 1916, National Cooperative Business Association, formerly called the Cooperative League of America, has sought to tussle with corporations for a piece of various markets, from banking to agriculture. In the 1930's, when large electricity companies rebuffed New Deal efforts to bring power to rural areas, cooperatives moved to fill the gap. World War II sparked the association to form the Cooperative for American Remittances to Europe, today called CARE, to deliver food parcels to troops overseas.
The association, whose ranks now include Land O' Lakes, the second-largest dairy cooperative in the United States, behind the Dairy Farmers of America, advocates a form of democratic capitalism. The member businesses, which also include co-ops dedicated to organic foods, are owned by customers and co-op members.
Although most co-ops already operate Web sites ending in .com or .org, Mr. Hazen said, "they didn't fit neatly into those categories." He said co-ops may use the new domain to find a space between the business interests of .com and the nonprofit world of .org.
The association said it expected to register 155,000 addresses in the first four years. The proposed registration fee, $75, is much higher than the fees proposed by the other winning sites, which average about $5. Mr. Hazen said the fee would allow his association and the International Co-operative Alliance to screen every request for a new Internet address, to ensure that the applicant was a certified cooperative.
The association said the strict registration process would give users confidence about the quality of a .coop business.
"Consumer mistrust of e-commerce is growing," Patricia A. Brownell, executive director of the National Credit Union Foundation, wrote to Icann in support of the association. "It's critical that cooperative businesses have an opportunity to identify themselves to consumers and others who prefer to do business with us."
Despite the association's selection, Icann board members said during the process that they were wary of proposals to regulate the content on any new domain. Citing fears about the ability to control what appears on a Web site, the board turned down applications for .kids, a domain for children proposed by .Kids Domains Inc., and .health, a domain for medical Web sites proposed by the World Health Organization.
"There are a whole variety of potential traps here," Vinton G. Cerf, the newly elected Icann chairman, said in an interview. But he added that the board set aside its fears and "chose dot-coop because co-ops are usually reasonably well-defined."
Icann officials said that they chose the first seven domains in general because they seemed the most likely to work. Based on how true that actually turns out, Icann will consider releasing new ones, including many of the ones not accepted in this first round, Mr. Cerf said.
Mr. Hazen said he expected cooperatives to move slowly onto .coop, especially well-known groups with established Web sites. Some may continue to use .com to market to the public while marketing .coop to members of their cooperative, he said.
"We can create a place for just cooperatives on the Internet, but they can also play in dot-com land if they want to," he said.
A spokeswoman for the National Council of Farmer Cooperatives, a trade association based in Washington, said she had heard little discussion among the council's membership about jumping the dot-com ship.
"Most of them do have a dot-com site," said Pamela Valenzuela, director of community and member relations. "Whether they're going to move to dot-coop, especially if they're well established, I doubt it."
With access restricted to co-ops, however, smaller businesses that never found room on .com may discover the space they need. A New York-based seller of electricity, 1st Rochdale Cooperative Group, said it planned to abandon its .net site as soon as it could get new business cards and stationery, probably within six months after .coop appears.
"Co-op will be the one we'll be using in future advertising," said Allen L. Thurgood, chief executive.
