HEADLINE: Health Costs Underestimated, Experts Say

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Health costs will grow faster than the government now assumes, partly because of advances in medical technology, and the long-term financial outlook for Medicare is therefore less rosy than officials assume, a federal advisory panel has concluded.
The chairman of the panel, composed of three economists and three actuaries, presented its findings today to Donna E. Shalala, the secretary of health and human services. It is the first such outside review in nearly a decade. Dr. Shalala is one of six Medicare trustees. 
 In an interview tonight, Dale H. Yamamoto, the chairman of the panel, said: "We think health care costs will grow somewhat faster than the trustees now assume. That means that the current long-term projections of Medicare spending over the next 75 years are low."
The trustees of the Medicare program said in April that the hospital insurance trust fund, which pays hospital bills for Medicare beneficiaries, would be solvent until 2025. But under the new assumptions recommended by the advisory panel, the trust fund would run out of money four years sooner, in 2021.
Medicare provides care to 39 million people who are elderly or disabled. The annual cost, $218 billion in the fiscal year that ended on Sept. 30, is expected to double by 2010.
The panel, known as the Technical Review Panel on the Medicare Trustees' Reports, was created by Dr. Shalala to evaluate the assumptions, estimates and forecasts made each year by the trustees. She will present the panel's conclusions this week to other trustees of the Medicare program, including Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers and Labor Secretary Alexis M. Herman.
New technology includes medical devices, diagnostic products, drugs and surgical procedures -- everything from digital mammography to laser surgery to artificial skin, from angioplasty to hip replacement to the radioactive "seeds" used to treat prostate cancer.
The panel did not discuss proposals for new benefits, like coverage of prescription drugs. But its report is likely to reinforce the need for caution as Congress considers such expansions of the program.
For several years, the Medicare trustees have assumed that average health costs for each beneficiary will grow, after 2025, at the same rate as the gross domestic product.
But Mr. Yamamoto, an expert on employee benefits at Hewitt Associates in Lincolnshire, Ill., said he had told Secretary Shalala that "that assumption is not necessarily reasonable."
In the long term, Mr. Yamamoto said, "members of the panel believe that health care costs per beneficiary will grow about 1 percentage point more than the annual increase in gross domestic product per capita."
Len M. Nichols, an economist at the Urban Institute who serves on the panel, said: "This is an important change. The current assumption by the Medicare trustees, in their last report, was that per capita G.D.P. would grow 1.2 percent a year over the next 75 years."
If the higher estimates are accurate, they imply that the annual cost of Medicare, after 75 years, would be 60 percent higher than now assumed, panel members said.
What does that mean in practice?
David M. Cutler, a professor of economics at Harvard who is on the panel, said: "Health spending is currently about 13.5 percent of gross domestic product. In 75 years, under the assumptions suggested by our panel, it would be 30 percent of G.D.P."
Another panel member, Michael E. Chernew, a health care economist at the University of Michigan in Ann Arbor, said, "Technology is the primary factor driving the growth of per capita health care costs in the long run."
Mr. Cutler said he believed that medical services would "get better as they become more expensive," so Americans would be willing to pay the additional costs. "Technology," he said, "accounts for half to two-thirds of the growth in medical spending that is not attributable to inflation in the economy as a whole."
In 1993, after President Clinton took office, officials predicted that Medicare's hospital trust fund would run out of money in 1999. But improvements in Medicare's financial condition have reduced the sense of urgency. A strong economy has generated additional tax revenues. Cutbacks in payments to health care providers, combined with a crackdown on fraud, have slowed the growth of Medicare spending.
The six Medicare trustees, including two representatives of the public, supervise the financial operations of the program and make recommendations to Congress on how to improve its financial condition.
The advisory panel does not see managed care as a panacea.
"Managed care may get inefficiencies out of the system and can reduce the level of health care costs," Mr. Chernew said, "but it does not necessarily slow the rate of growth in costs to a sustainable rate. It's uncertain how much managed care will slow the adoption of new medical technology."
One of the panel members, Alice F. Rosenblatt, said she agreed that "Medicare costs will grow more than current assumptions indicate."
Ms. Rosenblatt, a senior vice president of WellPoint Health Networks, said the government should be doing more analysis of Medicare spending. At WellPoint, a managed care company with 7.7 million patients, she said, "we do more analysis of data and trends" than does the Medicare program, which serves five times as many people.
Mr. Yamamoto said he felt confident in predicting that technology would drive up health costs.
"We have a generation of people coming into the Medicare program who have lived a pretty good life," he said. "They will demand more technology."
Even procedures that save money in an individual case may add to health spending over all, experts said. Doctors now usually remove the gallbladder with a few small incisions in the abdomen, using a technique that is safer and less expensive than the old procedure. But, Mr. Chernew said, more people have the operation now, so, in many health plans, total spending is higher.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Jitters About New Economy Bolster Old-Economy Issues

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
While a welcome assessment of the nation's economic health sent blue-chip stocks rising yesterday, investors again punished technology issues for their bleak earnings prospects.
And after the market closed, Gateway, which makes and sells personal computers, warned that profits and sales for the fourth quarter would be lower than analysts' estimates because consumer holiday sales of personal computers have been much weaker than expected. 
 Gateway said it expected operating income of at least 37 cents a share, 25 cents below analysts' consensus estimates. Its shares fell $10.70, to $20.30, in regular trading, but were up 20 cents in after-hours trading.
Shares of personal computer makers tumbled on Tuesday after Salomon Smith Barney suggested excess inventory could be a problem.
Yesterday, the Dow Jones industrial average rose 121.53 points, or 1.2 percent, to 10,629.11, but the Nasdaq composite index fell 28.05 points, or 1 percent, to 2,706.93. The Nasdaq's decline gave it yet another new low for this year.
The broader Standard & Poor's 500-stock index advanced 5.82 points, or 0.4 percent, to 1,341.91.
Despite the decline in technology stocks, investors traded with cautious optimism on new evidence that the nation's economic growth was slowing, analysts said. The Commerce Department said yesterday that the gross domestic product grew at an annual rate of 2.4 percent last summer, the slowest rate in four years and a steep decline from the 5.6 percent rate in the second quarter.
Wall Street considered the report good news because the slowdown could be an incentive for the Federal Reserve to declare at its meeting on Dec. 19 that inflation is less of a risk to the economy. That could be a precursor to lower interest rates.
"We're still feeling quite bullish on the market," said Eugene G. Mintz, financial markets analyst at Brown Brothers Harriman. "The Fed likely will go to a more neutral bias, which is what today's G.D.P. figure pointed to."
While other recent reports have pointed to a slowing economy, investors have been waiting for a significant drop in G.D.P. because it carries more weight with the Fed, analysts said.
The possibility of lower interest rates, Mr. Mintz said, would help ease some of the expected damage from fourth-quarter earnings disappointments.
"Earnings are going to be mainly weaker than people expected," he said, adding that history has shown that "lower interest rates will overpower disappointing earnings."
Investors have been selling since September because of lackluster results and worries about inflation and have been focusing on expensive technology issues that are not producing the same stellar profits as last year.
The technology sector, already punished for its dismal performance, tumbled further yesterday. Microsoft lost $1.94, to $65.06; Sun Microsystems slipped $1.50, to $79.75.
Investors continued to migrate to old-economy stocks, whose earnings are seen as more predictable.
"You are seeing the rally in safe havens like McDonald's and drugs and places like retailers that have been washed out," said Jim Myers, director of technical research at Janney Montgomery Scott.
McDonald's rose $1.25, to $32.50.
Drug and financial issues, which are often considered safer bets in uncertain or bearish markets, also bolstered the blue chips. Merck picked up $2.62, to $94.88, and Bank One rose $1.34, to $35.56.
Retailers, which have suffered on concerns that a dip in consumer confidence and the slowing economy would hamper holiday sales, were mixed. Wal-Mart Stores advanced $2.34, to $51.75. Limited fell $1.56, to $20.38, after releasing November sales results that were short of Wall Street's expectations.

Treasury Prices Rally
By Reuters
Bonds extended their rally yesterday after the government said the economy grew at its slowest pace in four years in the third quarter and technology stocks continued to slide.
Yields on most Treasuries tumbled to their lowest levels in a year or more, trading three-quarters to one percentage point below the Federal Reserve's 6.5 percent rate on overnight loans between banks, an indication the market was betting official rates would follow suit.
The price of the benchmark 10-year note rose 15/32, to 101 23/32, while its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 5.52 percent from 5.58 percent on Tuesday. The 30-year bond rose 16/32, to 108 22/32. Its yield fell to 5.64 percent from 5.68 percent.

HEADLINE: CONTESTING THE VOTE: ELECTIONS AROUND THE WORLD;
Yes, Democracy Is Imperfect, Even in Those Places That Never Heard of Chads

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN

DATELINE: BERLIN, Nov. 29

 BODY:
In India, portable polling stations are carried into the desert on the backs of camels. Others are hauled through forests and across rivers as the world's largest democracy organizes elections for more than 600 million voters.
But once inside the polling booth, Indians have a relatively easy task, at least by the standards of the butterfly punch-card ballot in Palm Beach County. Presented the names of candidates and their political parties, listed one beneath the other, voters apply an ink stamp beside one of them to indicate their choice. 
 So it goes in much of the world, an informal survey suggests. Democracy is still a low-tech, old-economy business: ballots are marked by hand -- with crosses or stamps or fingerprints -- and then counted by hand, with an assortment of officials supposed to guarantee impartiality looking on.
If manual counting is "subjective," as George W. Bush suggested this week, then global democracy is overwhelmingly a subjective thing. Tallying by "objective" machines or computers is still extremely rare.
"We don't have any problems with electronic ballots, because we don't have any," said Iain McLean, an expert on a British electoral system where hand counting is standard practice. "But we should hardly pride ourselves on our bumbling amateurism."
An explosion of pride in various home-grown or start-up expressions of democracy has, however, accompanied the travails of the United States as it seeks to decipher what the American people said. From Serbia to Zimbabwe to Azerbaijan, glee at American adversity in its trademark product -- democracy -- has been irrepressible.
"If this had happened in Zimbabwe," said Jonathan Moyo, the spokesman for Robert Mugabe's government, "we would have been subjected to sanctions."
Such Schadenfreude, now reaching epidemic proportions, is the price paid by the world's "beacon of democracy" when the outcome of a presidential election is seen to dangle by "dimpled" or "pregnant" or "hanging" chads -- those tantalizing fragments of ballots that have been partly punched or indented, but not so decisively as to convince an automated counter.
There were no such problems in the presidential election in the Ivory Coast last month. After registering, voters dipped a finger in ink that would stain their skin for a couple of days and so deter any inclination to vote twice. Then they made a print of their finger inside a box next to the candidate of their choice.
Those candidates were indicated by name and party affiliation. They were also represented by photographs intended to help illiterate voters. (In Egypt, for similar reasons, symbols like a camel, a crescent or a Ramadan lamp are used to represent parties on ballots.)
After applying a fingerprint, Ivory Coast voters dropped the ballot into a glass box, a symbol of transparency. Later, election officials tallied the results in the company of representatives from each candidate. Democracy at work in more or less the form it takes in much of the world.
But democracy, as is well known, is only the least imperfect of political systems. There was a shortage of ballots in some areas, a shortage of election officials in others.
There was also a shortage of willingness to accept any but the preordained result. When the military ruler who had called the election, Gen. Robert Guei, was found to be losing, officials close to him uncovered "massive fraud" and declared him the winner. As in Yugoslavia, it took a street uprising to give the ballot backbone and dislodge an authoritarian ruler from power.
In Mexico, democracy has existed for decades. Every six years, over a period of 71 years, voters went to the polls and selected the candidate from the Institutional Revolutionary Party, or PRI, to govern them. Democracy-as-clockwork.
Things changed only this year, after President Ernesto Zedillo set up a new agency, the Federal Elections Institute, that spent almost $1 billion cleaning up the mechanisms of democracy in its Mexican guise.
New booths for the nation's 112,500 polling stations were designed. They were the first to have a curtain and the first to fit only one voter. No longer did many Mexicans mark their ballot in sight of, and under the suasion of, the local PRI boss.
In addition, for the first time, 700,000 Mexicans were randomly selected to act as polling officials, and a computer system was established that rapidly posted on the Internet the results of the manually counted voting.
As Mexico shows, democracy is pliable. Intimidation, voting by dead people (but not their election to the Senate) and ballot stuffing are some of the ways of endowing democracy with a despotic edge. In the Indonesia of President Suharto, people had to vote at work, where ballots were opened and tallied on a big board visible to everybody.
So the privacy of the polling booth is critical. But the Philippines has come up with an alternative. People vote in little fold-out cardboard enclosures set atop school desks, before putting their ballot in boxes.
The Philippines is a highly literate nation, and voters there are required to write in the name of their candidate -- an unthinkable notion in Cambodia, where most people are semiliterate at best and a selection of powerful reading glasses is offered to voters at booths.
To counter the "subjectivity" of hand counting, the Philippines makes the tally of votes a very public business. Boxes are opened, ballots read out and the results marked on huge hand-lettered paper sheets taped to walls. Courtyards of polling places ring to the sounds of names like Cruz, Ocampo and Manglapus.
Family names may be sullied in Kazakhstan if it should emerge that democratic dissent exists within a family. So under a system known as "family voting," the father often collects all the voting cards from family members who are eligible and takes them to the polls to vote for his entire clan. Although not strictly legal, the practice is widely tolerated.
In many countries, absence from the polls is not forgiven, or, if so, only grudgingly. No absentee voting by mail is allowed in Mexico, and so border towns like Tijuana get very crowded on election day. Turkey also has no provision for absentee voting.
British citizens living abroad must appoint a proxy to go to the polling place with an authorization to vote for them. In Germany, any postal ballot must be received by the close of business on election day; Florida's 10-day wait for mail from overseas is unthinkable.
In some places, voting is not optional. Australia makes its people vote; citizens are fined, at least in theory, if they do not do their civic duty. Turks are also obliged by law to vote, although the penalties for not doing so have been lifted. Brazilians must explain themselves if they fail to abide by an obligation to vote.
Some European countries are beginning to test machines to count the votes. Germany has used a few in local elections, but they must be publicly tested first and approved by the Interior Ministry. Britain uses machines in some voting for local offices, but legislation would be required for their use in a general election -- the vote for a parliament and a prime minister -- and no such legislation is pending.
Egypt, however, has simplified matters to the point where machines would be superfluous, at least in its presidential election. Parliament nominates one person to run for president, on a yes-or-no ballot. Since parliament has been in the control of the state party, the National Democratic Party, since the revolution, the candidate (and future president) is always the incumbent.
But this, too, is democracy, some would argue.
"Just because a country does not have a perfect election," said Rauf Huseynov, an adviser to the president of Azerbaijan, "that does not mean it is undemocratic, as the United States has now shown."

HEADLINE: Clients Fatten Rolodex Of Giuliani's Best Friend;
Private Sector Prizes Ex-Adviser's Help

BYLINE: By ELISABETH BUMILLER

 BODY:
For years, Peter J. Powers was the calm to Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's storm, the shy boyhood friend who grew up to govern alongside the mayor and become, quietly, the second most powerful man in City Hall.
Now, four years out of City Hall but after only two as the president of his own consulting firm, Mr. Powers has assembled a stunning list of clients with hundreds of millions of dollars in business before the Giuliani administration. Colleagues in the business credit his judgment and savvy, but also his 40-year friendship with the mayor. 
 Among his clients are the New York Jets, whose owners are negotiating with the city for a new home in a proposed stadium on the West Side. Mr. Powers, a former corporate tax lawyer who left City Hall to join a hedge fund that went out of business, also advises the financial information company of Michael R. Bloomberg. Mr. Bloomberg, who is worth $4 billion, is considering running for mayor.
Mr. Powers's other clients include the Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum, which this week was given the right and financial help by the city to build a 40-story, $678 million headquarters on the East River. Another client of Mr. Powers, the Museum of Modern Art, received the approval of a City Council zoning panel this week for a $650 million expansion.
Mr. Powers also advises Pfizer, the nation's largest drug company, which has headquarters on 42nd Street and a manufacturing plant in a large urban renewal zone in Brooklyn. He advises Waste Management Inc., the nation's largest trash hauler, which has $259 million in contracts to collect city garbage. And he advises the Greater New York Hospital Association's profit-making subsidiary, GNYHA Ventures, whose executive vice president, Lee H. Perlman, first met Mr. Powers at City Hall.
Mr. Powers, 56, emphasizes that his company, Powers Global Strategies, gives advice to businesses, and that he is not a registered lobbyist seeking action from government officials. "I don't do it," he said in an interview in the Midtown offices of Howard J. Rubenstein, the city's premier public relations consultant, from whom Mr. Powers rents space. "Never. Look, I'm legally entitled to lobby. There's no restriction on me lobbying; it's just a choice that I've made." Why? "When you do that, when you had the position I had, it could be embarrassing. People would question it all the time."
Consultants, unlike lobbyists, do not have to file reports under the city's stringent lobbying laws, meaning that Mr. Powers also does not have to disclose his fees. Nonetheless, major companies in New York City typically pay consultants at least $5,000 to $10,000 a month. Although Mr. Powers would not disclose the number of his clients, he did confirm 10, meaning that he may gross $600,000 to $1.2 million annually from those clients alone. His salary in 1995 as first deputy mayor was $112,000 a year.
Mr. Powers, who was always seen as a conciliatory voice in a tough-minded City Hall never known for building friendships with outsiders, is hardly the first mayoral aide to create a lucrative career based on government connections and expertise. But his career is striking in an administration that has tried, not always successfully, to present itself as freer of such influence than City Halls past.
Four years ago, after news accounts detailed the thriving lobbying business of two of the mayor's most influential unpaid advisers, Raymond B. Harding and Herman Badillo, Mr. Giuliani reacted at first by saying he wanted to ban all lobbying of city agencies. But skeptics said that would be like banning winter, and the mayor conceded that he did not know how such a ban would work. Nothing, in short, changed.
"The truth of it is, if I wanted to do business in New York City, then Peter Powers would be high on my list to talk to," said Mel Miller, a successful city lobbyist and the former speaker of the New York State Assembly. "He can tell you who to talk to. Now, obviously, if he talks to that person, he's lobbying. But if he tells you to talk to that person, he's not."
The difference between offering advice and lobbying is a fine one. Under city law, lobbying includes any contact with a government official on behalf of a client to influence a city action, like the awarding of a contract.
"I don't set up meetings with people," Mr. Powers said, "I don't do winks, I don't do nods, I don't call the deputy mayors, I don't call the commissioners, I don't call anybody." Nothing, however, prevents a client from contacting the government official and then using the consultant's name. Even so, Mr. Powers said he tells clients not to do so, although it is unclear how much he can actually control name-dropping. "That does not go on," he said emphatically.
Joseph J. Lhota, the deputy mayor who in essence has Mr. Powers's old job, said yesterday that although he saw Mr. Powers at social events, he did not talk to him about clients. "Peter Powers has never lobbied me," he said. "He's probably the most ethical person I've ever met in my entire life. I have no idea who his clients are."
Mr. Giuliani said through his spokeswoman, Sunny Mindel, that he was also unaware of Mr. Powers's clients, even though Mr. Powers's relationship with the Jets is well known. Mr. Powers, when asked how much he talks about his business with Mr. Giuliani, replied, "Zero."
What, then, does Mr. Powers do for his clients?
The Guggenheim describes Mr. Powers's role as sounding board and general strategist. Last year, Mr. Powers also helped the museum put together its presentation to the City Economic Development Corporation asking that the museum be given the right to develop its new headquarters on three East River piers.
"This was something we'd never done before," said Laurie Beckelman, the museum's deputy director for special projects. "So we thought it would be helpful to have someone who understands how the city works, and who could advise us."
Ms. Beckelman, a former chairwoman of the Landmarks Preservation Commission under both Mr. Giuliani and Mayor David N. Dinkins, said that much of Mr. Powers's advice was practical.
"What I've done with him is say, 'This is the way I'm proposing to do it, these are my bullet points. Is there anything you think I should add?' " Ms. Beckelman said.
GNYHA Ventures, in contrast, describes Mr. Powers's role as more of a go-between for attracting business partners. For example, Mr. Powers recently introduced Mr. Perlman, the executive vice president of GNYHA, to another of his clients, a technology company. Mr. Perlman had been looking for a partner to help him develop a new GNYHA business, babypressconference.com, a hospital amenity that allows new parents to introduce their newborns to family and friends live over the Internet.
"He had the right people from the company at the table, all set up with an agenda," Mr. Perlman said. "I think he's basically taken the time to recreate himself as a business consultant rather than just leveraging his former relationships."
Mr. Bloomberg described yet another role for Mr. Powers, that of general counselor. "I do rely on him for his judgment," Mr. Bloomberg said. "He's a smart, honest guy. Everything he says I think is well reasoned. And he's not a mean guy; he's just a nice guy that's smart. And he's got a lot of restraints."
Mr. Powers would not say specifically that he is talking to Mr. Bloomberg about his possible mayoral bid, other than to say that he is advising him on "certain things" but that "I don't consider myself a political consultant, nor do I intend to be a political consultant to any campaign."
Although the Bloomberg company has hired a lobbyist in an effort to get tax breaks from the city for a new headquarters on the site of the old Alexander's store in Midtown, company representatives said Mr. Powers was not a part of that process.
Jay Cross, the president of Jets Development, the subsidiary that is looking for a new home for the football team, said that he relies on Mr. Powers to decode the arcane world of development in New York City, and that he also leans on his reputation. "He's credible within the community where we are trying to make this project happen," Mr. Cross said.
Another executive at one of the companies that Mr. Powers advises described a more amorphous role, similar to that played by Mr. Rubenstein. In essence, the executive said, $5,000 or $10,000 a month is not a lot of money for a large company to have Mr. Powers as an insurance policy should there be trouble with City Hall, or some other government agency, later on.
"You have a guy around who's smart," said the executive, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "Rubenstein is on our payroll, too. If I ever do need someone, I can call Howard. And I think Peter serves exactly the same role."
The larger question is whether Mr. Powers will have staying power beyond the tenure of Mr. Giuliani, whose term is up on Dec. 31, 2001.
"Will he survive past the mayor?" Mr. Rubenstein said. "Peter Powers will survive."

LENGTH: 1107 words

HEADLINE: COUNCIL APPROVES A LONG-TERM PLAN FOR CITY GARBAGE

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON

 BODY:
After years of planning and politicking, New York City took its first formal step yesterday into a future after the Fresh Kills Landfill, when the City Council overwhelmingly approved a long-term plan to deal with the city's daily mountain of refuse through at least the opening decades of the new century.
Fresh Kills, in Staten Island, once the most trash-deluged spot in the nation, is to close next year. The new plan is essentially a blueprint for trying to live without it, through a system of transfer stations that will send the city's residential garbage out of state. 
 But the replacement package, a $6 billion network of stations, barges, trucks and trains, is also for some a moral document, because it finally commits the city -- on paper and through force of law -- to abandon a policy many historians and residents have come to see as a betrayal of Staten Island. Fresh Kills was opened with a solemn promise to borough residents that the dump would be no more than a stopgap. That was more than 50 years ago.
"This is an act of moral responsibility," said Stephen J. Fiala, a Republican councilman from Staten Island, in an emotional statement late Tuesday after the Council's Environmental Protection Committee voted to approve the measure.
Politics also played a role, and the closing of Fresh Kills is a testament to the influence of the Staten Island borough president, Guy V. Molinari, a Republican and longtime supporter of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and Gov. George E. Pataki.
The waste management plan, which passed by a 45-to-1 vote, with one abstention, also marks a number of new beginnings for the city, and some lawmakers warned of potential disasters.
To make the system work, a huge new barge-to-rail transfer station in Linden, N.J., will have to be built. The plant will require at least four years to build, as well as the support of Gov. Christie Whitman of New Jersey, who has warned that the possibility of even one scrap of paper falling into New Jersey waters would give her reason to deny a permit.
An amendment, added only this week to the final draft, would require the city's Department of Sanitation, which now mostly handles residential garbage, to study the city's vast amount of commercial waste, which is now mostly handled by private carting companies. Some lawmakers said the study would be the first step toward bringing commercial garbage under city jurisdiction.
At its core, the plan also presents a return to an older style of sanitation management that predates the Fresh Kills dump, giving local control to each of the city's five boroughs. Because so many local politicians and residents were concerned that their district or neighborhood would somehow become the next Fresh Kills -- a central depository or transfer station for the whole city -- the plan gives each borough a guarantee that it will handle no trash but its own, at its own transfer stations.
Some of the stations already exist and would require retrofitting, like the one in Greenpoint, Brooklyn. Others would have to be built. Staten Island's station, for example, would be built on part of the site of the current landfill.
Some lawmakers, even as they voted for the plan, expressed concerns that the city was committing itself to a path of least political resistance that may come back to haunt residents or lawmakers.
"We keep pushing this problem away, hoping it will go away," said John D. Sabini, a Democratic councilman from Queens. Past generations of New Yorkers mostly dealt with their own trash. Under the new plan, Mr. Sabini said, a booming New York is paying to make its trash go away, and people elsewhere are, for now, willing partners.
"We're relying on the good graces of people in other states to take our garbage," Mr. Sabini said. "Those good graces won't be there forever."
Other experts in sanitation policy said the great significance of the bill, which is technically a series of major amendments to a 10-year waste management plan passed by the city in 1992, was that it could be done at all. As late as the mid-1990's, city sanitation officials said that Fresh Kills could be closed no sooner than perhaps 2008 or 2010.
Mayor Giuliani and Governor Pataki then announced in May 1996 that Fresh Kills would close by the end of 2001. That deadline created an overwhelming pressure. The city's daily total of about 13,000 tons of garbage, most of which was going to Fresh Kills, would have to go somewhere else. In the interim, the city has been gradually reducing the use of Fresh Kills. Currently, about 3,000 tons a day is disposed of there.
"The history of solid-waste management in New York suggests that change only comes with a crisis looming," said Eric A. Goldstein, a lawyer with the Natural Resources Defense Council, a private conservation group.
As part of the city's interim plan, which will continue until the Linden plant and the other transfer stations in the boroughs are ready, trucks carry garbage to landfills and other sites in Virginia, Pennsylvania and New Jersey. It is not yet known what sites will be used under the long-term plan. The new plan would greatly reduce that truck traffic. A spokesman for Governor Whitman also mentioned the appeal of fewer trucks crossing the Hudson River.
Under the plan, the trash would be packed onto covered barges at the city's borough transfer stations, to be sent to Linden. Once there, it would be transferred into closed rail cars. Sanitation Department officials said the rail cars would take the trash to landfills or other sites.
Because both Mr. Pataki and Mr. Giuliani support the goal of closing Fresh Kills, Sanitation Department officials said the New York approvals were essentially accomplished.
In New Jersey, the Union County Board of Freeholders is expected to take up the plan for Linden as early as tonight, and while New York City officials said they believed that the plan would pass there as well, local residents opposed to the idea are expected to show up in force. If the plan is passed by the county, the New Jersey Department of Environmental Protection will then consider it.
One woman who came from Linden yesterday to witness the New York vote, Beatrice Bernzott, said she was organizing busloads of residents from Linden and surrounding communities to attend the Union County meeting to protest. Ms. Bernzott said that she tried to get people to come to the city and demonstrate against the plan, but that in her community, the disdain or fear of the city was simply too much to overcome.
"I couldn't bribe people to come to New York," she said.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1714 words

HEADLINE: Economics Driving an Expansion Of Hospitals Catering to Children

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER

 BODY:
Of all the things that New York hospitals have been up to in the last decade -- research, mergers and fights with managed-care companies -- erecting new buildings has not been among them.
The trend has been to pull back on space for inpatient care. Hospitals can ill afford to build new buildings because so much care now takes place outside them. Since 1980, 178 hospitals in the state have either closed, merged or been converted to other uses. 
 But there is one striking exception: hospitals devoted to the care of children.
Three hospitals in the New York City region are now building major children's hospitals. In the Bronx, Montefiore Medical Center's children's hospital is scheduled for completion next November. Westchester Medical Center plans to open its children's hospital in 2003. And New York Presbyterian Hospital broke ground this month for its new center next to its respected but dilapidated Babies and Children's Hospital in Washington Heights. New Jersey already has several children's hospitals of its own.
The expansion of hospitals that cater only to children is driven largely by straightforward and powerful economic realities. Such centers have a much easier time attracting philanthropic money than adult centers do, health care experts say. Further, Congress recently allocated extra money to cover some costs of training doctors in children's hospitals, and a federal program to insure needy children may help hospitals that serve the poor.
New York finally appears to be catching up with the enormous expansion of children's hospitals that has occurred nationwide in the last 15 years. Between 1993 and 1997, at least 70 percent of the nation's 175 medical centers devoted to children -- free-standing, independent centers, as well as those affiliated with large hospitals like the three under way in New York -- made major capital investments by building entire new centers or extensively revamping existing space.
Of the roughly $622 million being spent on capital projects at hospitals around New York State, 70 percent is being used to upgrade services for children.
The shift of children's care into specialized centers also signals that New York hospitals are at last facing the reality that they can no longer dabble in every area of care -- like pediatrics, heart surgery and neurology -- and expect to thrive financially. Increasingly, they are looking to jettison departments that are handling dwindling numbers of patients.
"Because of the current cost pressures, hospitals are focusing on what they do best and trimming off other services where there are other providers of them," said Jeannie Cross, vice president of the Healthcare Association of New York State, a trade group.
Pediatrics has been a tough business for adult hospitals in recent years. The average length of a child's hospital stay has declined about 7 percent in the last decade to roughly six days, largely because many chronic illnesses like asthma are now treated outside hospitals. The shorter stays have eroded revenues.
At the same time, the illnesses of the children who do stay in hospitals tend to be much more serious, in part because of advances in treating once-untreatable diseases like pediatric cancers. Those sicker children tend to stay in the hospital longer and require very specialized care. Decreased reimbursements from managed-care companies make providing this care all the more costly.
As a result, dozens of hospitals around New York State have eliminated or scaled back pediatric services. In the New York City region, pediatrics discharges fell 14 percent from 1995 to 1998, according to the latest figures available.
Continuum Health Partners, which runs Beth Israel Medical Center and St. Luke's-Roosevelt Hospital Center in Manhattan, is consolidating pediatric services this year, after 10 years during which pediatric admissions declined about 30 percent at Beth Israel. "We have to make some very intelligent decisions about what services we should be offering," said Peter A. Kelly, the system's chief operating officer.
Mr. Kelly said that the corporation would offer most of its pediatric services at St. Luke's hospital in Morningside Heights. Beth Israel, he said, would retain only a handful of pediatric beds on an adult floor. As for the new children's hospitals, Mr. Kelly said, "We would refer our children there enthusiastically."
The planned children's hospitals may therefore not lack for patients, and their focus and superior resources are very likely to result in higher-quality care, advocates say.
The ability of children's hospitals around the country to corner the market on pediatric care has caused Moody's Investors Service to give bonds for children's hospitals far better ratings than those for adult centers, said Lisa Martin, a senior credit officer at Moody's. While adult centers tend to break even or lose money, children's centers often have modest profit margins.
The hospitals will have pediatric specialists, rooms and equipment scaled to children's sizes and a sensitivity to the effect that a child's illness has on the entire family.
Patient rooms at the new 172-bed hospital at New York Presbyterian will have an elaborate outer space theme, with beds modeled after spacecraft. The rooms will be large enough for parents to sleep in comfortably. The 106-bed children's center at Montefiore -- which will also be decorated with a celestial theme -- will have miniature cassette players with children's tapes. At Westchester, which will offer 150 beds, architects have tried to make the hospital seem more like a mall, with a food court and stores.
All three are scrambling to poach high-end pediatric specialists from among the limited number at other hospitals around the country. "Children's hospitals simply devote more resources to get the best children's specialists," said Lawrence A. McAndrews, the president of the National Association of Children's Hospitals and Related Institutions.
The enthusiasm for building children's hospitals is largely a consequence of their success in attracting philanthropy. These hospitals are strikingly able to draw dollars, often from high-profile sources; the singer Paul Simon, for example, gave $1 million for the Montefiore center.
New York Presbyterian's new center will be known as Morgan Stanley Dean Witter Children's Hospital of New York, reflecting a gift of more than $55 million raised by employee donations. "It is very easy to get people focused on children," said John J. Mack, president and chief operating officer of the financial services company, who helped raise $3.5 million in 1994 for a new pediatric intensive-care unit.
In 1997, he was host of a cocktail party in the completed unit, so employees could see what they had paid for. Children battling cancer and newborns struggling for life made for many wet eyes in the room, Mr. Mack and others who were present recalled. It took only one year to raise the additional $55 million.
Children's centers also are getting some new government financing. Last year, Congress earmarked $285 million for free-standing children's hospitals to provide help in paying the enormous costs associated with training doctors, something adult centers have enjoyed for years through Medicare. The new hospitals in New York may not qualify for this money, however, because they are part of larger hospital systems.
In 1997, Congress approved a federal supplement for states to insure children who are too poor to afford commercial insurance but do not qualify for Medicaid, and states are now slowly creating their insurance programs. New York State has been far ahead of that curve; its health insurance program predates the federal one, and the state has already enrolled more than 550,000 children.
"I won't tell you we didn't have that in mind when we decided" to build the children's hospital, said Edward A. Stolzenberg, president of Westchester Medical Center.
But can New York sustain three hospitals devoted just to children, especially when there are several children's centers in New Jersey? Executives at all three hospitals say it can.
"I think we will all do fine," said Dr. Irwin Redlener, president of Montefiore's new children's hospital. "Philadelphia has three kids' hospitals. I personally believe we need another center in Brooklyn because the need here is so overwhelming."
While the centers will obviously compete with one another, each has a slightly different strategy. Montefiore has a large number of uninsured chronically ill and asthmatic children in its region, and a major portion of its pediatric business comes from its emergency room, where poor parents often come to seek primary care. Dr. Redlener says he views the new hospital as the hub of a greatly expanded primary care system with 250 pediatricians.
Parents like Janet Cruz, whose 7-year-old son, Mark Anthony, has been hospitalized 21 times since birth for asthma, craves pediatricians who are more accessible, since she and similar parents of limited means usually have to rely on clinics with limited hours. "This new pediatrician has hours Tuesday until 8, and that works for me," Ms. Cruz said of a doctor who is already associated with Montefiore's planned center.
At New York Presbyterian, executives are betting that the hospital's long tradition of specializing in children will continue to attract top-notch doctors and medical researchers. "Where we will stand unique is that the highest end of science will be cheek by jowl with clinical care," said Dr. Herbert Pardes, chief executive of the hospital. And Mr. Stolzenberg of the Westchester Medical Center said he would be happy if he could dominate the region north of New York City and south of Albany.
Relying on government programs has rarely been a profitable formula for hospitals serving mainly adults. And if children's hospitals follow the example of adult centers by creating managed-care plans and buying physician practices, they may find themselves equally in the red. But for now, they seem to be following a well-mapped path.
"Hospitals are focusing on centers of excellence to meet what the market values," said Ms. Cross of the Healthcare Association. "And the market values children."

HEADLINE: Rescue Plan For Nassau Is Approved

BYLINE: By AL BAKER 

DATELINE: MINEOLA, N.Y., Nov. 29

 BODY:
After a frantic day of argument and confusion over the size of a proposed tax increase, Nassau County officials agreed tonight on a fiscal rescue plan that allows one of the nation's wealthiest counties to avert financial ruin and -- at least for now -- prevent a state takeover of its day-to-day operations.
The plan, passed unanimously by the County Legislature shortly before midnight, includes an increase of 7.9 percent next year in the county portion of property taxes -- an additional $73 for the average household. And it calls for an additional 5 percent increase in each of the following three years, or about $57 a year for the average household, said Eric Naughton, acting director of the Legislature's Office of Budget Review. 
 The tax increases were a key sticking point in months of often chaotic negotiations among the Democratic and Republican leaders of the legislature; County Executive Thomas S. Gulotta, a Republican; and a state oversight board monitoring the county's efforts, whose leader was handpicked by Gov. George E. Pataki.
But the state board, known as the Nassau Interim Finance Authority, threatened to take over the county's finances if Mr. Gulotta and all 10 Democrats and 9 Republicans in the Legislature did not support the plan. After the Legislature grudgingly went along, at 11:30 p.m., the state board issued a statement from its chairman, Frank G. Zarb.
"While there are many management challenges still facing the county," the statement read, "this plan may avoid hard controls while NIFA continues to assist Nassau County in achieving long-term structural balance."
Still, the story of the county's fiscal mayhem in the midst of one of the nation's longest stretches of prosperity is far from over. The four-year plan approved tonight is, for the most part, merely a blueprint. Although it sets a 2001 budget, the proposals for the following three years could change, especially if a recession hits the region.
The debt-ridden county still must allow the state to monitor its finances and future budgets, and it must still make millions of dollars in spending cuts over the next four years. If that effort fails, and the county drifts back to the edge of bankruptcy, the state board can swoop in at any time and seize fiscal control from elected officials.
On Dec. 8, the board, at the direction of Mr. Zarb -- who is also the chairman of the group that runs the Nasdaq stock market -- is expected to accept the plan.
On the day that they expected to finally approve the plan, county officials were thrown into confusion today over the size of the property tax increase it would impose next year.
The Legislature had planned to vote at 1 p.m. to pass the plan, which it had worked out with Mr. Gulotta and the state board, unanimously.
But after waiting through the afternoon for Mr. Gulotta to make final calculations, the lawmakers said he told them that the 7.9 percent increase he had calculated in next year's county property taxes might be wrong. To make the budget work, he said, he thought he might need an 8.5 percent increase.
Mr. Gulotta's revision sent county officials back into negotiations.
But in the end, the tax increase for homeowners was found to be 7.9 percent after all.
"This is nowhere near the end of the process," said Richard M. Kessel, one of seven members of the state board. "It is the end of the beginning of the process. We made a step forward, but there are many steps left to take before Nassau County is on strong financial footing."
In stronger words, the often battling legislative leaders -- Judith A. Jacobs, the Democratic presiding officer, and Mr. Schmitt, the Republican minority leader -- said the plan fell far short of pleasing them or ruling out future budget problems.
Before the vote tonight, Ms. Jacobs said the plan was far better than any the county had seen in the last decade. But, she said, it left Democrats in her caucus feeling deflated. She said it relied too heavily on revenue that is not sure to materialize from year to year -- $6.6 million in a one-time source of revenue -- and could set the stage for future budget problems. Democrats had agreed to support the plan, she said, to prevent a state takeover.
"We still believe that the concerns we raised during the budget process will rear their head during the fiscal year," Ms. Jacobs said. "We do not kid ourselves."
Mr. Schmitt, who said he would immediately push to freeze wages and win contract concessions from government employees, and seek even further spending cuts in the months ahead, was even more critical. He accused the state board and Governor Pataki of "dictating policy" to the county's elected officials by insisting that lawmakers pass the plan unanimously. A spokesman for the governor rejected that charge.
"The governor made it very clear that he hoped the county leaders would reach a bipartisan consensus that would result in long-term reforms that protected the taxpayers and resulted in a more efficient government," said the spokesman, Michael F. McKeon. "How they got there was up to them, so long as it could pass muster with NIFA."
The political sparring began almost from the day in May when Mr. Pataki offered a state bailout of Nassau: a plan to give the county $100 million in aid, plus $5 million to revamp its antiquated tax assessment system in exchange for tens of millions of dollars in spending cuts or new revenues.
On Sept. 15, Mr. Gulotta proposed a 2001 budget with a 3.8 percent increase in county property taxes and hundreds of layoffs. But the Democratic majority changed the tax increase to 15.4 percent and scrapped the layoff plans. Mr. Gulotta vetoed the tax increase, instead proposing a rise of 7.9 percent.
In recent days, the state board offered to let the county dip into a projected $27 million to $40 million surplus next year to pay for what Democrats wanted: a $10 million rainy-day fund; the hiring back of 15 percent of the 120 employees who had left the Parks and the Public Works Departments; $665,000 to hire 10 deputy county attorneys; and $1 million for technological improvements in the county.
The Democrats had long opposed using projected surplus funds to pay for those items. But the state board and other independent fiscal monitors said the 2001 budget, including those projections, was solid. And the board stepped up its warnings that it could stage a takeover.
The board also insisted on 5 percent tax increases in 2002, 2003 and 2004, as the Republican lawmakers had initially suggested. That was more than the 3 percent increase Mr. Gulotta had sought in each of those years, but the board said the revenue would ensure the future contingency funds, lawmakers said.
Many in the county were left shaking their heads tonight, wondering how Nassau's attempts to right its financial ship will play out.
"This whole budget, if it is going to work, relies to a large degree on trust," said Chuck Cutolo, a counsel to the Democratic lawmakers. "And none of the events today have helped enhance that trust."
 
Main Features Of Fiscal Plan For County
 
TAXES -- The county portion of property taxes, 19 percent of residents' overall property tax bill, will rise by 7.9 percent in 2001. That is an annual increase of $73 for an average household, which now pays $1,050 a year. The long-term plan calls for those taxes to rise 5 percent, $57 a year for the average household, in each of the next three years.
 
SPENDING CUTS -- The $2.1 billion budget for 2001 includes $109 million in cuts. Further reductions are included in the plan for the three years after that: $13.8 million in 2002, $25 million in 2003 and $22.5 million in 2004, for expenses like supplies and equipment and contractual costs. A $14 million subsidy to Long Island Bus will go to $7 million in 2001 and $5 million in 2002. County officials plan to ask the state to pay the difference.
 
WORK FORCE REDUCTIONS -- A total of 69 nonunionized county employees will be laid off in 2001, but no further layoffs are projected. About 10 percent of the county work force, 800 employees (including 200 police officers), have agreed to retire early or accept severance incentives.
 
CONTINGENCY MONEY -- The county, at the direction of the state oversight board, will establish a $10 million rainy day fund for each of the four years. It is financed in the first year by $792 million to $796 million in sales tax revenue the county expects in 2000. The county expects that figure to grow by 3 percent each year.
 
STATE AID -- The county expects to get $100 million in state aid, plus $5 million to update its tax assessment system, in the next four years. In 2001, the county expects $25 million of that money as well as $88 million in savings, because the state is refinancing part of the county's $2.6 billion debt. The county owes millions of dollars in interest on old debt.

HEADLINE: Metro Business Briefing;
BANK GRANT FOR BROOKLYN

BYLINE: By Terry Pristin 

 BODY:
Fleet bank has selected Brooklyn as one of four Northeastern communities that will receive an economic development grant, officials said yesterday. The $750,000 award will be used to help attract and stabilize businesses in Fort Greene, Bedford-Stuyvesant and Clinton Hill and will provide merchants and entrepreneurs with technical assistance, said Rena DeSisto, a Fleet spokeswoman. The grant is to be administered by Fulton First, which includes the Brooklyn Chamber of Commerce and three community groups. The other communities receiving grants are Buffalo, New Haven and Lowell, Mass., Ms. DeSisto said. Terry Pristin

HEADLINE: Steel City, Slovakia;
An American Giant Places a Bet in Eastern Europe

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: KOSICE, Slovakia

 BODY:
Lukas Cervienka vividly remembers the day the giant VSZ steel mill here ran out of iron.
It was January 1999, and Mr. Cervienka was sitting in a control room overlooking the automated ladles that pour 180 tons of molten metal at a time. The company was so broke that suppliers were refusing to deliver raw materials, and the company's creditors had hired a new president to prevent a complete collapse. 
 "He came in here and asked why we weren't doing anything," recalled Mr. Cervienka, head of a unit that maintains machinery. "I told him we didn't have any iron to work with. We didn't have anything to do."
Today, two years later, the mill has been rescued from the brink of bankruptcy. And it now has a new owner: U.S. Steel, a unit of the USX Corporation.
At $450 million, the acquisition of VSZ is a small deal in financial terms. But as a measure of transformation it is a big event as much for U.S. Steel as for this struggling nation.
For Slovakia, a small and impoverished country that split off from the Czech Republic in 1993, the steel mill here is the biggest enterprise and its most visible victim of post-Communist corporate abuse. The rescue is crucial to re-establishing Slovakia's credibility with the West and repairing a national image damaged by corruption and crony capitalism.
"This is a sign that the people of this country want to live in today's world," said Zdenko Trebula, the mayor of Kosice. "If the gentlemen from U.S. Steel decided to invest their money here, it means they trusted this place."
For U.S. Steel, this is the first major expansion after more than two decades of relentless contraction. The Slovak mill will account for one-quarter of U.S. Steel's entire production capacity. With more than 17,000 workers, the operations here employ twice as many people as U.S. Steel's biggest American sites. But while American steelworkers earn $35 to $42 an hour, wages here are about $2 an hour.
"If you talk about consolidation of the steel industry in the U.S., you are talking about cuts," said John Goodish, who will be chief executive of U.S. Steel Kosice. "Our strategy here is expansion and maximizing production. It is something no other American steel company has done."
Both company and country are making a calculated bet about globalization. U.S. Steel executives say they are convinced that Central Europe, with its low wages and increasingly pro-business policies, is becoming a major manufacturing center for companies from General Motors to General Electric. Slovak leaders are convinced that the only path to prosperity involves adopting western practices on everything from monetary policy to accounting practices and bankruptcy law.
For Slovaks, the last few years have been grueling. Unemployment averages about 20 percent across the country; 23 percent in Kosice. Real wages, adjusted for inflation, have declined more than 8 percent this year.
Here in Kosice, a picturesque city of 300,000, the municipal government is so short of money that it is trying to sell public forest land and buildings. Young people graduating from vocational school or the university set their sights on leaving, and many parents encourage them.
By all accounts, the two-year-old government of Prime Minister Mikulas Dzurinda has pushed through crucial economic changes. It has deregulated old monopolies, greatly improved the country's global credit ratings and revived exports.
Foreign investment has picked up. The Whirlpool Corporation has an appliance factory here and is now expanding. Volkswagen has a big factory in Bratislava, the capital, and BMW is considering building a car assembly plant not far from Kosice.
And Moody's, the credit-rating agency, praised Slovakia earlier this month, noting that it had "greatly improved" its budget and trade balances as well as its competitiveness.
The European Union, which shunned Slovakia as a potential member two years ago, now lists it as a candidate for entry just behind front-runners like Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic.
"It is a very, very big success," said Robert Prega, an economist in Bratislava for Raiffeisen Zentralbank of Austria.
But the risks are substantial and few institutions embody them as vividly as the VSZ steel mill here in Kosice.
After being privatized, the company came under the control of Alexandr Rezes, a close associate of former Prime Minister Vladimir Meciar. Under Mr. Rezes, VSZ squandered hundreds of millions of dollars on dubious investments: sports teams, real estate, banks, a travel agency, a bankrupt steel company in Hungary, a never-finished iron-ore processing factory in Ukraine and even a small airline.
Mr. Rezes also split the company into a baffling maze of more than 120 affiliated companies. He installed his son as a top executive in the holding company and other relatives elsewhere.
The spree ended in late 1998, when the company abruptly ran out of cash and defaulted on $450 million worth of loans. Part of the problem stemmed from a worldwide decline in steel prices.
But the biggest reasons were internal. The company had been buying its equipment and raw materials at inflated prices from exclusive suppliers; many of its customers had not paid their bills for three years. Most of the investments were worthless and unrecoverable.
Gabriel Eichler, a former executive at Bank of America who was recruited by VSZ's creditors to take over as chief executive, found the company in chaos at the end of 1998.
"I walked into a company that had no money," said Mr. Eichler, an American citizen who was born in Slovakia. "There were no financial controls, no consolidated figures. It took us about three months to tell the banks what we had."
It was hardly a unique situation. In the neighboring Czech Republic, once praised as a fearless example of free-market reforms, all the major banks and many of the industrial conglomerates were driven into the ground by shoddy practices and outright corruption.
Indeed, Czechs and Slovaks both use the word "tunneling" to describe the process by which major companies are systematically drained of their assets. Tunneling is the economic equivalent of termite infestation: a maze of subterranean deals that leaves little more than a rickety shell.
Two of the Czech Republic's biggest steel companies -- Nova Hut and Witcowice -- are virtually bankrupt and are being put through a government-run restructuring. In Poland, the Katowice steel plant has more than $570 million in debt and is still losing money. Earlier this month, a British steel company, the Corus Group, pulled out of talks to acquire a one-third stake.
Here in Kosice, neither Mr. Rezes nor other former executives at VSZ have been charged with wrongdoing. The Slovak government has temporarily taken Mr. Rezes's passport, because it is investigating allegations that he used company money to build a fortress-like villa.
But Mr. Eichler said it was fruitless to chase the former management. "It just isn't worth going after the past," he said. "We have to look to the future."
Mr. Eichler and Mr. Goodish say there is ample evidence of tunneling. VSZ was buying much of its equipment and raw materials at inflated prices under exclusive contracts from favored suppliers. Mr. Eichler quickly abolished the exclusive contracts, which reduced input costs by 20 percent in 1999.
Mr. Rezes could not be reached for comment. But even the most loyal supporters of VSZ are angry about his tenure.
"This was a company that used to invest millions of crowns annually in new equipment," said Mr. Trebula, the mayor. "In the 1980's, it was rated very highly."
Mr. Trebula, like most people here, says he does not know whether Mr. Rezes acted improperly. But he said Slovakia had been plagued by business executives who drained their own companies.
"The idea of owning something here is that I should get my money out of it, build a big house, buy a Mercedes, take my vacation in Tahiti or my winters in Thailand," he said. "They didn't realize that to own a company means you have to take care of it, develop it, invest in it."
Why would U.S. Steel want to buy a troubled company like VSZ? The two companies already had a three-year-old tin-plating joint venture, which both companies say proved profitable and successful. By the time VSZ defaulted on its loans, U.S. Steel executives were actively looking to acquire steel plants in Europe.
"Our customers were all becoming multinational, and they were telling us, 'We want you to be multinational, too, so we can get the same level of service everywhere,' " Mr. Goodish said.
Western European steel producers were too expensive. Factories in Poland, Romania and the Czech Republic either could not produce the more sophisticated and profitable forms of coated steel or they needed intensive modernization.
For all its financial difficulties, VSZ's physical plant was in fairly good shape. It had been built in the 1960's and was younger than many of its rivals. The old regime had been fairly generous about investing in new equipment.
"It is 100 percent continuous-casting with three blast furnaces," Mr. Goodish said. "It's very similar to our machines in Pittsburgh."
Indeed, that view is echoed in Prague, where Czech officials are desperately looking for investors to rescue their nearly bankrupt steel producers. "We were a little surprised by U.S. Steel's move," said one Czech government official with a wince of regret. "But the fact is, VSZ is a very good plant."
U.S. Steel, pounded by cheap foreign rivals and chronic overcapacity in the United States, spent more than two decades shrinking to a shadow of its former self. Since 1980, it has reduced its work force to 20,000 from about 83,000. It abandoned the idea of being a supermarket for steel, a producer of everything from steel sheet to girders and nails. It sold or closed many lines of business, cutting its annual production capacity from more than 30 million tons of steel in 1982 to 12.8 million tons.
The sprawling complex here will add the capacity to make 4.7 million tons of steel a year. U.S. Steel is paying $60 million in cash and issuing $325 million in new bonds to VSZ's creditors. Depending on the new company's performance over the next two years, it will pay up to $75 million more.
On top of that, U.S. Steel has pledged to invest $700 million over the next 10 years. And in a big concession to local anxieties, the company has pledged not to reduce the work force except through attrition for the next several years.
Mr. Goodish faces trying circumstances. A team of nearly 50 U.S. Steel executives has been working elbow-to-elbow here in a pair of cramped offices. Though Kosice has no English-language schools, American executives have decided to enroll their children in local schools and bring in translators to help.
VSZ is still paying about $60 a ton for coal, while the market price is $45 to $50, and the company is almost certainly overpaying for other purchases as well.
Most workers are relieved about the takeover, but are not sure where things go from here. "It won't get better," said one worker as he left the mill on a recent Friday afternoon. "Better already happened."
